Five False Interpretations of Idol Worshippers
The author enumerates five invalid justifications that idol worshippers give for their practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five false justifications that idol worshippers offer for their practice, as identified by Imam Sanusi.

Core Idea
Idol worshippers rationalize their actions through five invalid interpretations.

Imam Sanusi now turns to the rationalizations used by idol worshippers to justify their practices. He identifies five false interpretations, each rooted in incorrect beliefs about God, angels, or the efficacy of created objects. Understanding these will help you see why Islamic theology rejects them.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note that the author lists five distinct interpretations, each introduced by a number.
The word 'تأويلات باطلة' means 'false interpretations'.
2
Examine each interpretation
Go through each of the five justifications. Understand what the idol worshippers claim: that idols represent spirits, stars, talismans, God's form, or deceased saints.
3
Identify the falseness
Reflect on why each interpretation is considered false. Consider how they contradict Islamic beliefs about tawhid (oneness of God) and transcendence.
The author labels them 'باطلة' (false) without further comment here, but the context of the book indicates they are invalid.
4
Connect to the lesson's goal
Relate the passage back to the learning objective: these are rationalizations that must be understood to defend divine transcendence.
This enumeration is part of a larger argument against idol worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام : فلهم في ذلك تأويلات باطلة : الأول: أنها صور أرواح تدبر أمرهم وتعتني بإصلاح حالهم على ما سبق . الثاني : أنها صور الكواكب التي إليها تدبير هذا العالم فزينوا كلاً منها بما يناسب ذلك الكوكب. الثالث : أن الأوقات الصالحة للطلسمات القوية الآثار لا توجد إلا أحياناً من أزمنة متطاولة جداً فعملوا في ذلك الوقت طلسمـاً لمطلوب خاص يعظمونه ويرجعون إليه عند طلبه . الرابع : أنهم اعتقدوا أن الله تعالى جسم على أحسن ما يكون من الصورة، وكذا الملائكة، فاتخذوا صوراً بالغوا في تحسينها وتزيينها وعبدوها لذلك. الخامس : أنه لما مات منهم من هو كامل المرتبة عند الله تعالى اتخذوا تمثالاً له وعبدوه.
The Imam said: They have regarding that false interpretations: The first: that they are images of souls that manage their affairs and take care of improving their condition as mentioned previously. The second: that they are images of the stars to which belongs the management of this world, so they decorated each of them with what suits that star. The third: that the times suitable for strong influential talismans only exist occasionally from very long periods, so they made at that time a talisman for a specific desired goal, magnified it, and returned to it when seeking it. The fourth: that they believed that God is a body in the most beautiful form, and likewise the angels, so they made images which they exaggerated in beautifying and adorning, and worshipped them for that. The fifth: that when one of them who was of perfect rank with God died, they took an image for him and worshipped it.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of five false interpretations (تأويلات باطلة) that idol worshippers use to justify their worship of idols. The author, referred to as al-Imam, lists them: (1) The idols are images of souls that manage their affairs, as previously mentioned regarding spirits. (2) The idols are images of stars that govern the world, so they decorate them accordingly. (3) Because the times for effective talismans are rare, they made talismans for specific needs and then venerated them. (4) They believed God and angels have beautiful physical forms, so they made beautified images and worshipped them. (5) When a spiritually perfect person died, they made an image of him and worshipped it. These interpretations are deemed 'false' (باطلة) because they are based on incorrect beliefs about God's nature or about the efficacy of created objects.

Key Terms
تأويلات باطلة
صور الأرواح
صور الكواكب
الطلسمات
جسم
تمثال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the five justifications are valid reasons for worshipping idols.
Correction: All five are labeled as 'false interpretations' (تأويلات باطلة) because they are based on incorrect beliefs about God, spirits, stars, talismans, or deceased humans.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do idol worshippers believe about the timing of talismans?
A) They can be made at any time
B) They require specific rare times from very long periods
C) They are only effective at night
D) They are made automatically

Key Takeaway
Idol worshippers attempt to justify their practices through five false interpretations, all of which Islamic theology categorically rejects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these five false interpretations resonates most with rationalizations you have made to justify actions you knew were wrong? How will you break that pattern starting today?
?
The idol worshippers took something created and made it a focus of devotion. What created things (people, status, possessions) do you find yourself disproportionately attached to, and how can you redirect that attachment to Allah alone?
?
If someone confronted you with one of your subtle justifications for sin, would you defend it like these idol worshippers defended their statues? What is one specific justification you need to abandon right now, and what act will you take to do so?

Five Invalid Interpretations of Idol Worship
Al-Taftazani enumerates five false justifications for idol worship given by polytheists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five false justifications polytheists gave for idol worship and why they are considered invalid.

Core Idea
Polytheists rationalized idol worship with five flawed arguments—all rejected.

Al-Taftazani presents five invalid interpretations (ta'wilat batila) that polytheists used to justify their practice. Each interpretation attempts to give a rational or symbolic meaning to idolatry, but all are ultimately rejected. This passage catalogues these justifications so that the student can recognize and refute them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the list of five interpretations come through.
Notice that each interpretation is introduced with a number (الأول, الثاني, etc.).
2
Identify each interpretation
For each of the five, write down the core claim (e.g., 'images of spirits', 'images of planets', etc.).
3
Consider why they are invalid
Think about the theological problem in each: attributing management to spirits/planets, likening Allah to a body, using talismans for worship, or taking images as intercessors.
The passage labels them 'batila' (invalid) without explicit refutation; the student should understand the underlying errors.
4
Reflect on the pattern
Notice that all five try to reduce idol worship to something else (symbolism, astronomy, magic, anthropomorphism, or intercession).
This shows the polytheists' attempts to rationalize their practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام : فلهم في ذلك تأويلات باطلة : الأول: أنها صور أرواح تدبر أمرهم وتعتني بإصلاح حالهم على ما سبق . الثاني : أنها صور الكواكب التي إليها تدبير هذا العالم فزينوا كلاً منها بما يناسب ذلك الكوكب. الثالث : أن الأوقات الصالحة للطلسمات القوية الآثار لا توجد إلا أحياناً من أزمنة متطاولة جداً فعملوا في ذلك الوقت طلسماً لمطلوب خاص يعظمونه ويرجعون إليه عند طلبه . الرابع : أنهم اعتقدوا أن الله تعالى جسم على أحسن ما يكون من الصورة، وكذا الملائكة، فاتخذوا صوراً بالغوا في تحسينها وتزيينها وعبدوها لذلك. الخامس : أنه لما مات منهم من هو كامل المرتبة عند الله تعالى اتخذوا تمثالاً على صورته وعظموه تشفعاً إلى الله تعالى وتوسلا (۱)
The Imam said: They have invalid interpretations regarding that: First: that they are images of spirits that manage their affairs and attend to improving their condition as mentioned before. Second: that they are images of the planets to which the management of this world is attributed, so they adorned each of them with what suits that planet. Third: that the appropriate times for powerful-effect talismans only occur occasionally in very long periods, so they made a talisman at that time for a specific desired object which they venerate and turn to when seeking it. Fourth: that they believed that Allah Most High is a body in the best possible form, and likewise the angels, so they made images which they exaggerated in beautifying and adorning and worshipped them for that. Fifth: that when a person of perfect rank with Allah died among them, they made a statue in his image and venerated it as an intercessor to Allah and as a means of approach.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage lists five false justifications (ta'wilat batila) that polytheists used to rationalize their idol worship. The Imam (likely al-Razi) refutes them implicitly. The first is that idols represent spirits that manage affairs. The second is that they represent planets that govern the world. The third is that they are talismans made at auspicious times for specific needs. The fourth is that since Allah and angels are bodies of best form, they made images of them. The fifth is that images of righteous people were taken as intercessors. Each of these is presented as an invalid interpretation.

Key Terms
تأويلات باطلة
صور أرواح
صور الكواكب
طلسمات
جسم
تمثال
تشفع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These interpretations provide legitimate reasons for idol worship.
Correction: They are all invalid (batila) according to Islamic theology because they rely on false premises about spirits, planets, magic, anthropomorphism, or intercession.

Checkpoint
Which of the five invalid interpretations involves the use of talismans?
A) First interpretation
B) Second interpretation
C) Third interpretation
D) Fourth interpretation

Key Takeaway
Polytheists offered five rationalizations for idol worship, but all are based on false premises and are invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult situation, do you ever find yourself relying on a 'lucky' object, a specific time, or a person more than directly turning to Allah? Which of these five invalid justifications might subtly appear in your own heart? Commit today to identifying one such hidden association and uprooting it with sincere tawheed.
?
The polytheists used the image of a righteous person as an intercessor. Do you ever admire a scholar or saint to the point where you feel their presence or memory brings you closer to Allah more than direct supplication? How can you honor the righteous without crossing into the very justification this passage condemns? Write one concrete action you will take to ensure your love for them remains within tawheed.
?
The fourth justification assumed Allah is a body in the best form. Have you ever subconsciously pictured Allah in your mind, even with good intent? What steps can you take to worship Allah as He truly is—beyond your imagination—based on what you have learned from this lesson about the invalidity of such presumptions?

Refuting Claims of Divine Sonship
Taftazani refutes Jewish and Christian claims of divine sonship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muslim theologians interpret scriptural terms like 'father' and 'son' metaphorically to uphold divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Scriptural 'father' and 'son' are figurative, not literal.

Al-Taftazani addresses two major claims of divine sonship from Jews and Christians. He shows that even if the scriptural texts are accepted, the terms must be understood metaphorically. This lesson examines his reasoning and its foundation in tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two claims
Notice that the Jews claimed 'Uzayr is the son of God because of his revival and memorization of Torah; Christians claimed Jesus is the son of God because of his miraculous birth and Gospel language.
These are the two main claims refuted in the passage.
2
Understand the interpretive principle
Taftazani reinterprets 'father' as lordship and 'son' as total devotion or honor, using the analogy of 'ibn al-sabil' (wayfarer).
This shows the metaphorical method of kalam.
3
Examine the Gospel citation
The author quotes the Gospel where Jesus says 'my Father and your Father', indicating that the term is applied to believers as well, proving it is not literal.
This internal scriptural evidence strengthens the argument.
4
Connect to the broader principle of tawhid
The passage concludes that shirk in divinity is negated by reason and revelation, and worship is due only to one God.
This reaffirms the core Islamic belief in tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم اليهود القائلون: إن عزيراً ابن الله؛ لما أحياه الله تعالى بعد موته وكان يقرأ التوراة عن ظهر قلبه، ومنهم النصارى القائلون بأن المسيح ابن الله حيث ولد بلا أب، وورد في الإنجيل ذكرهما بلفظ الأب والابن. والجواب : أنه إن صح النقل من غير تحريف فمعنى الأبوة الربوبية، وكونه المبدأ والمرجع ومعنى النبوة التوجه إلى جناب الحق عز وجل بالكلية كابن السبيل أو قصد التشريف والكرامة، ولهذا نقل في الإنجيل مثل ذلك في حق الأمة أيضاً حيث قال : إني صاعد إلى أبي وأبيكم وإلهي وإلهكم . وبالجملة فنفي الشركة في الألوهية ثابت عقلاً وشرعاً وفي استحقاق العبادة شرعاً، وما أمروا إلا ليعبدوا إلهاً واحداً لا إله إلا هو سبحانه عما يشركون (۳)
And among them are the Jews who say that 'Uzayr is the son of Allah because Allah revived him after his death and he recited the Torah by heart, and among them are the Christians who say that the Messiah is the son of Allah because he was born without a father, and it is mentioned in the Gospel with the expression 'father' and 'son'. The answer: If the transmission is sound without distortion, then the meaning of fatherhood is lordship (rububiyya) and being the origin and reference, and the meaning of sonship is total orientation to the court of the Truth, Mighty and Majestic, like 'ibn al-sabil' (wayfarer) or for honor and nobility. For this reason, similar expressions are transmitted in the Gospel regarding the community as well, when He said: 'I am ascending to my Father and your Father, and my God and your God.' In summary, the negation of association (shirk) in divinity is established rationally and legally, and in the deserving of worship legally, and they were commanded only to worship one God. There is no god but Him; exalted is He above what they associate.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage refutes the claims of the Jews regarding 'Uzayr and the Christians regarding Jesus being sons of God. The author first states the claims: Jews said 'Uzayr is the son of God because he was revived and knew the Torah by heart; Christians said Jesus is the son of God because he was born without a father, and the Gospel uses 'father' and 'son'. The response clarifies that if the scriptural text is sound and not distorted, the term 'father' means lordship (rabb) and origin, while 'son' (ibn) means total devotion to God, like 'ibn al-sabil' (a traveler) or for honor. This is supported by the Gospel verse where God says 'my Father and your Father'. The passage concludes that shirk in divinity is negated by reason and revelation, and worship is owed only to one God, citing the Quranic verse (9:31).

Key Terms
عزير
ابن الله
المسيح
الأبوة
النبوة
كابن السبيل
الإنجيل
الشركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the term 'son of God' implies literal biological sonship for Jesus or 'Uzayr.
Correction: The passage explains that such terms are metaphorical, meaning lordship and devotion, not literal paternity.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the true meaning of 'father' (al-ubuwwa) when used of God in the Gospel?
A) Biological paternity
B) Lordship and being the origin
C) Adoption
D) It is an error in translation

Key Takeaway
Muslim theologians interpret anthropomorphic terms in scripture metaphorically to preserve God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear religious texts from other faiths use terms like 'son of God', do you instinctively reject them as false, or do you pause to consider how they might be interpreted figuratively to preserve God's oneness? What prevents you from offering that same hermeneutical charity to others that you expect for your own scripture?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking of God in human terms—as a father figure who shares your emotions or preferences—because it feels more comforting than His absolute transcendence? How would this lesson's insistence on metaphorical language challenge that tendency in your daily prayer and trust?
?
Taftazani uses a Gospel quote that applies 'Father' to all believers. If you were to defend tawheed to a Christian, could you calmly point to that same verse without hostility? What would you need to let go of—pride, fear, or certainty—to engage that conversation as a seeker of truth rather than a debater?

The Prohibition of Applying 'Body' and 'Substance' to Allah
Identifies opponents who apply 'body' and 'substance' loosely and prohibits such usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is prohibited to apply the terms 'body' (jism) and 'substance' (jawhar) to Allah, even in a loose sense, and the legal and rational reasons for this prohibition.

Core Idea
Applying 'body' to Allah, even metaphorically, opens the door to anthropomorphism and Christian trinitarian errors.

In this passage, the author identifies opponents who use terms like 'body' and 'substance' in a broad sense, equating them with 'existent' and 'self-subsistent'. While these usages might seem harmless, he argues they are prohibited both legally (due to lack of scriptural permission) and rationally (because they imply corporeality or align with Christian teachings). Understanding this prohibition is foundational to preserving divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three groups of opponents
Read the passage and note the three categories: those who use 'body' loosely, the corporealists (mujassimah), and the assimilators (mushabbihah).
The first group is the focus of this lesson.
2
Understand the first group's usage
They apply 'body' to mean simply 'existent' and 'substance' to mean 'self-subsistent'. The author says this is prohibited.
3
Learn the legal and rational reasons
Legally, there is no permission from the Lawgiver. Rationally, it suggests corporeality and aligns with Christian trinitarian doctrine (one substance, three hypostases).
The reference to Ibn Karram shows even a respected figure's usage is not followed.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the author is cautious about terminology: even if a term could be interpreted harmlessly, it may lead to error.
This is a principle of guarding against misconceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المخالفون في تنزيه الله تعالى
قال : وأما المخالفون : فمنهم أطلق الجسم بمعنى الموجود والجوهر بمعنى القائم بنفسه، والحق المنع شرعاً واحتياطاً ومنهم المجسمة : القائلون بأنه جسم على صورة شاب أمرد أو شيخ أشمط أو شبيكة بيضاء تتلألأ .
والمشبهة : القائلون : بأنه في جهة العلو وفوق العرش تماساً له أو محاذياً ببعد متناه أو غير متناه متمسكين بأن كل موجود جسم أو جسماني أو متحيز أو حال فيه ومتصل بالعالم أو منفصل وداخل العالم أو خارجه، وبظواهر النصوص المشعرة بالجهة والجسمية والجواب ظاهراً .
أما المخالفون إجراء الجسم مجرى الموجود فمخالف للعرف واللغة. ولما اشتهر من الاصطلاحات، لكن إطلاق الجوهر بمعنى الموجود القائم بنفسه، الذات والحقيقة اصطلاح شائع فيما بين الحكماء.
وبمعنى
فمنها هنا يقع في كلام بعضهم إطلاق لفظ الجوهر على الواجب.
وفي كلام ابن كرام أن الله احدى الذات احدى الجوهر، ومع هذا فلا ينبغي أن يجزأ على ذلك، ولا على إطلاق الجسم عليه بمعنى الموجود . إما سمعاً فلعدم إذن الشارع، وإما عقلا فلإيهامه بما عليه المجسمة من كونه جسماً بالمعنى المشهور، ولما عليه النصارى من أنه جوهر واحد ثلاثة أقانيم على ما سيجيء .
وأما القائلون بحقيقة الجسمية والحيز والجهة فقد بنوا مذهبهم على قضايا وهمية كاذبة تستلزمها، وعلى ظواهر آيات وأحاديث مشير بها. أما الأول : فكقولهم كل موجود فهو إما جسم أو حال في جسم والواجب يمتنع أن يكون حالاً في الجسم لامتناع احتياجه فيتعين أن يكون جسماً . وكقولهم : كل موجود إما متحيز أو حال في التحيز ويتعين كونه متحيزاً لما مرّ، وكقولهم : الواجب إما متصل بالعالم أو منفصل عنه ، وأياً ما كان يكون في جهة منه
.
وكقولهم : الواجب إما داخل في العالم فيكون متحيزاً أو خارجاً عنه فيكون في جهة منه ، ويدعون في صحة هذه المنفصلات، وتمام انحصارها الضرورة والجواب : المنع كيف وليس تركيبها عن الشيء ونقيضه، أو المساوى لنقيضه، وأطبق أكثر العقلاء على خلافها، وعلى أن الموجود إما جسم أو جسماني، أو ليس بجسم، ولا جسماني، وكذا باقي التقسيمات المذكورة، والجزم بالانحصار في القسمين، إنما هو من الأحكام الكاذبة للوهم، ودعوى الضرورة مبنية على العناد والمكابرة، أو على أن الوهميات كثيراً ما تشتبه بالأوليات .
وأما الثاني : فلقوله تعالى : ( وجاء ربك ) هل ينظرون إلا أن يأتيهم
The opponents regarding the transcendence of Allah the Exalted. He said: As for the opponents: among them are those who apply 'body' (jism) in the sense of existent, and 'substance' (jawhar) in the sense of self-subsistent, and the correct view is prohibition, legally and as a precaution. And among them are the corporealists (mujassimah): those who say that He is a body in the form of a beardless young man or a gray-haired old man or a white tuft that glitters. And the assimilators (mushabbihah): those who say that He is in the direction of above and above the Throne, in contact with it or facing it at a finite or infinite distance, adhering to the view that every existent is either a body or corporeal or occupying space or inhering in it, and connected to the world or separate, and inside the world or outside it, and to the apparent meanings of texts that indicate direction and corporeality, and the answer is apparent. As for those opponents who apply 'body' in the sense of existent, it is contrary to custom and language and to what is well-known of technical terms. However, the application of 'substance' in the sense of existent self-subsistent, essence and reality, is a common technical term among the philosophers. And by meaning: hence here occurs in the speech of some of them the application of the word 'substance' to the Necessary Being. And in the speech of Ibn Karram that Allah is one in essence, one in substance, yet despite this it is not permissible to apply that to Him, nor to apply 'body' to Him in the sense of existent—either legally, because of the lack of permission from the Lawgiver; or rationally, because it suggests what the corporealists hold, that He is a body in the well-known sense, and because of what the Christians hold, that He is one substance with three hypostases, as will come. And as for those who affirm the reality of corporeality, spatial location, and direction, they have built their doctrine on false imaginative premises that entail them, and on apparent meanings of verses and hadiths that indicate them. As for the first: such as their saying: every existent is either a body or inhering in a body, and the Necessary Being cannot be inhering in a body because of the impossibility of His need, so it is determined that He is a body. And their saying: every existent is either occupying space or inhering in what occupies space, and it is determined that He occupies space for the reason mentioned. And their saying: the Necessary Being is either connected to the world or separate from it, and whichever it is, He is in a direction from it. And their saying: the Necessary Being is either inside the world, in which case He occupies space, or outside it, in which case He is in a direction from it. And they claim necessity for the soundness of these disjunctions and the complete exhaustiveness of them. And the answer: denial; how so? Their composition is not of a thing and its opposite, or of the equivalent of its opposite; and the majority of rational people have agreed contrary to them, and that the existent is either a body or corporeal, or not a body nor corporeal; and likewise the rest of the mentioned divisions. And the assertion of exhaustiveness in the two divisions is only one of the false judgments of the imagination, and the claim of necessity is based on obstinacy and stubbornness, or on the fact that imaginative judgments are often confused with primary ones. And as for the second: it is His saying, the Exalted: 'And your Lord comes' (Quran 89:22); 'Do they await but that He should come to them?'
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation of opponents of divine transcendence (tanzih). The author identifies three groups among the opponents. First, those who use terms like 'body' (jism) and 'substance' (jawhar) loosely—applying 'body' to mean simply 'existent' and 'substance' to mean 'self-subsistent'. The author states that the correct view is to prohibit such usage both legally (because no permission from the Lawgiver) and as a precaution. Second, the corporealists (mujassimah) who affirm that God is a literal body, describing Him in human-like forms. Third, the assimilators (mushabbihah) who assert that God is in a direction above the Throne, holding that every existent must be either a body or corporeal, etc., and they cling to apparent meanings of scripture. The author then discusses the first group, noting that applying 'body' to God is contrary to custom and language. However, applying 'substance' in the sense of self-subsistent essence is common among philosophers, but even so, it should not be applied to God because it suggests corporeality and aligns with Christian trinitarian doctrine (one substance, three hypostases). The author then turns to the second and third groups (those who affirm real corporeality, spatial location, and direction). He states that their doctrine is built on false imaginative premises and literal interpretations of scripture. He lists their arguments: every existent is either a body or inhering in a body; every existent is either occupying space or inhering in it; the Necessary Being must be either connected to the world or separate, inside or outside, etc. They claim these divisions are exhaustive by necessity. The author refutes this by denying the exhaustiveness: the divisions are not true disjunctions (they are not of a thing and its opposite), and the majority of rational people reject them, allowing for a third category: that which is neither body nor corporeal. The claim of necessity is a false judgment of the imagination, born of obstinacy or confusion between imaginative and primary truths. Finally, he addresses their scriptural evidence, citing the verse 'And your Lord comes' (Quran 89:22) and the phrase 'Do they await but that He should come to them?', implying that such verses are taken literally by the opponents, but the author's full refutation is implied to continue.

Key Terms
مخالفون
جسم
جوهر
مجسمة
مشبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is acceptable to apply the terms 'body' or 'substance' to Allah in a loose, figurative sense without any negative consequences.
Correction: The author argues that such usage is prohibited both legally (no scriptural permission) and rationally (it suggests corporeality and aligns with Christian trinitarian doctrine). Even if the intention is not literal, the terminology can lead to misunderstanding and error.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it prohibited to apply the term 'body' (jism) to God even in the sense of mere existence?
A) Because it contradicts the consensus of the philosophers.
B) Because it is legally prohibited due to lack of permission from the Lawgiver, and rationally because it suggests corporeality and aligns with Christian teachings.
C) Because the term 'body' always implies physical form.
D) Because the Quran explicitly forbids it.

Key Takeaway
Applying the terms 'body' or 'substance' to Allah, even in a figurative sense, is prohibited because it leads to corporealist and trinitarian errors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah in your daily conversations, do you ever use words that might unintentionally imply similarity to created things? Identify one such phrase you have used and ask yourself: does it compromise the principle of tanzih? What will you replace it with?
?
This lesson shows that even well-intentioned theological terms can lead to grave errors like corporealism and trinitarianism. Are there any beliefs about Allah that you hold simply because they are common or comfortable, without verifying that they align with pure tawheed? Write down one belief you will re-examine today.

Refuting the Arguments of Corporealists and Assimilators
Refutes corporealists and assimilators by denying the exhaustiveness of their divisions and attributing their arguments to false imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the false imaginative premises and scriptural misinterpretations used by corporealists and assimilators, and how they are refuted through logical denial of exhaustiveness and rational consensus.

Core Idea
The corporealists' claims that every existent must be either a body or in a body are false imaginative judgments, not necessary truths.

The author now turns to those who affirm literal corporeality, spatial location, and direction for Allah. He exposes their reliance on false imaginative premises—such as the false dichotomy that every existent is either a body or inhering in a body—and their literalist interpretation of verses like 'And your Lord comes'. This lesson explores the refutation of these arguments, showing that the claimed exhaustive divisions are not true disjunctions and that rational consensus rejects them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups and their doctrine
The corporealists (mujassimah) and assimilators (mushabbihah) affirm that Allah is a body, spatially located, or in a direction.
Their doctrine is built on two things: imaginative premises and apparent scriptural meanings.
2
Examine the imaginative premises
The passage lists several of their divisions: every existent is either a body or inhering in a body; every existent is either occupying space or inhering in it; the Necessary Being is either connected to the world or separate; etc.
They claim these divisions are exhaustive by necessity.
3
Understand the refutation: denial of exhaustiveness
The author denies that these are true disjunctions because they are not composed of a thing and its opposite. He also notes that the majority of rational people disagree, accepting a third category: neither body nor corporeal.
The claim of necessity is a false judgment of the imagination.
4
Consider the scriptural evidence
The opponents cite verses like 'And your Lord comes' (Quran 89:22) literally. The author implies that such verses have interpretations that do not imply corporeality.
The full refutation of scriptural evidence is not completed here but is indicated as apparent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المخالفون في تنزيه الله تعالى
قال : وأما المخالفون : فمنهم أطلق الجسم بمعنى الموجود والجوهر بمعنى القائم بنفسه، والحق المنع شرعاً واحتياطاً ومنهم المجسمة : القائلون بأنه جسم على صورة شاب أمرد أو شيخ أشمط أو شبيكة بيضاء تتلألأ .
والمشبهة : القائلون : بأنه في جهة العلو وفوق العرش تماساً له أو محاذياً ببعد متناه أو غير متناه متمسكين بأن كل موجود جسم أو جسماني أو متحيز أو حال فيه ومتصل بالعالم أو منفصل وداخل العالم أو خارجه، وبظواهر النصوص المشعرة بالجهة والجسمية والجواب ظاهراً .
أما المخالفون إجراء الجسم مجرى الموجود فمخالف للعرف واللغة. ولما اشتهر من الاصطلاحات، لكن إطلاق الجوهر بمعنى الموجود القائم بنفسه، الذات والحقيقة اصطلاح شائع فيما بين الحكماء.
وبمعنى
فمنها هنا يقع في كلام بعضهم إطلاق لفظ الجوهر على الواجب.
وفي كلام ابن كرام أن الله احدى الذات احدى الجوهر، ومع هذا فلا ينبغي أن يجزأ على ذلك، ولا على إطلاق الجسم عليه بمعنى الموجود . إما سمعاً فلعدم إذن الشارع، وإما عقلا فلإيهامه بما عليه المجسمة من كونه جسماً بالمعنى المشهور، ولما عليه النصارى من أنه جوهر واحد ثلاثة أقانيم على ما سيجيء .
وأما القائلون بحقيقة الجسمية والحيز والجهة فقد بنوا مذهبهم على قضايا وهمية كاذبة تستلزمها، وعلى ظواهر آيات وأحاديث مشير بها. أما الأول : فكقولهم كل موجود فهو إما جسم أو حال في جسم والواجب يمتنع أن يكون حالاً في الجسم لامتناع احتياجه فيتعين أن يكون جسماً . وكقولهم : كل موجود إما متحيز أو حال في التحيز ويتعين كونه متحيزاً لما مرّ، وكقولهم : الواجب إما متصل بالعالم أو منفصل عنه ، وأياً ما كان يكون في جهة منه
.
وكقولهم : الواجب إما داخل في العالم فيكون متحيزاً أو خارجاً عنه فيكون في جهة منه ، ويدعون في صحة هذه المنفصلات، وتمام انحصارها الضرورة والجواب : المنع كيف وليس تركيبها عن الشيء ونقيضه، أو المساوى لنقيضه، وأطبق أكثر العقلاء على خلافها، وعلى أن الموجود إما جسم أو جسماني، أو ليس بجسم، ولا جسماني، وكذا باقي التقسيمات المذكورة، والجزم بالانحصار في القسمين، إنما هو من الأحكام الكاذبة للوهم، ودعوى الضرورة مبنية على العناد والمكابرة، أو على أن الوهميات كثيراً ما تشتبه بالأوليات .
وأما الثاني : فلقوله تعالى : ( وجاء ربك ) هل ينظرون إلا أن يأتيهم
The opponents regarding the transcendence of Allah the Exalted. He said: As for the opponents: among them are those who apply 'body' (jism) in the sense of existent, and 'substance' (jawhar) in the sense of self-subsistent, and the correct view is prohibition, legally and as a precaution. And among them are the corporealists (mujassimah): those who say that He is a body in the form of a beardless young man or a gray-haired old man or a white tuft that glitters. And the assimilators (mushabbihah): those who say that He is in the direction of above and above the Throne, in contact with it or facing it at a finite or infinite distance, adhering to the view that every existent is either a body or corporeal or occupying space or inhering in it, and connected to the world or separate, and inside the world or outside it, and to the apparent meanings of texts that indicate direction and corporeality, and the answer is apparent. As for those opponents who apply 'body' in the sense of existent, it is contrary to custom and language and to what is well-known of technical terms. However, the application of 'substance' in the sense of existent self-subsistent, essence and reality, is a common technical term among the philosophers. And by meaning: hence here occurs in the speech of some of them the application of the word 'substance' to the Necessary Being. And in the speech of Ibn Karram that Allah is one in essence, one in substance, yet despite this it is not permissible to apply that to Him, nor to apply 'body' to Him in the sense of existent—either legally, because of the lack of permission from the Lawgiver; or rationally, because it suggests what the corporealists hold, that He is a body in the well-known sense, and because of what the Christians hold, that He is one substance with three hypostases, as will come. And as for those who affirm the reality of corporeality, spatial location, and direction, they have built their doctrine on false imaginative premises that entail them, and on apparent meanings of verses and hadiths that indicate them. As for the first: such as their saying: every existent is either a body or inhering in a body, and the Necessary Being cannot be inhering in a body because of the impossibility of His need, so it is determined that He is a body. And their saying: every existent is either occupying space or inhering in what occupies space, and it is determined that He occupies space for the reason mentioned. And their saying: the Necessary Being is either connected to the world or separate from it, and whichever it is, He is in a direction from it. And their saying: the Necessary Being is either inside the world, in which case He occupies space, or outside it, in which case He is in a direction from it. And they claim necessity for the soundness of these disjunctions and the complete exhaustiveness of them. And the answer: denial; how so? Their composition is not of a thing and its opposite, or of the equivalent of its opposite; and the majority of rational people have agreed contrary to them, and that the existent is either a body or corporeal, or not a body nor corporeal; and likewise the rest of the mentioned divisions. And the assertion of exhaustiveness in the two divisions is only one of the false judgments of the imagination, and the claim of necessity is based on obstinacy and stubbornness, or on the fact that imaginative judgments are often confused with primary ones. And as for the second: it is His saying, the Exalted: 'And your Lord comes' (Quran 89:22); 'Do they await but that He should come to them?'

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation of opponents of divine transcendence (tanzih). The author identifies three groups among the opponents. The author then turns to the second and third groups (those who affirm real corporeality, spatial location, and direction). He states that their doctrine is built on false imaginative premises and literal interpretations of scripture. He lists their arguments: every existent is either a body or inhering in a body; every existent is either occupying space or inhering in it; the Necessary Being must be either connected to the world or separate, inside or outside, etc. They claim these divisions are exhaustive by necessity. The author refutes this by denying the exhaustiveness: the divisions are not true disjunctions (they are not of a thing and its opposite), and the majority of rational people reject them, allowing for a third category: that which is neither body nor corporeal. ?

?

Christian Trinity and Its Critique
Taftazani describes and critiques the Christian trinitarian concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Christian doctrine of one substance with three hypostases and Taftazani's logical critique of it.

Core Idea
The Christian trinity is a confused mixture of monotheism and polytheism.

Taftazani begins by laying out the core Christian belief about God: one substance, three hypostases. He then exposes its logical flaws. This passage sets the stage for understanding the Christian view before delving into their specific theories of incarnation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the trinity
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the key terms: جوهر, أقانيم, etc.
Taftazani uses very precise language.
2
Identify the three hypostases and the critique
The passage defines the hypostases as existence, knowledge, and life, corresponding to Father, Son, Holy Spirit. Taftazani then points out two ignorances: making one three, and restricting to three attributes.
3
Reflect on why this is considered ignorance
Consider why Taftazani calls it ignorance. What is the logical problem with saying one substance is three persons? Why is limiting attributes to three arbitrary?
This connects to the broader Islamic emphasis on tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما النصارى فقد ذهبوا إلى أن الله تعالى جوهر واحد، ثلاثة أقانيم، هي الوجود والعلم والحياة المعبر عنها عندهم، بالآب والابن وروح القدس على ما يقولون: آباً ابناً روحاً قدساً، ويعنون بالجوهر القائم بنفسه، وبالأقنوم الصفة، وجعل الواحد ثلاثة جهالة، أو ميل إلى أن الصفات نفس الذات، واقتصارهم على العلم والحياة دون القدرة وغيره جهالة أخرى، وكأنهم يجعلون القدرة راجعة إلى الحياة والسمع والبصر إلى العلم ثم قالوا: إن الكلمة وهي أقنوم العلم، اتحدت بجسد المسيح وتدرعت بناسوته بطريق الامتزاج كالخمر بالماء عند الملكانية، وبطريق الإشراق كما تشرق الشمس من قوة على بلور عند النسطورية، وبطريق الانقلاب لحماً ودماً بحيث صار الإله هو المسيح عند اليعقوبية. ومنهم من قال ظهر اللاهوت بالناسوت، كما يظهر الملك في صورة البشر وقيل تركب اللاهوت والناسوت كالنفس مع البدن. وقيل: إن الكلمة قد تداخل الجسد فيصدر عنه خوارق العادات. وقيل: تفارقه فتحله الآلام والآفات إلى غير ذلك من الهذيانات.
As for the Christians, they hold that God is one substance with three hypostases (aqanim), which are existence, knowledge, and life, expressed among them as Father, Son, and Holy Spirit, as they say: Father, Son, Holy Spirit. They mean by substance the self-subsistent, and by hypostasis the attribute. Making the one three is ignorance, or a tendency to [believe that] the attributes are identical with the essence; and their restriction to knowledge and life without power and other [attributes] is another ignorance. It is as if they make power reducible to life, and hearing and sight reducible to knowledge. Then they said: the Word (al-kalima), which is the hypostasis of knowledge, united with the body of Christ and was clothed in his humanity (nasut) by way of mixture like wine and water according to the Melkites, and by way of illumination as the sun shines from a force upon crystal according to the Nestorians, and by way of transformation into flesh and blood such that the divine became the Christ according to the Jacobites. And some of them said the divine (lahut) appeared through the human (nasut) as the king appears in the form of a human. And it was said the divine and human combined like soul with body. And it was said the Word interpenetrated the body so that extraordinary acts (khawariq al-'adat) issue from it. And it was said it departed from him, so that pains and afflictions befell him, and other such deliriums.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage details the Christian doctrines of union and incarnation. Taftazani begins by explaining the Christian trinitarian concept of one substance (jawhar) with three hypostases (aqanim): existence, knowledge, life, corresponding to Father, Son, Holy Spirit. He critiques this by noting that making one three is ignorance, and their restriction to three attributes is arbitrary. He then explains their view of the Word (the hypostasis of knowledge) uniting with Christ's body. He enumerates the three Christian sects: Melkites believe in mixture (imtizaj) like wine and water; Nestorians believe in illumination (ishraq) like sunlight on crystal; Jacobites believe in transformation (inqilab) into flesh and blood, so that God became Christ. He then lists additional notions: appearance of divine through human, combination like soul and body, interpenetration causing miracles, and separation allowing suffering. The passage ends by dismissing these as deliriums.

Key Terms
جوهر
أقنوم
آب
ابن
روح القدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Christians believe in three gods.
Correction: Christians affirm one substance but three persons; Taftazani shows this is self-contradictory.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, what do Christians mean by 'jawhar' (substance)?
A) The essence of God
B) The self-subsistent
C) The attribute
D) The person

Key Takeaway
The Christian trinity is logically flawed because it makes one three and arbitrarily selects which attributes are divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a beautiful or complex belief about God, do you examine it for logical consistency, or do you accept it because it is common or emotionally appealing? How does Taftazani’s critique of the Trinity call you to be more rigorous in your own understanding of tawheed?
?
The Christians restricted God’s attributes to only three; we affirm many. Honestly, do you worship God with full awareness of all His attributes, or do you neglect some, like His power or hearing? Which attribute have you not reflected on recently?
?
Taftazani called their restriction ‘ignorance’. In what areas of your faith have you settled for ignorance—content with a superficial understanding of Allah’s names, the purpose of worship, or the logic of divine unity? What will you do today to confront that ignorance?

Christian Theories of Union and Incarnation
Taftazani enumerates the three Christian sects and their theories of union.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Christian sects' theories of how the Word united with Christ and be able to distinguish between mixture, illumination, and transformation.

Core Idea
Christians disagree among themselves on how union occurred: mixture, illumination, or transformation.

After critiquing the trinity, Taftazani turns to the incarnation. Christians believe the Word (the hypostasis of knowledge) united with the human Jesus. But they differ on the nature of this union, leading to three main sects. This passage outlines those views and adds extra notions, all dismissed as deliriums.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on union theories
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the three sects named: Melkites, Nestorians, Jacobites.
The passage is a catalog of doctrines.
2
Identify the three sects and their metaphors
Melkites: mixture like wine and water. Nestorians: illumination like sun on crystal. Jacobites: transformation into flesh and blood, so God became Christ.
Each metaphor implies a different relationship between divine and human.
3
Notice the additional notions and the dismissal
After the three main views, Taftazani lists other ideas: appearance like king, combination like soul, interpenetration causing miracles, separation causing suffering. He ends by calling all this 'deliriums' (hazyānāt).
This shows the incoherence from the Islamic perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما النصارى فقد ذهبوا إلى أن الله تعالى جوهر واحد، ثلاثة أقانيم، هي الوجود والعلم والحياة المعبر عنها عندهم، بالآب والابن وروح القدس على ما يقولون: آباً ابناً روحاً قدساً، ويعنون بالجوهر القائم بنفسه، وبالأقنوم الصفة، وجعل الواحد ثلاثة جهالة، أو ميل إلى أن الصفات نفس الذات، واقتصارهم على العلم والحياة دون القدرة وغيره جهالة أخرى، وكأنهم يجعلون القدرة راجعة إلى الحياة والسمع والبصر إلى العلم ثم قالوا: إن الكلمة وهي أقنوم العلم، اتحدت بجسد المسيح وتدرعت بناسوته بطريق الامتزاج كالخمر بالماء عند الملكانية، وبطريق الإشراق كما تشرق الشمس من قوة على بلور عند النسطورية، وبطريق الانقلاب لحماً ودماً بحيث صار الإله هو المسيح عند اليعقوبية. ومنهم من قال ظهر اللاهوت بالناسوت، كما يظهر الملك في صورة البشر وقيل تركب اللاهوت والناسوت كالنفس مع البدن. وقيل: إن الكلمة قد تداخل الجسد فيصدر عنه خوارق العادات. وقيل: تفارقه فتحله الآلام والآفات إلى غير ذلك من الهذيانات.
As for the Christians, they hold that God is one substance with three hypostases (aqanim), which are existence, knowledge, and life, expressed among them as Father, Son, and Holy Spirit, as they say: Father, Son, Holy Spirit. They mean by substance the self-subsistent, and by hypostasis the attribute. Making the one three is ignorance, or a tendency to [believe that] the attributes are identical with the essence; and their restriction to knowledge and life without power and other [attributes] is another ignorance. It is as if they make power reducible to life, and hearing and sight reducible to knowledge. Then they said: the Word (al-kalima), which is the hypostasis of knowledge, united with the body of Christ and was clothed in his humanity (nasut) by way of mixture like wine and water according to the Melkites, and by way of illumination as the sun shines from a force upon crystal according to the Nestorians, and by way of transformation into flesh and blood such that the divine became the Christ according to the Jacobites. And some of them said the divine (lahut) appeared through the human (nasut) as the king appears in the form of a human. And it was said the divine and human combined like soul with body. And it was said the Word interpenetrated the body so that extraordinary acts (khawariq al-'adat) issue from it. And it was said it departed from him, so that pains and afflictions befell him, and other such deliriums.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage details the Christian doctrines of union and incarnation. Taftazani begins by explaining the Christian trinitarian concept of one substance (jawhar) with three hypostases (aqanim): existence, knowledge, life, corresponding to Father, Son, Holy Spirit. He critiques this by noting that making one three is ignorance, and their restriction to three attributes is arbitrary. He then explains their view of the Word (the hypostasis of knowledge) uniting with Christ's body. He enumerates the three Christian sects: Melkites believe in mixture (imtizaj) like wine and water; Nestorians believe in illumination (ishraq) like sunlight on crystal; Jacobites believe in transformation (inqilab) into flesh and blood, so that God became Christ. He then lists additional notions: appearance of divine through human, combination like soul and body, interpenetration causing miracles, and separation allowing suffering. The passage ends by dismissing these as deliriums.

الملكانية

خوارق العادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Christians believe the same about incarnation.
Correction: The text shows three distinct sects with different metaphors for the union.

Checkpoint
According to the Jacobites, how does the Word unite with Christ?
A) By mixture like wine and water
B) By illumination like sun on crystal
C) By transformation into flesh and blood
D) By interpenetration

Key Takeaway
The three Christian sects propose different mechanisms for the union of divine and human in Christ, but all are dismissed as irrational.

?

What concrete step can you take this week to learn about the diversity within a group you usually label with one word?
?

What would it take for you to apply the same level of scrutiny to your own worldview as Taftazani applies to the Christians? Will you commit to identifying one such belief today?
?
Throughout this lesson, we see multiple theories that attempt to explain how God relates to creation. In your own life, are you ever tempted to force God into a human framework—expecting Him to act in a certain way or be limited by your understanding? How does the Islamic emphasis on God's transcendence (above any union or anthropomorphism) confront your natural desire for a God you can 'grasp'? What will you do this week to worship a God who is beyond your full comprehension?

Christian Sects and Their Theories of Incarnation
Al-Taftazani summarizes Christian views on the incarnation of the divine Word.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the variety of Christian theories of incarnation as summarized by Al-Taftazani and why they are rejected in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Christian doctrines of incarnation are diverse, contradictory, and ultimately seen as nonsense in kalam.

Al-Taftazani presents a concise doxographical overview of Christian views on how the divine Word (الكلمة) became incarnate. He lists several theories held by different sects, ranging from effulgence to a literal transformation into flesh. This passage not only informs us about Christian theology but also demonstrates the method of describing and critiquing opposing views in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the different sects mentioned and the theories attributed to each.
Pay attention to the terms for divine nature (لاهوت) and human nature (ناسوت).
2
Identify each sect and its theory
List the Melkites, Nestorians, Jacobites, and the unnamed groups. For each, note the specific theory of incarnation mentioned.
The passage presents theories in a mixed order, some explicitly linked to a sect and others as general views.
3
Reflect on the critique
Al-Taftazani ends by calling these theories 'nonsense' (هذيانات). Consider why an Islamic theologian would dismiss them outright.
This connects to the Islamic principle of tawhid, which rejects any concept of God becoming incarnate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الملكانية بطريق الإشراق كما تشرق الشمس من قوة على بلور عند النسطورية، وبطريق الانقلاب لحماً ودماً بحيث صار الإله هو المسيح عند اليعقوبية. ومنهم من قال ظهر اللاهوت بالناسوت، كما يظهر الملك في صورة البشر. وقيل تركب اللاهوت والناسوت كالنفس مع البدن. وقيل: إن الكلمة قد تداخل الجسد فيصدر عنه خوارق المعادات. وقيل: تفارقه فتحله الآلام والآفات إلى غير ذلك من الهذيانات.
The Melkites [say incarnation] by way of effulgence as the sun shines from a power upon crystal? At the Nestorians, and by way of transformation into flesh and blood so that the god became the Messiah according to the Jacobites. And among them are those who said the divine nature appeared through the human nature, as the king appears in the form of a human. And it was said: the divine and human natures combine like the soul with the body. And it was said: the Word has entered into the body so that supernatural wonders issue from him. And it was said: it departs from him so that pains and afflictions befall him, and other such nonsense.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a doxographical survey of Christian theories of incarnation. It lists several views: 1) The Melkites believe in a kind of effulgence of the divine into the human. 2) The Nestorians hold a similar illuminative theory. 3) The Jacobites assert a literal transformation of the Word into flesh and blood, equating God with the Messiah. 4) Some Christians say the divine nature (لاهوت) appeared through the human nature (ناسوت) as a king appears in human form. 5) Others speak of a composition of the two natures like soul and body. 6) Another theory is that the Word entered the body, producing miracles. 7) Yet another holds that the Word departs, leaving the human to suffer. The author dismisses all as nonsense (هذيانات).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Christians hold one unified doctrine of incarnation.
Correction: The passage shows multiple contradictory theories among different sects, demonstrating diversity within Christianity itself.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Christian theories of incarnation are varied and, according to Al-Taftazani, amount to nonsense from an Islamic perspective.

?

?
This passage lists several human attempts to describe the divine-human union. Have you ever tried to make God fit into your own limited categories? Will you spend time in prayer (du'a) asking God to purify your understanding of Him from any trace of tashbih (anthropomorphism)?

Extremist Shi'a Belief in Divine Incarnation (Hulul)
Al-Taftazani reports extremist Shi'a views on divine incarnation in Imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extremist Shi'a argument for divine incarnation in the Imams and why it is considered a deviation by mainstream Islamic theology.

Core Idea
God cannot be confined to a physical form, even by analogy with angels.

Al-Taftazani reports a heretical view held by extremist Shi'a groups. They argue that if angels and jinn can appear in human form, it is not impossible for God to do likewise. They then apply this to the Imams, claiming superhuman powers for them. This lesson examines their reasoning and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the analogical argument used by the extremists.
The comparison to Gabriel and jinn is key.
2
Identify the analogy
The extremists use the precedent of spiritual beings appearing physically to argue for God's incarnation. Reflect on why this analogy fails from a Sunni perspective.
3
Understand the conclusion
They identify 'Ali and his sons as the most likely candidates for divine manifestation. Note the claim that they perform superhuman acts.
4
Recognize the deviation
Understand that mainstream Islam rejects any physical incarnation of God. This view is limited to extremist Shi'a (ghulat) and is not representative of all Shi'a.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المنتمون إلى الإسلام، فمنهم بعض غلاة الشيعة القائلون، بأنه لا يمتنع ظهور الروحاني بالجسماني كجبريل في صورة دحية الكلبي، وكبعض الجن أو الشياطين في صورة الأناسى، فلا يبعد أن يظهر الله تعالى في صورة بعض الكاملين. وأولى الناس بذلك علي وأولاده المخصوصون الذين هم خير البرية، وفي الكلمات العلمية، والعملية فلهذا كان يصدر عنهم في العلوم والأعمال ما هو فوق الطاقة البشرية.
As for those who belong to Islam, among them are some extremists of the Shi'a who say that it is not impossible for a spiritual being to appear in a physical form, like Gabriel in the form of Dihya al-Kalbi, and like some jinn or devils in the form of humans, so it is not far that God Most High should appear in the form of some perfect ones. And the most deserving of people for that are 'Ali and his special sons who are the best of creation, and in scientific and practical matters, therefore they used to produce from them in sciences and deeds that which is beyond human capacity.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage deals with a heretical group within Islam: the extremists of the Shi'a (غلاة الشيعة). They argue by analogy: if angels like Gabriel can appear in human form (e.g., as Dihya al-Kalbi), and jinn and devils can take human shape, then it is not improbable that God Himself could appear in the form of some perfect human beings. They consider the most fitting candidates to be 'Ali and his descendants, whom they regard as the best of creation. Consequently, they believed these imams performed supernatural acts beyond human power. The author is reporting this view as a deviation.

Key Terms
غلاة الشيعة
الروحاني
الجسماني
الحلول
دحية الكلبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Shi'a believe in the incarnation of God in the Imams.
Correction: Only a small extremist fringe (ghulat) holds this view; mainstream Shi'a theology firmly rejects incarnation and upholds God's transcendence.

Checkpoint
What analogy do the extremist Shi'a use to justify the possibility of God appearing in human form?
A) The rising of the sun
B) The incarnation of the Word in Christianity
C) The appearance of angels and jinn in human form
D) The miracles of the prophets

Key Takeaway
The extremist Shi'a claim of divine incarnation in the Imams is based on a false analogy and contradicts the core Islamic belief in God's absolute transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The extremists used an analogy to justify their belief. Are there analogies you rely on to excuse actions you know are wrong? Ask yourself: 'If this analogy were used to justify shirk, would I still accept it?' Then commit to testing all your reasoning against the Qur'an and Sunnah.
?
This passage shows how a group venerated individuals to the point of compromising God's transcendence. Is there any person, group, or even a concept in your life that you have come to treat as indispensable, as a means of salvation? Repent and redirect that reverence exclusively to Allah.

Extremist Sufi Belief in Union with God
Al-Taftazani reports Sufi extremists who claim divine indwelling and union.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extremist Sufi claims of hulul and ittihad and why Sunni orthodoxy considers them corrupt.

Core Idea
Some Sufi extremists claim God dwells in or unites with the seeker, nullifying religious law.

Al-Taftazani warns against a dangerous deviation among certain Sufis who allege that the spiritual seeker can become so immersed in God that divine indwelling or union occurs. This claim, he argues, leads to the rejection of religious obligations and is self-evidently corrupt. In this passage, we see both the description of the belief and its swift refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the strong rejection phrase 'exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say'.
The author uses a Quranic phrase to denounce the claim.
2
Identify the two variant claims
The passage mentions two ways the extremist Sufis describe the relationship: hulul (indwelling) and ittihad (union). Note how each negates distinction between God and the seeker.
The analogy of fire in stone illustrates indistinctness.
3
Spot the antinomian consequence
According to these extremists, once union occurs, the seeker is no longer bound by divine commands and prohibitions. See how this ties to the claim of performing 'strange and wonderful things'.
This consequence shows the danger of the belief.
4
Understand the author's refutation
Al-Taftazani dismisses both views as 'self-evidently corrupt'. Reflect on why he considers them obviously false from an Islamic perspective.
The corruption lies in violating tawhid and the permanence of sharia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم بعض المتصوفة القائلون بأن السالك إذا أمعن في السلوك، وخاصة لجهة الأصول، فربما يحل الله فيه تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علواً كبيراً، وكالنار في الحجر، بحيث لا يتمايز أو يتحد به بحيث لا اثنينية ولا تغاير. وصح أن يقول: هو أنا، وأنا هو. وحينئذ يرتفع الأمر والنهي، ويظهر من الغرائب والعجائب ما لا يتصور من البشر، وفساد الرأيين غني عن البيان.
And among them are some of the Sufis who say that the seeker, when he persists in spiritual wayfaring, especially concerning the fundamentals, perhaps God may dwell in him—exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation—like fire in stone, in such a way that [the two] are not distinct, or He unites with him such that there is no duality nor otherness. And it is correct for him to say: He is I, and I am He. And then command and prohibition are lifted, and strange and wonderful things appear that are not conceivable from humans, and the corruption of both views is self-evident.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage describes a deviant group among the Sufis. They claim that a spiritual seeker, through intense effort, especially in foundational matters, may experience God dwelling in him (hulul) or uniting with him (ittihad). The author explicitly rejects this by saying 'exalted is God' and calls them wrongdoers. The analogy is like fire in stone—indistinct. They claim that in this state, duality disappears, so the seeker can say 'He is I, I am He', and the obligations of religious law are lifted. The author dismisses both the hulul and ittihad views as obviously corrupt.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Sufis believe in union with God or that the seeker becomes one with God.
Correction: The passage explicitly refers to 'some of the Sufis' (بعض المتصوفة), not all. Mainstream Sufism upholds strict tawhid and adheres to sharia.

Checkpoint

C) Becoming a prophet
D) Withdrawal from society

Key Takeaway
Extremist Sufi claims of divine indwelling or union are rejected as corrupt because they violate tawhid and nullify the obligations of sharia.

?

Opponents of Additional Attributes and Their Formulations
Listing the groups that denied attributes additional to the essence and their different formulations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main groups that denied attributes additional to the essence and the various formulations they proposed for God's attributes.

Core Idea
Even opponents of additional attributes disagreed on how God is described.

This passage introduces us to the historical opposition to the doctrine of attributes being additional to the essence. The author lists the major groups and then shows that they themselves had different ways of expressing God's attributes. This diversity reminds us that theological debates often involve multiple positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing groups
Look for which sects are mentioned as opposing additional attributes. Note their names: philosophers, Mu'tazila, and those following them.
These groups are labeled as 'people of innovation and desires.'
2
Understand the label 'Sifatiyya'
Notice that opponents coined this term for those affirming additional attributes. Reflect on what this label implies about their view of such attributes.
3
Examine the different formulations
The passage lists four ways opponents expressed God's attributes. Read each carefully and compare them. Consider why they might have differed.
The formulations range from 'for Himself' to 'by His essence' to 'not for Himself nor for causes.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخالف في القول بزيادة الصفات أكثر الفرق كالفلاسفة والمعتزلة ، ومن يجري مجراهم من أهل البدع والأهواء، وسموا القائلين بها بالصفاتية، ثم اختلفت عباراتهم فقيل : هو حي عالم قادر لنفسه، وقيل بنفسه. وقيل لكونه على حالة أخص صفاته، وقيل لا لنفسه، ولا لعلل . وكلام الإمام الرازي في تحقيق إثبات الصفات وتحرير محل النزاع، ربما يميل الى الاعتزال.
Most sects opposed the statement that attributes are additional, such as the philosophers and the Mu'tazila, and those who follow their path from the people of innovation and desires. They called those who affirm them 'Sifatiyya.' Then their expressions differed: it was said that He is living, knowing, powerful for Himself; said by His essence; said because He is in a state particular to His attributes; said not for Himself nor for causes. The words of Imam al-Razi in verifying the affirmation of attributes and clarifying the locus of disagreement perhaps incline toward Mu'tazilism.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage identifies the opponents of the doctrine of additional attributes and lists their various formulations. The opponents include the majority of sects (akthar al-firaq): the philosophers, the Mu'tazila, and those following their path among the people of innovation and desires. They labeled the proponents of additional attributes as 'Sifatiyya.' Within the opposition, there was disagreement on how to express God's attributes: some said 'He is living, knowing, powerful for Himself' (li nafsihi), others said 'by His essence' (bi nafsihi), others said 'because He is in a state particular to His attributes' (li kawnihi 'ala halatin akhassa sifatihi), and others said 'not for Himself nor for causes' (la li nafsihi wa la li 'ilal). The passage ends by noting that Imam al-Razi's investigation into this issue perhaps leans toward Mu'tazilism, implying that he did not fully endorse the Ash'ari position of additional attributes.

Key Terms
الفلاسفة
المعتزلة
الصفاتية
الرازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all opponents agreed on a single formulation, but the passage shows they varied.
Correction: The opponents had at least four different expressions: for Himself, by His essence, due to a particular state, or neither for Himself nor for causes.

Checkpoint
What was the term used by opponents to refer to those who affirm additional attributes?
A) Mu'tazila
B) Philosophers
C) Sifatiyya
D) Ahl al-Haqq

Key Takeaway
The denial of attributes additional to the essence was not monolithic; opponents differed among themselves on how to describe God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that even the opponents of additional attributes disagreed among themselves, does it make you question the certainty you have about God's attributes? What specific formulation do you hold, and why do you trust it over the others?
?
The passage ends by noting that Imam al-Razi's position perhaps leaned toward Mu'tazilism. Are you willing to re-examine a belief you hold strongly if a great scholar like al-Razi seemed to differ? What would it take for you to change your mind?
?
If someone accused you of being a 'Sifatiyya' (or any label), how would you defend your belief without becoming arrogant? Does your understanding of God's attributes produce humility and worship, or just intellectual pride?

Mu'tazila Argument Against Divine Power and Its Refutation
A brief objection and answer on God's power (qudra).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's argument that divine power cannot create bodies based on analogy with human power, and the refutation that the common cause may be more specific and the difference greater.

Core Idea
Shared terms do not imply identical properties; divine power is categorically different.

This passage presents a compact objection from the Mu'tazila regarding God's attribute of power (qudra). They argue that if God had power, it could not create bodies, because human power cannot. The author offers a crisp refutation: the common cause that prevents creation might be more specific, and the difference between divine and human power may be far greater than any variation among human powers. Let's unpack this dense exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: the objection is given first, then the answer.
The passage is short but dense; pay attention to the logical connectors.
2
Identify the two prongs of the objection
The Mu'tazila give two reasons: (1) the common cause argument (the shared 'powerness' prevents creation) and (2) the resemblance/difference argument (any power either exactly resembles human power or differs to some degree, but neither allows creation).
These are two distinct lines of reasoning; the refutation addresses both.
3
Examine the refutation
The answer says 'perhaps the cause is more specific and the difference is greater.' This means the common cause that prevents creation may be specific to human power, not shared by divine power. Also, the difference between divine and human power could be so great that divine power can create while human power cannot.
The refutation is concise but powerful; it blocks the analogy.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how this refutation upholds the Islamic doctrine that God's attributes are incomparable to created things (mukhalifatun lil-hawadith).
This is a key principle in Sunni kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ولهم في نفي القدرة. أنه لو كانت له قدرة لما تعلقت بخلق الأجسام لأن قدرة الشاهد ليست كذلك إلا لعلة مشتركة هي كونها قدرة ولأنها إما أن تماثل قدرة الشاهد أو تخالفها بقدر تخالفها . قلنا : لعل العلة أخص والمخالفة أشد .
He said: And to them in denying power: That if He had power, it would not be related to creating bodies because the power of the witness (i.e., human) is not like that except for a common cause, which is its being power; and because it either resembles the power of the witness or differs from it by the extent of its difference. We said: Perhaps the cause is more specific and the difference is greater.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a brief objection-and-answer. The Mu'tazila claim that if God had power (qudra), it could not apply to creating bodies, since in the witnessed world (human power) it does not. They argue this is because either (1) the common nature of 'being power' prevents it, or (2) any power would either exactly resemble human power or differ only to some degree—yet human powers vary but still cannot create bodies. The author's answer is concise: the common cause (illah) that prevents creation may be more specific (akhass) than just 'being power', and the difference (mukhalafah) between divine and human power could be greater (ashadd) than differences among human powers, allowing divine power to create bodies while human power cannot.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's power is just like human power but bigger.
Correction: The refutation shows that the nature of divine power is fundamentally different; the common cause may be more specific and the difference greater than any human variation.

Checkpoint
What is the core reason the Mu'tazila give in this argument for why God's power cannot create bodies?
A) Because God does not have power.
B) Because human power cannot create bodies, and any power must be like human power.
C) Because creating bodies requires a physical body.
D) Because the Qur'an denies it.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's analogy between divine and human power fails because the common cause may be specific to human power, and the difference between divine and human power is far greater than any variation among human powers.

?
Think of a situation where you have been waiting for God's help, but deep down you doubted because it seemed 'impossible by normal means'. How does the refutation in this lesson—that divine power is not limited by the same 'cause' that limits human power—expose the smallness of your trust? What will you actively do to enlarge your expectation of God's power starting today?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed that because 'power' is the same word, it must work the same way. In your spiritual life, where are you subtly assuming that God's attributes—His mercy, His justice, His knowledge—function like human versions? Choose one attribute and describe how you have been thinking wrongly, and how this lesson corrects you.
?
Are you guilty of 'taming' God by fitting Him into human categories? The correction here is that the difference between God and creation is far greater than any similarity.
