The Mu'tazila Argument Against God's Power and Its Refutation
Two-fold Mu'tazila argument against divine power and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's two-fold argument that God cannot have a power that creates bodies, and al-Taftazani's systematic refutation of each proof.

Core Idea
Shared properties do not force identical effects; divine power is unique.

The Mu'tazila argued that if God had a power (qudra) like human power, it could not create bodies. Al-Taftazani presents their two proofs and then dismantles each, showing that the logic of shared causes and similarity is flawed. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and his response.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's two proofs
Read the first part of the passage where the two proofs are presented. Notice how they rely on the concept of a shared cause (illa mushtaraka) and the principle that like things have like effects.
The proofs are labeled 'first' and 'second' in the text.
2
Study al-Taftazani's refutation
Read the answer section. He denies the necessity of a single shared cause and argues that the difference between divine and human power may be greater than among human powers.
His response addresses each proof in order.
3
Identify the logical moves
For each proof, identify the key assumption that al-Taftazani challenges. For proof one, it's the assumption that a hukm mushtarak requires a single illa. For proof two, it's the assumption that difference cannot be greater for divine power.
4
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why this debate matters: defending God's power to create without limitations imposed by analogy with human power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ولهم في نفي القدرة تمسك المعتزلة في امتناع كون البارى تعالى قادراً بالقدرة بأنه لو كان كذلك لما كان قادراً على خلق الأجسام، واللازم باطل وفاقاً ببيان الملازمة من وجهين : أحدهما : أن عدم صلوح قدرة العبد لخلق الأجسام حكم مشترك ، لا بد له من علة مشتركة، وما هي إلا كونها قدرة ، فلو كان للباري أيضاً قدرة، لكانت كذلك. وثانيهما : أن قدرة الباري على تقدير تحققها، إما أن تكون مماثلة لقدرة العباد فيلزم أن لا تصلح لخلق الأجسام، لأن حكم الأمثال واحد، وإما أن تكون مخالفة لها ، وليست تلك المخالفة أشد من مخالفة قدرة العباد بعضها للبعض ، ومع ذلك لا يصلح شيء منها لخلق الأجسام، فكذا التي تخالفها هذا القدر من المخالفة والجواب : أنا لا نسلم أنه لا بد للحكم المشترك من علة مشتركة. بل يجوز أن يعلل بعلل مختلفة، إذ لا يمتنع اشتراك المختلفات في لازم واحد، فها هنا يجوز أن يعلل عدم صلوح قدرة العباد لخلق الأجسام بخصوصياتها . ولو سلم، فلا نسلم أنه لا مشترك بينها سوى كونها قدرة الجواز أن تكون أمراً أخص من ذلك بحيث تشمل قدرة العباد، ولا تشمل قدرة الباري. ولا نسلم أن مخالفة قدرة الباري لقدرة العباد ليست أشد من مخالفتها فيما بينها لجواز أن تنفرد بخصوصية لا توجد في شيء منها، فتصلح لخلق الأجسام دونها .
He said: And to them in denying power, the Mu'tazila cling to preventing the Creator being powerful with power, that if it were so, He would not be able to create bodies, and the consequent is false by agreement, by demonstrating the entailment in two ways: First, that the unfitness of the slave's power to create bodies is a shared property (hukm mushtarak), which must have a shared cause, and it is nothing but its being power; so if the Creator also had power, it would be likewise. Second, that the Creator's power, on the supposition of its existence, is either similar to the power of servants, so it would not be fit to create bodies because the rule of similars is one; or it differs from them, and that difference is not stronger than the difference of servants' powers among themselves, and yet none of them is fit to create bodies; so likewise what differs by this amount of difference. The answer: We do not concede that a shared property must have a shared cause. Rather, it is permissible that it be caused by different causes, since it is not impossible for different things to share a single necessary consequence. So here it is permissible that the unfitness of servants' powers to create bodies be caused by their particularities. And even if conceded, we do not concede that there is no commonality among them besides being power; it is possible that there be something more specific than that, including the powers of servants but not including the Creator's power. And we do not concede that the difference of the Creator's power from servants' powers is not stronger than their differences among themselves, for it is possible that it be distinguished by a particularity not found in any of them, so it becomes fit to create bodies without them.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a detailed objection-and-answer. The Mu'tazila present two proofs (wajh) for their claim that God cannot be powerful with a power (qudra) that can create bodies. Proof One: The inability of human power to create bodies is a shared property (hukm mushtarak); therefore it must have a shared cause (illah mushtarakah), which is simply being power. So if God had power, it would also be unable to create bodies. Proof Two: Assuming God's power exists, either it resembles human power (mumathalah) or differs (mukhalafah). If it resembles, it follows the same rule—unable to create. If it differs, the difference cannot be greater than differences among human powers themselves (which still cannot create), so it too is unable. The author (al-Taftazani) refutes both. Against Proof One, he denies that a shared property necessarily has a single shared cause; different things can produce the same effect, and here the inability may be due to specific properties of human powers. Against Proof Two, he argues that (a) the commonality between human powers might be more specific than mere 'being power', excluding divine power; and (b) the difference of divine power from human powers could be much greater, perhaps due to a unique particularity that allows creation. Thus God's power can create bodies while human power cannot.

Key Terms

الحكم المشترك
العلة المشتركة
التماثل
المخالفة
الخصوصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that a shared attribute (e.g., being power) must produce identical effects in all bearers.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that a shared property does not force a shared cause, and the difference in particularities can lead to different effects.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof the Mu'tazila use in this passage to argue that God cannot have a power that creates bodies?
A) God's power is not eternal.
B) The inability of human power to create bodies is a shared property that requires a shared cause, which is being power.
C) The Qur'an says only humans create bodies.
D) God's power is unlike any power.

Key Takeaway
Shared properties do not force identical effects; divine power is unique and not bound by the limitations of human power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's power, do you unwittingly imagine it as a larger version of your own ability to act? In what specific area of your life have you limited God by projecting your own constraints onto Him? Write down one situation and consciously replace that limitation with the truth that His power is utterly unique and unrestricted.
?
This lesson shows that shared attributes do not mean shared effects. How does this challenge your instinct to measure God's attributes against human standards? What is one specific way you need to change how you speak about God's power to avoid reducing Him to your level?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that if God had power, it would be like human power and thus unable to create. Have you ever doubted God's ability to intervene in a situation because it seemed impossible from a human perspective? Commit today to recalling this refutation whenever such doubts arise, affirming that God's power is not bound by the rules of created things.

First Mu'tazila Argument Against God's Knowledge and Its Refutation
Al-Taftazani presents the Mu'tazila objection and his refutation in four points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that God's knowledge would be like human knowledge and how al-Taftazani refutes it by denying that shared necessary consequences imply similarity or equality.

Core Idea
Shared consequence does not prove similarity, and similarity does not prove equality.

This passage presents a key theological objection from the Mu'tazila against God's attribute of knowledge. They argue that if God's knowledge were like human knowledge, it would be similar and therefore share properties like being created or eternal. Al-Taftazani responds by clarifying the logical limits of analogy and similarity. Let's read the objection and its refutation carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila argument
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is presented. Notice that they argue from analogy with human knowledge ('as in the witnessed world').
The argument uses tamathul (similarity) based on the relation (ta'alluq) to the known.
2
Identify the consequences
The Mu'tazila draw three consequences: (1) both knowledges would share in being eternal or originated, (2) God's sciences would be infinite, (3) there would be a more knowing being above God. Note how they use a Qur'anic verse.
These are meant to show absurdity.
3
Examine al-Taftazani's refutation
Now read the refutation ('We said...'). It contains four points: shared necessary consequence does not entail similarity, similarity does not entail equality in attributes, a single entity can have multiple relations ad infinitum, and the universal verse does not particularize.
Each point directly addresses a premise of the argument.
4
Connect to broader kalam
Reflect on why this argument matters: It defends the uniqueness of God's attributes against anthropomorphic analogy. Understanding this helps appreciate the Sunni insistence on tanzih (transcendence).
This argument is a classic example of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy from the unseen to the witnessed).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وفي نفي العلم . أنه لو كان عالماً بعلم كما في الشاهد لكان العلمان متماثلين لتعلقهما بالمعلوم من وجه واحد فيلزم اشتراكهما في القدم أو الحدوث بخلاف العالمية فإنها فيه يتعلق الذات وفينا يتعلق العلم. ولكانت علومه غير متناهية لكونه عالماً بما لا نهاية له ولكان فوقه عليم لقوله تعالى : وفوق كل ذي علم عليم . قلنا لا يلزم من الاشتراك في اللازم التماثل ولا من التماثل الاستواء في الصفات ولا يمتنع كثرة تعلقات الواحد ولو إلى غير نهاية ولا تخصيص العمومات .
He said: And in denying knowledge. That if He were knowing with knowledge as in the witnessed world, then the two knowledges would be similar (mutamathilan) because they relate to the known from one aspect, so they would necessarily share in being eternal or originated—unlike the attribute of being knowledgeable (al-'alimiyyah), for in Him it relates to the essence and in us it relates to knowledge. And His sciences would be infinite because He knows what is infinite, and there would be above Him a more knowing (alim), according to His saying: 'And above every possessor of knowledge is one more knowing' (Qur'an 12:76). We said: Shared consequence does not entail similarity, nor does similarity entail equality in attributes; nor is it impossible for one thing to have multiple relations, even to infinity; nor does the universal verse particularize.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning God's knowledge (ilm). The Mu'tazila argue that if God were knowing with a knowledge (as in human knowledge), then His knowledge and human knowledge would be similar (mutamathilan) because both relate to the same known thing from one aspect—the relation of knowledge to its object. Consequently, they would share in being either both eternal (qadim) or both originated (hadith). The Mu'tazila distinguish this from the attribute of being knowledgeable (al-'alimiyyah), where God's is related to His essence while ours relates to our knowledge. Additionally, they claim God's sciences would be infinite (since He knows infinite things) and there would be a more knowledgeable being above Him, citing the Qur'anic verse. The author's response: First, sharing a necessary consequence (lazim) does not prove similarity (tamathul); two different things can share a property. Second, even if they were similar, that does not entail equality in all attributes (istiwa' fi al-sifat). Third, a single entity can have multiple relations (ta'alliqat) to infinity, so God's knowledge can relate to infinite objects without becoming multiple. Fourth, the verse is a general statement ('umum) that does not specify a particular application to God.

Key Terms
العلم
التماثل
التعلق
القدم
الحدوث
العالمية
اللازم
الاستواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share a necessary consequence (like being knowledge that relates to an object), they must be similar in essence.
Correction: Sharing a necessary consequence (lazim) does not prove similarity (tamathul). Two distinct entities can share a property without being alike in essence.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila's main concern that they express in this argument against God's knowledge?
A) That God's knowledge would be finite.
B) That if God's knowledge and human knowledge relate to the same object in the same way, they would be similar and thus share in being eternal or originated.
C) That knowledge is a created attribute.
D) That God does not need knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argument fails because similarity in one aspect (relation to the known) does not necessitate equality in essential attributes like eternity or createdness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's knowledge, do you unconsciously measure it against your own limited understanding? What step will you take today to purify your concept of divine knowledge from anthropomorphic traces?
?
This lesson shows that a shared attribute (like knowledge) does not mean God’s attribute is like ours. Where in your life do you still expect God to act like a human? What is one assumption you will consciously surrender right now?
?
The Mu'tazila used a verse to argue against God's knowledge. We are corrected that general verses do not particularize. Have you ever twisted a verse to fit your own limited reasoning? What will you do to submit to the divine meaning even when it challenges your intellect?

First Face of Mu'tazila Argument on God's Knowledge and Its Refutation
Mu'tazila argument and its refutation regarding divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazila argument against God's knowledge and al-Taftazani's response that a shared relation does not necessitate similarity.

Core Idea
Same relation does not mean same nature — the key to refuting the Mu'tazila.

The Mu'tazila raise a powerful objection: if God knows particulars through His knowledge, then His knowledge and ours relate to the same object in the same way. That would imply they are alike, forcing either His knowledge to be temporal or ours eternal. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and shows why al-Taftazani's simple point — that different things can share a single necessary consequence — dismantles the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first the argument, then the answer.
Pay attention to the phrase 'وجه اللزوم' which introduces the entailment.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The Mu'tazila present a conditional statement, an entailment, and a conclusion. Trace each part: premise, middle step, and impossible consequence.
3
Examine the refutation
al-Taftazani rejects the similarity claim by noting that different things can share a necessary consequence. Consider an example like fire and sun both causing heat.
The key insight is that a shared relation does not force identity of essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وفي نفي العلم تمسكوا في امتناع كونه عالماً بالعلم بوجوه : الأول : أنه لو كان كذلك لزم حدوث علمه، أو قدم علمنا، وكلاهما ظاهر البطلان ، وجه اللزوم أنه إذا تعلق علمنا بشيء مخصوص تعلق به علمه كان كلاهما على وجه واحد، وهو طريق تعلق العلم بالمعلوم. لا أن يكون علمه تعلق الذات، وعلمنا به بطريق تعلق العلم كما في عالميته، وعالميتنا. وإذا كان كلاهما على وجه واحد كانا متماثلين فيلزم استواؤهما في القدم أو الحدوث. والجواب : أن تعلقهما من وجه واحد لا يوجب تماثلهما لجواز اشتراك المختلفات في لازم واحد .
He said: And in denying knowledge, they cling to preventing Him being knowing with knowledge by several faces: First: That if it were so, His knowledge would necessarily be originated, or our knowledge would be eternal, both of which are clearly false. The manner of entailment is that when our knowledge is related to a particular thing, His knowledge is also related to it; both are in one mode—the mode of relation of knowledge to the known—not that His knowledge relates to the essence and our knowledge to it by way of relation of knowledge as in His being knowledgeable (al-'alimiyyah) and our being knowledgeable. And since both are in one mode, they are similar, so they would necessarily be equal in eternity or origination. The answer: Their relation from one aspect does not necessitate their similarity, for it is possible that different things share a single necessary consequence.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents the first 'face' (wajh) of the Mu'tazila argument against God's having knowledge (ilm). They argue that if God knows through a knowledge, then our knowledge and His knowledge relate to the same specific object in the same manner—the way knowledge relates to its known. This is unlike the attribute of being knowledgeable (al-'alimiyyah), where God's relates to His essence and ours relates to our knowledge. Since both knowledges share the same mode of relation (taliq), they would be similar (mutamathilan), leading to an impossible consequence: either His knowledge is originated (hadith) or ours is eternal (qadim). The author's answer is clear: the fact that they share a single relation (wajh wahid) does not force them to be similar, because different entities can share a common necessary consequence (lazim). For example, fire's heat and the sun's heat both cause burning, but they are not identical. Therefore, divine and human knowledge can both relate to the same object without being alike in essence or attribute.

Key Terms

التعلق
التماثل

الوجوه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two knowledges relate to the same object in the same way, they must be identical in kind.
Correction: Different things can share a single necessary consequence; a shared relation does not force similarity of essence.

Checkpoint
In this 'first face' of the argument, what impossible consequence do the Mu'tazila claim follows from God having knowledge?
A) God would have multiple knowledges.
B) His knowledge would be originated or our knowledge would be eternal.
C) Knowledge would be a substance.
D) God would not know particular things.

Key Takeaway
A shared relation between two knowledges does not force them to be identical in nature, because different things can share the same necessary consequence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own knowledge of God, do you secretly imagine it is like God's knowledge in quality, even if you know it is limited? How does this lesson’s correction of the Mu'tazila error expose that pride? What step will you take today to repent from that assumption?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed that because both knowledges relate to the same object in the same mode, they must be alike. In your daily interactions, when do you judge others' actions as identical to yours based on surface appearances? How can remembering that shared consequences do not imply shared nature transform your heart's judgments?
?
If you truly accept that God's knowledge is utterly unlike yours despite both knowing the same truths, how should that affect your awe of Him? Write one concrete change in your worship or supplication that will flow from this truth.

The Magians' Denial of Divine Power over Evil
Magians' dualist objection to divine power over evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Magians' claim that God cannot create evil, and why this leads to dualism contrary to tawhid.

Core Idea
Denying God's power over evil leads to dualism and contradicts Islamic monotheism.

The first major objection to the comprehensiveness of God's power comes from the Magians, who argued that evil cannot come from a good God. This lesson explores their position and its implications for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Magians
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Focus on the claim that God cannot create evil and the introduction of a second creator, Ahriman.
Notice the term 'أهرمن' (Ahriman) as the evil principle.
2
Identify the Magians' reasoning
Why do they deny God's power over evil? They argue that a perfectly good God cannot be the source of evil, so a second creator must exist.
Their reasoning: 'لئلا يلزم كون الواحد خيراً شريراً'.
3
Understand the theological problem
Consider how this violates tawhid (Oneness of God). Islamic theology insists that everything, including evil, is under God's power and will.
The Magians' dualism is rejected because it implies two eternal creators.
4
Reflect on the kalam position
Though the refutation is not fully given here, later arguments affirm that God's power encompasses all, and evil is relative or a privation.
The passage says 'وقد عرفت ذلك' indicating the refutation is known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مخالفة المجوس في شمول القدرة
قال : وخالف المجوس .
في الشرور حتى الأجسام المؤذية والنظام في خلق الجهل والكذب وسائر القبائح، وعباد فيما علم تعالى أنه لا يقع لامتناع وقوعه عنه، والبلخي في مثل مقدور العبد لكونه عبثاً أو سفهاً أو تواضعاً. ورد بعد تسليم الحصر بأنها عوارض لا تمنع التماثل والجبائي في عينه لأن المقدور بين قادرين يستلزم صحة مخلوق بين خالقين، ونحن نمنع اللزوم بناء على أن قدرة العبد غير مؤثرة، وأبو الحسن بطلان اللازم كما في حركة جوهر ملتصق بكفي جاذب. ودافع معاً.

قال : وخالف المجوس المنكرون لشمول قدرة الله تعالى. طوائف منهم المجوس القائلون بأنه لا يقدر على الشرور حتى خلق الأجسام المؤذية . وإنما القادر على ذلك فاعل آخر عندهم (أهرمن) لئلا يلزم كون الواحد خيراً شريراً، وقد عرفت ذلك . ومنهم النظام وأتباعه القائلون بأنه لا يقدر على خلق الجهل والكذب والظلم ، وسائر القبائح ، إذ لو كان خلقها مقدوراً له لجاز صدورها عنه ، واللازم باطل
Opposition of the Magians regarding the comprehensiveness of power. He said: And the Magians opposed. Concerning evils, even harmful bodies, and al-Nazzam [concerning] the creation of ignorance, lying, and all ugly acts; and 'Ubad concerning what the Exalted knows does not occur because its occurrence from Him is impossible; and al-Balkhi concerning something like the object of power of the servant because it would be futile or foolish or condescending. And [he] replied after conceding the restriction that they are accidents that do not prevent similarity; and al-Juba'i concerning its essence because an object of power between two powerful beings entails the correctness of a creature between two creators; and we deny the entailment based on the fact that the servant's power is not effective; and Abu al-Hasan [replied by] invalidating the consequent, like the movement of an atom attached to two hands, one pulling and one pushing simultaneously. He said: And the Magians opposed, denying the comprehensiveness of the power of Allah the Exalted. Among them are sects: the Magians who say that He is not able over evils, even the creation of harmful bodies. Rather, the one able over that is another agent according to them (Ahriman), lest it entail that the One is good and evil, and you have known that. And among them are al-Nazzam and his followers, who say that He is not able to create ignorance, lying, injustice, and all ugly acts, for if their creation were within His power, their occurrence from Him would be permissible, and the consequent is false.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage first presents the matn (original text) listing several groups that deny the comprehensiveness of God's power. The Magians deny God's power over evil, even harmful bodies, attributing them to a second creator (Ahriman). Al-Nazzam denies that God can create ignorance, lying, and other ugly acts. 'Ubad limits power to what God knows will occur, since what is known not to occur cannot be within power. Al-Balkhi restricts God's power over things like the servant's actions, deeming them futile or unbecoming. A reply is given after accepting the restriction that these are accidents that do not negate similarity—this seems to be an interjection. Al-Juba'i argues that if an object were within the power of two agents, it would entail two creators, which is impossible. The author then responds that they deny the entailment because the servant's power is not effective. Abu al-Hasan invalidates the consequent by analogy of an atom pulled by two opposite forces. The commentary then elaborates: it explains the Magians' view that God cannot create evil to avoid duality in the divine nature, and al-Nazzam's view that ugly acts cannot proceed from God because that would be permissible evil. The commentary cuts off before presenting the full refutation.

Key Terms
مجوس
شرور
أهرمن
قادر
خالق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying God's power over evil absolves Him from responsibility for evil.
Correction: This actually implies a second creator, which is polytheism, and does not solve the problem of evil.

Checkpoint
According to the Magians, why can God not create evil?
A) Because evil is not real.
B) Because God is not all-powerful.
C) To avoid the implication that God is both good and evil.
D) Because evil is created by humans.

Key Takeaway
The Magians' denial of God's power over evil leads to dualism, which is incompatible with Islamic monotheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face something painful or unjust, do you find yourself secretly blaming someone or something other than Allah—like your circumstances, other people, or even shaytan—rather than recognizing that Allah alone has power over all events, including the ones that hurt? How will you consciously train your heart to see every single event, both good and seeming evil, as coming from Allah alone, as an act of pure tawhid?
?
The Magians sought to 'protect' God from evil by denying His involvement in it. In your own life, is there a situation where you have tried to 'protect' Allah by attributing a trial to anything other than His will—perhaps to a mistake you made, or to someone else's fault? How does this lesson expose that such thinking actually diminishes Allah's lordship? Will you repent and affirm that nothing happens except by His will, trusting that He is wise and just even when you do not understand?
?
Read the phrase 'lest it entail that the One is good and evil'. The Magians thought that if God created evil, He would be evil. But Islamic theology teaches that Allah's actions are not measured by human standards of good and evil; He is al-Hakim (the All-Wise). In a recent trial, did you question Allah's goodness or wisdom because of the pain you experienced? What does your heart need to let go of to fully submit to Allah's decree, knowing that His power encompasses all things and that He does no injustice?

Rationalist Objections and Kalam Responses
Listing of rationalist objections and kalam responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections of al-Nazzam, 'Ubad, al-Balkhi, al-Juba'i, and Abu al-Hasan to the comprehensiveness of divine power, and the logical responses of kalam theologians.

Core Idea
Kalam theologians defend God's absolute power through precise logical distinctions against rationalist objections.

While the Magians based their denial on the problem of evil, other thinkers raised logical and moral objections to the universality of God's power. This lesson examines these objections and the sophisticated replies developed in Ilm al-Kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage listing objections
Read the Arabic and translation, paying attention to each group's specific claim about what God cannot do.
Al-Nazzam, 'Ubad, al-Balkhi, al-Juba'i, and Abu al-Hasan are mentioned.
2
Distinguish each objection
Al-Nazzam objects to ugly acts, 'Ubad to what God knows won't happen, al-Balkhi to futile actions. Note also the responses.
3
Analyze the kalam replies
The replies include: accidents do not prevent similarity (for al-Balkhi), we deny entailment of two creators (for al-Juba'i), and the example of the atom (for Abu al-Hasan).
These replies show the logical rigor of kalam.
4
Synthesize the lesson
Understand that these objections challenge God's omnipotence, but kalam theologians defend it through careful logical distinctions.
The common principle is that God's power is absolute, and human rational limitations do not constrain it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مخالفة المجوس في شمول القدرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if an action is morally bad, God cannot do it because it would be unjust.
Correction: Kalam holds that God's actions are not subject to human moral evaluation; 'ugly' is a relative term, and God's power is absolute.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nazzam, why can God not create ignorance and lying?
A) Because they are impossible in themselves.
B) Because they are ugly acts that cannot proceed from God.
C) Because they are not real.
D) Because God does not know them.

Key Takeaway
Kalam theologians defend God's absolute power by logically refuting rationalist objections, affirming that no independent constraint limits divine omnipotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself mentally limiting what God can do in your life, like the rationalists who said God could not create certain acts? Identify one area where you have doubted God's absolute power—perhaps a difficulty you think is too big or a sin you think cannot be forgiven. This week, when that doubt arises, consciously say: 'Allah is capable of all things' and then act accordingly.
?
The kalam theologians used precise logic to defend God's power. When you face objections to your faith, do you run from them or engage them? Think of one objection you have heard (or have yourself) about God's power. This week, write down a reasoned response based on what you have learned here, and share it with a friend or keep it as a personal reminder.
?
Al-Nazzam argued that God cannot create ugly acts because that would make Him ugly. Have you ever blamed God for something 'ugly' that happened to you, assuming it could not be from Him? Choose one past trial where you questioned God's goodness. Now, write a prayer acknowledging that God's power and wisdom are above all, and ask Him to reveal His purpose in that trial.

beginner

The Many Opponents of Divine Agency
Lists non-philosopher groups opposing Ash'ari doctrine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various religious and philosophical groups that reject the Ash'ari principle that only Allah is the true agent in existence.

Core Idea
Many groups, not just philosophers, deny that Allah alone acts.

In defending the Ash'ari position that Allah is the sole agent, al-Sanusi lists several opposing factions. These include philosophers, Sabians, astrologers, naturalists, and the Mu'tazila. Each group has its own reasons for attributing causality to other entities. This lesson focuses on the non-philosopher groups mentioned.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on non-philosopher groups
Read from the beginning down to 'وسيأتي' in the Arabic, and the corresponding translation. Identify each group: Sabians, astrologers, naturalists, and Mu'tazila.
The passage is part of a larger discussion; we focus on the part not yet covering philosophers' specific doctrine.
2
Understand each group's attribution
Note that Sabians and astrologers attribute events to spheres and stars. Naturalists attribute them to temperaments and natures, relying on the concept of rotation. Mu'tazila attribute evils and voluntary actions to creatures.
Al-Sanusi brackets these as preliminary mentions; fuller discussion comes later.
3
Recognize the Ash'ari position being opposed
The common thread is that all these groups deny that Allah is the sole agent, instead positing secondary causes. The Ash'ari view affirms tawhid in action.
This sets the stage for the philosophers' more systematic opposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وخالفت الفلاسفة. مخالفة الفلاسفة في الأفلاك والعناصر وما فيها من الحوادث بل فيما سوى العقل الأول. وقد سبق والصائبة والمنجمون في حوادث هذا العالم حيث أسندوها إلى الأفلاك والكواكب بما لها من الأوضاع والحركات الطبيعية حيث أسندوها إلى الأمزجة والطبائع. وغاية متشبثهم الدوران. والمعتزلة في الشرور والقبائح والأفعال الاختيارية للحيوانات وسيأتي. وخالفت الفلاسفة القول بأنه لا مؤثر في الوجود سوى الله تعالى مذهب البعض من أهل السنة كالأشاعرة ومن يجري مجراهم، وخالف فيه أكثر الفرق من المليين وغيرهم. فذهبت الفلاسفة إلى أن الصادر عنه بلا واسطة هو العقل الأول، وهو مصدر عقل ونفس وفلك، وهكذا ترتيب المعلولات مستنداً بعضها إلى البعض. فالفاعل للأفلاك عقول، والحركاتها نفوس، وللحوادث بعض هذه المباديء أو
He said: And the philosophers opposed (the view). The opposition of the philosophers regarding the spheres and the elements and what is in them of events, rather regarding everything other than the First Intellect. And it has preceded, and the Sabians and astrologers regarding the events of this world, when they attributed them to the spheres and stars by virtue of their configurations and natural motions, when they attributed them to temperaments and natures. And the utmost of their clinging is (the notion of) rotation. And the Mu'tazila regarding evils, ugly acts, and the voluntary actions of animals, and it will come. And the philosophers opposed the statement that there is no agent in existence except Allah most high, which is the doctrine of some of the Ahl al-Sunnah like the Ash'aris and those who follow their way. And most of the sects among the religious adherents and others opposed it. So the philosophers held that what emanates from Him without intermediary is the First Intellect, and it is the source of intellect, soul, and sphere, and thus is the order of effects, each depending upon another. So the agents for the spheres are intellects, and their motions are souls, and for events, some of these principles or
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage classifies the opposing factions to the Ash'ari doctrine that only Allah is the true agent in existence. The author first notes the philosophers' opposition regarding the celestial spheres, elements, and all events except the First Intellect (which they consider the first emanation). Then he mentions that the Sabians and astrologers attribute events to the spheres and stars with their configurations and natural motions, and the naturalists (those who attribute events to temperaments and natures) also oppose; their ultimate recourse is the concept of rotation. The Mu'tazila are cited for their views on evils, ugly acts, and animal voluntary actions, which will be discussed later. The core of the philosophers' opposition is their claim that there is no agent besides Allah—they propose an emanation scheme: from Allah emanates the First Intellect, which then gives rise to intellect, soul, and sphere in a hierarchy. Thus, the spheres have intellects as their agents, souls for their motions, and events are caused by some of these principles or [continues].

Key Terms
الصائبة
المنجمون
الأمزجة والطبائع
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That only philosophers oppose the Ash'ari view of divine agency.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the naturalists (أصحاب الأمزجة والطبائع) ultimately cling to as their basis?
A) The spheres
B) The First Intellect
C) Rotation (الدوران)
D) The elements

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari principle of exclusive divine agency is rejected by multiple sects, each with its own secondary causal explanations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the normal events of your day—like getting sick, receiving a promotion, or facing a setback—where do you instinctively locate the primary cause? Do you habitually think of natural processes, human efforts, or coincidences before considering Allah’s sole agency? What would it take to shift that default?
?
This lesson names the Mu'tazila as opposing the Ash'ari view specifically in the realm of evil and voluntary actions. When you witness or experience something you consider evil or unjust, do you find yourself internally blaming a secondary cause (a person, a system, fate) rather than submitting to the decree of Allah? How might this passage challenge your heart to realign?
?
The Sabians and astrologers attribute worldly events to stars and celestial configurations. In what subtle ways do you rely on ‘scientific’ or astrological explanations (e.g., horoscopes, personality tests, economic forecasts) as if they were independent agents, giving them a trust reserved for Allah alone? What step can you take today to practice tying all outcomes directly to Him?

The Philosophers' Emanation Hierarchy
Details philosophers' emanation hierarchy opposing Ash'ari tawhid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' emanation scheme, which posits the First Intellect as the first creation and a chain of causality that opposes the Ash'ari view of Allah as sole agent.

Core Idea
Philosophers replace direct divine action with a chain of emanated intellects and souls.

After listing several opposing factions, al-Sanusi turns to the philosophers' specific doctrine. They agree with the Ash'ari that no agent acts independently, but they introduce a hierarchy of causation: from Allah emanates the First Intellect, then a series of intellects, souls, and spheres. This lesson unpacks that emanation scheme.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on philosophers' opposition
Read from 'وخالفت الفلاسفة القول...' to the end of the available Arabic. Note the claim that philosophers oppose the statement 'there is no agent except Allah'.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the doctrine is incomplete in this excerpt.
2
Identify the emanation chain
The philosophers hold that the first emanation from Allah is the First Intellect. From it emanates another intellect, a soul, and a sphere. This chain continues hierarchically.
This is the core of their opposition: they introduce created agents.
3
Understand the role of intellects and souls
The agents for the spheres are intellects. The motions of the spheres are attributed to souls. Events in the lower world are caused by these principles.
This displaces direct divine causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction

According to the passage, what is the first thing that emanates from Allah without any intermediary?
A) The spheres

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness the regular rising of the sun, do you attribute it directly to Allah's command or to impersonal astronomical laws? How does this passage challenge you to see Allah's immediate agency in every motion?
?
The philosophers assigned intellects and souls as independent agents. In your own life, do you ever treat your skills, health, or opportunities as independent 'causes' rather than direct gifts from Allah? What would change if you internalized 'no agent but Allah'?
?
If you truly believed that every event—from a leaf falling to your heartbeat—is directly caused by Allah without any intermediary, how would your reliance on Him change? What one habit today can you start to feel that reality?

Hanabila and Hashwiyya View: God's Speech as Eternal Letters
Sanusi reports the Hanabila/Hashwiyya claim and ridicules it with absurd examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extreme anthropomorphic view of the Hanabila and Hashwiyya that identifies God's speech with physical sounds and letters, and the evidence of their ignorance as reported by Sanusi.

Core Idea
The Hanabila and Hashwiyya reduce the eternal speech of God to physical sounds and letters, even claiming the book's cover is eternal.

This passage presents a controversial view from two early sects regarding the nature of God's speech. Sanusi reports their claim that the very letters and sounds of the Qur'an, including their order and succession, are eternal and subsist in God's essence. He then provides extreme examples to illustrate the absurdity of their position, showing their confusion between the created physical object and the divine attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the claim and the examples. Notice the flow from assertion to evidence of ignorance.
The passage uses the phrase 'كفى شاهداً على جهلهم' to introduce the examples.
2
Identify the core claim
What exactly did the Hanabila and Hashwiyya say about sounds and letters? They claimed these are eternal (azal) and subsist in God's essence, despite their sequence and succession.
They also held that what is heard from reciters and seen in the book is identical to God's eternal speech.
3
Analyze the examples of ignorance
Sanusi presents two extreme reports: one that the leather binding and cover of the Quran are eternal, and another that the physical body used to write the Quran became eternal after being created. Understand why these are considered absurd.
These examples highlight the mistake of conflating the physical, created object with the uncreated attribute of God.
4
Reflect on the theological lesson
Consider why this view is opposed by mainstream Sunni theology. How does equating physical letters with God's speech lead to problematic implications?
This sets the stage for understanding the Ash'ari position on God's speech as an eternal attribute not composed of letters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قالت الحنابلة والحشوية: إن تلك الأصوات والحروف مع تواليها وترتب بعضها على البعض، وكون الحرف الثاني من كل كلمة مسبوقاً بالحرف المتقدم عليه كانت ثابتة في الأزل، قائمة بذات الباري تعالى وتقدس، وأن المسموع من أصوات القراء والمرئي من أسطر الكتاب نفس كلام الله تعالى القديم، وكفى شاهداً على جهلهم ما نقل عن بعضهم أن الجلدة والغلاف أزليان، وعن بعضهم أن الجسم الذي كتب به القرآن فانتظم حروفاً ورقوماً هو بعينه كلام الله تعالى، وقد صار قديماً، بعدما كان حادثاً.
Then the Hanabila and Hashwiyya said: Those sounds and letters, with their succession and ordering of one after another, and the fact that the second letter of every word is preceded by the preceding letter, were established in eternity (azal), subsisting in the essence of the Creator, exalted and sanctified. And that what is heard from the voices of reciters and seen from the lines of the book is the very same eternal speech of God, exalted. Sufficient as evidence of their ignorance is what is reported from some of them that the bind and cover are eternal, and from some of them that the physical body with which the Qur'an was written, then arranged into letters and inscriptions, is itself the speech of God, exalted, and it became eternal after having been temporal.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents the view of two anthropomorphic sects, the Hanabila and Hashwiyya. They claim that the actual sounds and letters of the Qur'an—including their order, succession, and the sequence of letters—are eternal (thābita fī l-azal) and subsist in God's essence. They further claim that what is heard from the voices of reciters and what is seen in the written text of the book is exactly the eternal speech of God. The author then uses an extreme example to demonstrate the absurdity of their position: some of them claim that the leather binding and cover of the mushaf are eternal. Others claim that the physical object (like the ink and paper) with which the Qur'an was written, after it became arranged into letters and inscriptions, is identical to God's speech, and that it became eternal after having been created. This shows their ignorance, as they conflate the created physical object with the divine attribute.

Key Terms

كلام الله

جلدة
غلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Hanabila view is similar to the Ash'ari view that God's speech is eternal.
Correction: The Ash'ari view holds that God's speech is an eternal attribute, not composed of letters or sounds, which are created. The Hanabila claim that physical letters and sounds are eternal is a distinct and rejected anthropomorphic view.

Checkpoint
What extreme claim is reported about some Hanabila and Hashwiyya regarding the physical book?
A) That the Qur'an is created.
B) That the leather binding and cover are eternal.
C) That God's speech is an attribute not letters.
D) That the reciter's voice is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The Hanabila and Hashwiyya erroneously identify God's eternal speech with physical letters and sounds, leading to absurd conclusions such as considering the book's cover as eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold the mus'haf, do you ever catch yourself venerating the paper and ink more than the divine meaning they convey? How does this lesson expose that subtle association?
?
Have you ever felt anger when a Qur'an is dropped or treated disrespectfully, and in that moment, did you reflect on whether your anger was for the Eternal Speech or for the physical object? What does this lesson demand of you regarding that instinct?
?
If you were to ask yourself honestly, do you sometimes feel that the Qur'an's power lies in its physical recitation or its written form, rather than in the eternal command it contains? What would it mean to truly shift your reverence to the uncreated meaning alone?

The Karramiyya Distinction Between Qawl and Kalam
Sanusi explains the Karramiyya distinction between created speech (qawl) subsisting in God and eternal power (kalam) to speak.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya view that God's eternal kalam is His power to speak, while the temporal letters subsisting in Him are His qawl.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya separate God's eternal power to speak from the temporal letters subsisting in Him.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the Karramiyya position on divine speech, a sectarian view that attempted to navigate between eternal letters and created speech. The Karramiyya distinguished between qawl (temporal letters subsisting in God) and kalam (God's eternal power to speak). Understanding this distinction helps clarify central debates in the theology of God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly. Notice the key terms: كرامية, قول, كلام, قدرة على التكلم, حادث, قديم. Try to identify the two main claims: what is qawl and what is kalam.
The passage is dense but structured; read it once without worrying about details.
2
Understand the Karramiyya rationale
The passage begins by explaining why the Karramiyya adopted this view: they chose a lesser evil. Contradicting the evidence (dalīl) was considered less severe than contradicting necessary knowledge (ḍarūra). This shaped their position on the temporal nature of letters.
Think about how this reasoning contrasts with other schools.
3
Identify the distinction between qawl and kalam
The Karramiyya defined qawl as the temporal composed letters subsisting in God, while kalam is God's eternal power to speak. This allowed them to affirm that the Qur'an (as letters) is created but still subsists in God.
Compare this with the Ash'ari view that God's speech is eternal and not composed of letters.
4
Examine the general rule for temporal entities
The passage concludes with a general principle: anything with a beginning that subsists in the essence is temporal by power (bi-l-qudra) and not made by another; anything separate from the essence is made by His word 'Be' (kun). This reinforces their system.
This rule is used to classify qawl (subsisting) vs. created things (separate).
5
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why other Sunni schools reject this view. The Karramiyya position still makes God a locus of temporal events, which contradicts divine immutability according to mainstream theologians.
This passage is part of a broader polemic; Sanusi likely critiques it later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما رأت الكرامية أن بعض الشر أهون من البعض، وأن مخالفة الضرورة أشنع من مخالفة الدليل ذهبوا إلى أن المنتظم من الحروف المسموعة حدوثه قائم بذات الله تعالى، وأنه قول الله تعالى لا كلامه، وإنما كلامه قدرته على التكلم وهو قديم، وقوله حادث لا محدث وفرقوا بينهما، بأن كل ما له ابتداء إن كان قائماً بالذات فهو حادث بالقدرة غير محدث، وإن كان مبايناً للذات فهو محدث بقوله كن لا بالقدرة.
And when the Karramiyya saw that some evil is lesser than others, and that contradicting what is necessary (ḍarūra) is worse than contradicting evidence (dalīl), they went to the position that the [entity] composed of heard letters—its temporality subsists in the essence of God, exalted—and that it is the qawl (saying) of God, not His kalam (speech). Rather, His kalam is His power to speak (qudra ʿalā l-takallum), which is eternal. And His qawl is temporal (ḥādith), not made temporal by another (lā muḥdith). And they distinguished between them: that everything with a beginning, if it subsists in the essence, is temporal by power (bi-l-qudra), not made by another (ghayr muḥdath). And if it is separate from the essence, it is made temporal by His word 'Be' (kun), not by power.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents the Karramiyya position on God's speech. The author frames their reasoning as a choice of the lesser evil: they found it less objectionable to contradict the evidence (dalīl) that God cannot be a locus of temporal events than to contradict the necessary knowledge (ḍarūra) that speech composed of letters is temporal. So they claimed that the composed letters (al-muntazam min al-ḥurūf al-masmū‘a) are temporal (ḥādith) but subsist in God's essence. However, they distinguished between qawl (saying) and kalam (speech). According to them, the temporal letters are God's qawl, not His kalam. His kalam is His power to speak (qudra ʿalā l-takallum), which is eternal. They then elaborate: anything that has a beginning and subsists in the essence is temporal by God's power (bi-l-qudra) and is not caused by another (ghayr muḥdath). Anything separate from the essence is made temporal by God's word 'Be' (kun), not by power. This is a nuanced attempt to avoid both the Hanbali/Hashwiyya claim of eternal letters and the Mu‘tazilī claim that speech is created external to God.

Key Terms
كرامية
قول
كلام
قدرة على التكلم
حادث
قديم
محدث
كن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Karramiyya view is a compromise between eternal and created speech.
Correction: The passage shows they maintained created letters subsisting in God, which Sunnis reject because it makes God a locus of temporal events.

Checkpoint
According to the Karramiyya, what is God's eternal kalam?
A) The letters of the Qur'an.
B) His power to speak (qudra ʿalā l-takallum).
C) His command 'Be' (kun).
D) The sound heard from reciters.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya separated God's eternal power to speak (kalam) from the temporal letters subsisting in Him (qawl), creating a unique position that attempted to reconcile scriptural evidence with necessary knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Karramiyya were willing to twist evidence (dalīl) to avoid contradicting what they felt was necessary knowledge (ḍarūra). When you read the Qur'an, do you ever interpret it in a way that fits your preconceived notions rather than letting it challenge you? What would it cost you to submit fully to its clear meanings?
?
They claimed that God's spoken words (qawl) are temporal, subsisting in Him but not eternal. If you truly believed the Qur'an you recite is the eternal speech of God, not a temporal product of power, how would your reverence, focus, and desperation for its guidance change? Is that how you recite it now?
?
The Karramiyya held that the letters of the Qur'an subsist in God's essence. But the Sunnis affirm His speech as an eternal attribute. Do you see the words you recite as merely a copy of something created, or as the very expression of God's eternal attribute? How does that difference affect your relationship with the Book?

The Mu'tazila Position on God's Speech: Created and Temporal
Sanusi presents the Mu'tazila's reasoning for a created and temporal divine speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila considered God's speech to be created and temporal, and the debate among them about using the term 'makhluq'.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, God's speech is created in a body, not an attribute of God.

The Mu'tazila faced a dilemma: they affirmed that the Qur'an is composed of letters and is temporal, but they also affirmed that God's essence cannot be a locus for temporal events. Their solution was to say that God creates speech in some created body. However, some were uncomfortable calling it 'created' (makhluq) because the term might suggest fabrication. This lesson establishes the foundational Mu'tazila position on divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the logical steps: (1) speech is letters, (2) it is temporal, (3) temporal cannot subsist in God, (4) therefore God creates speech in some body.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the logical argument
Work through the reasoning: why did the Mu'tazila reject that speech subsists in God? Because they assumed speech is composed of letters and sounds, which are temporal. And temporal cannot be in the eternal essence.
This is the key to their position.
3
Notice the disagreement on terminology
Some Mu'tazila avoided the word 'makhluq' (created) because it could imply fabrication or falsehood. The majority, however, allowed it. Ask yourself why they would be sensitive about that term.
The term 'khalq' can mean 'creation' but also 'fabrication' as in iftira'.
4
Reflect on the implications
If speech is created in a body (like Jibril or the Preserved Tablet), then it is not an attribute of God. This sets the stage for the sub-views of Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة لما قطعوا بأنه المنتظم من الحروف، وأنه حادث، والحادث لا يقوم بذات الله تعالى. ذهبوا إلى أن معنى كونه متكلماً أنه خلق الكلام في بعض الأجسام، واحترز بعضهم عن إطلاق لفظ المخلوق عليه لما فيه من إيهام الخلق والافتراء، وجوزه الجمهور ثم المختار عندهم، وهو مذهب أبي هاشم ومن تبعه من المتأخرين أنه من جنس الأصوات والحروف ولا يحتمل البقاء حتى إن ما خلق مرقوماً في اللوح المحفوظ أو كتب في المصحف لا يكون قرآناً، وإنما القرآن ما قرأه القارىء، وخلقه الباري من الأصوات المتقطعة والحروف المنتظمة، وذهب الجبائي إلى أنه من جنس غير الحروف تسمع عند سماع الأصوات، وتوجد بنظم الحروف، وبكتبها، ويبقى عند المكتوب والحفظ، ويقوم باللوح المحفوظ وبكل مصحف وكل لسان، ومع هذا فهو واحد لا يزداد بازدياد المصاحف، ولا ينقص بنقصانها، ولا يبطل ببطلانها.
And the Mu‘tazila, since they were certain that it [speech] is composed of letters, and that it is temporal, and that the temporal cannot subsist in the essence of God, exalted, went to the position that the meaning of His being speaking is that He created speech in some bodies. And some of them avoided applying the term 'created' (makhluq) to it because of the implication of fabrication and slander (khalq wa-iftirā'), but the majority permitted it. Then the preferred view among them, which is the school of Abu Hashim and those who followed him among the later ones, is that it is of the kind of sounds and letters and does not admit of endurance (lā yaḥtamil al-baqā'), such that what is created as an inscription in the Preserved Tablet or written in the muṣḥaf is not the Qur'an; rather the Qur'an is what the reader recites, and the Creator creates it from discontinuous sounds and ordered letters. And al-Jubba'i went to [the view] that it is of a kind other than letters, heard when sounds are heard, and exists by the order of letters and their writing, and remains with the written and the memorized, and subsists in the Preserved Tablet, in every muṣḥaf, and on every tongue. Moreover, it is one, not increasing with the increase of muṣḥafs, nor decreasing with their decrease, nor invalidated with their invalidation.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu’tazila position on God's speech. They were certain that speech as composed of letters is temporal (ḥādith) and that the temporal cannot subsist in God's essence. Therefore, they interpreted God being mutakallim as meaning that He creates speech in some bodies (like the angel Jibrīl or the Preserved Tablet). Some Mu‘tazila avoided calling it 'created' (makhluq) because it might imply fabrication, but the majority allowed it. Within this school, there were two main sub-views: The preferred view (mukhtār) is that of Abu Hashim (al-Jubbā’ī's son). He held that speech is of the kind of sounds and letters and does not endure; it is created in a body and perishes immediately. Thus, what is created as an inscription in the Preserved Tablet or written in the muṣḥaf is not the Qur'an; the Qur'an is only what the reader recites at the moment of recitation, created by God from discontinuous sounds and ordered letters. The second view is that of al-Jubba'i (Abu Hashim's father and teacher). He held that speech is of a kind other than letters (jins ghayr al-ḥurūf) that is heard when sounds are heard, and exists by the order of letters and their writing. It remains with the written text and in memory, and subsists in the Preserved Tablet, in every muṣḥaf, and on every tongue. Despite being in multiple places, it is one, not increasing or decreasing with the multiplication or loss of physical copies. This is an attempt to preserve the unity of the Qur'an while affirming its createdness.

Key Terms
معتزلة
مخلوق
حادث
كلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila believed God's speech is literally sounds and letters subsisting in God.
Correction: The Mu'tazila actually denied that speech subsists in God; they said it is created in a body. They considered sound and letters to be temporal creations.

Checkpoint
Why did the Mu'tazila say that God creates speech in some bodies rather than it subsisting in Him?
A) Because they believed speech is eternal.
B) Because they thought speech is composed of temporal letters and cannot be in the eternal essence.
C) Because they wanted to deny that God speaks at all.
D) Because they followed the Ash'aris.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila considered God's speech to be a created entity in a body, not a divine attribute, because they assumed speech is temporal letters.

Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i on Divine Speech
Sanusi elaborates on the two main Mu'tazila sub-views on divine speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differences between Abu Hashim's view that divine speech is sounds/letters without endurance and al-Jubba'i's view that it is a non-letter entity that remains in multiple places.

Core Idea
Abu Hashim: speech perishes instantly; al-Jubba'i: speech remains and is one.

Within the Mu'tazila school, there were two main sub-views on the nature of the created speech. Abu Hashim held that it consists of sounds and letters that do not last; thus the Qur'an is only the recitation at the moment it is recited. Al-Jubba'i, however, posited a different kind of entity that persists in written texts and memories, while remaining one. This lesson details these two positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of Abu Hashim's view
Read the passage on Abu Hashim. Note: speech is sounds/letters, no endurance. Therefore, the inscription in the Preserved Tablet is not the Qur'an; only the recitation is.
Abu Hashim was the son of al-Jubba'i.
2
Read al-Jubba'i's view
Al-Jubba'i held that speech is a kind other than letters, it is heard when sounds are heard, exists by order of letters, and remains. It subsists in the Preserved Tablet, every mus-haf, and on every tongue, yet it is one.
This view tries to preserve the unity of the Qur'an.
3
Compare the two views
Contrast: Abu Hashim's speech is transient; al-Jubba'i's is enduring. Abu Hashim says the mus-haf contains writing that is not Qur'an; al-Jubba'i says the Qur'an actually subsists in the mus-haf.
Both agree speech is created, but differ on its nature.
4
Ask: why do these differences matter?
The disagreement reflects deeper questions about the relationship between the divine word and its physical manifestations. It also shows the internal diversity within the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة لما قطعوا بأنه المنتظم من الحروف، وأنه حادث، والحادث لا يقوم بذات الله تعالى. ذهبوا إلى أن معنى كونه متكلماً أنه خلق الكلام في بعض الأجسام، واحترز بعضهم عن إطلاق لفظ المخلوق عليه لما فيه من إيهام الخلق والافتراء، وجوزه الجمهور ثم المختار عندهم، وهو مذهب أبي هاشم ومن تبعه من المتأخرين أنه من جنس الأصوات والحروف ولا يحتمل البقاء حتى إن ما خلق مرقوماً في اللوح المحفوظ أو كتب في المصحف لا يكون قرآناً، وإنما القرآن ما قرأه القارىء، وخلقه الباري من الأصوات المتقطعة والحروف المنتظمة، وذهب الجبائي إلى أنه من جنس غير الحروف تسمع عند سماع الأصوات، وتوجد بنظم الحروف، وبكتبها، ويبقى عند المكتوب والحفظ، ويقوم باللوح المحفوظ وبكل مصحف وكل لسان، ومع هذا فهو واحد لا يزداد بازدياد المصاحف، ولا ينقص بنقصانها، ولا يبطل ببطلانها.
And the Mu‘tazila, since they were certain that it [speech] is composed of letters, and that it is temporal, and that the temporal cannot subsist in the essence of God, exalted, went to the position that the meaning of His being speaking is that He created speech in some bodies. And some of them avoided applying the term 'created' (makhluq) to it because of the implication of fabrication and slander (khalq wa-iftirā'), but the majority permitted it. Then the preferred view among them, which is the school of Abu Hashim and those who followed him among the later ones, is that it is of the kind of sounds and letters and does not admit of endurance (lā yaḥtamil al-baqā'), such that what is created as an inscription in the Preserved Tablet or written in the muṣḥaf is not the Qur'an; rather the Qur'an is what the reader recites, and the Creator creates it from discontinuous sounds and ordered letters. And al-Jubba'i went to [the view] that it is of a kind other than letters, heard when sounds are heard, and exists by the order of letters and their writing, and remains with the written and the memorized, and subsists in the Preserved Tablet, in every muṣḥaf, and on every tongue. Moreover, it is one, not increasing with the increase of muṣḥafs, nor decreasing with their decrease, nor invalidated with their invalidation.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu’tazila position on God's speech. They were certain that speech as composed of letters is temporal (ḥādith) and that the temporal cannot subsist in God's essence. Therefore, they interpreted God being mutakallim as meaning that He creates speech in some bodies (like the angel Jibrīl or the Preserved Tablet). Some Mu‘tazila avoided calling it 'created' (makhluq) because it might imply fabrication, but the majority allowed it. Within this school, there were two main sub-views: The preferred view (mukhtār) is that of Abu Hashim (al-Jubbā’ī's son). He held that speech is of the kind of sounds and letters and does not endure; it is created in a body and perishes immediately. Thus, what is created as an inscription in the Preserved Tablet or written in the muṣḥaf is not the Qur'an; the Qur'an is only what the reader recites at the moment of recitation, created by God from discontinuous sounds and ordered letters. The second view is that of al-Jubba'i (Abu Hashim's father and teacher). He held that speech is of a kind other than letters (jins ghayr al-ḥurūf) that is heard when sounds are heard, and exists by the order of letters and their writing. It remains with the written text and in memory, and subsists in the Preserved Tablet, in every muṣḥaf, and on every tongue. Despite being in multiple places, it is one, not increasing or decreasing with the multiplication or loss of physical copies. This is an attempt to preserve the unity of the Qur'an while affirming its createdness.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all Mu'tazila agreed that the Qur'an is the written text in mus-hafs.
Correction: Abu Hashim taught that the written text is not the Qur'an; only the recited speech is. Al-Jubba'i disagreed and said the Qur'an subsists in the written text.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hashim, what is the Qur'an considered to be?
A) The letters written in the mus-haf.
B) The speech subsisting in God's essence.
C) The reader's recitation created from discontinuous sounds and letters.
D) An eternal attribute.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim sees the Qur'an as a transient recited event, while al-Jubba'i sees it as an enduring single entity that pervades multiple copies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a muṣḥaf, do you behave as if the words in your hands are the actual speech of Allah or merely a representation? Abū Hāshim said the muṣḥaf is not the Qur'an. If that thought unsettles you, ask yourself: does your treatment of the muṣḥaf reflect a deep conviction that these pages contain the words of your Lord? This week, before opening the muṣḥaf, pause for three seconds with the intention of reverence, and commit to handling it as you would if you believed it is truly Allah's speech.
?
Abū Hāshim believed that the true Qur'an exists only in the moment of recitation. If you only value your recitation when you are doing it, but neglect memorization, reflection, and acting upon it, are you unwittingly living out his view? What would change in your daily relationship with the Qur'an if you truly believed every letter you recite is a unique, unrepeatable gift from Allah? Choose one action this week to make your recitation more present and intentional.
?
Al-Jubbā'ī held that the created speech is one and remains in all copies. If you believe the Qur'an is uncreated and eternal, does your practice reflect the awe that such a belief demands? For instance, when you listen to recitation while driving or working, do you give it your full attention? If your belief is correct, then every sound you hear is the eternal speech of Allah—how can you afford to be distracted? Commit to listening to one recitation this week with complete focus, as if hearing it for the first time.

Refutation of Mu'tazilite and Hanbalite Objections to Divine Speech
Refutes two objections to divine speech as a subsisting attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite and Hanbalite objections to the definition of speech as a subsisting attribute and the Ash'ari response that focuses on meaning over endurance.

Core Idea
Speech subsists by its meaning, not by the endurance of audible letters.

In this passage, the author defends the Ash'ari view that divine speech is a meaning (ma'na) subsisting in God. Two major objections are raised: the Mu'tazila argue that the definition cannot apply to human speech because it lacks endurance, and the Hanabila claim that instantaneous letters could be ancient. The response clarifies that the active participle considers meaning, not endurance, and that flowing accidents suffice with partial realization. This nuanced argument preserves the timelessness of God's speech while accounting for human attribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections carefully
Read the passage once to identify the two objections. Note the specific claims: Mu'tazila on endurance, Hanabila on instantaneous letters.
2
Identify the common thread
Both objections target the necessity of endurance or order in speech. Consider why these are problematic for the Ash'ari position.
3
Unpack the response
Examine how the author uses the concept of 'flowing accidents' and the sufficiency of 'clothing with some parts' to refute the objections. Understand why meaning is key.
This step connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن اعترض من قبل المعتزلة أنه لو كان المتكلم من قام به الكلام، لما صح إطلاقه حقيقة على المتكلم بالكلام الحسي، لأنه لا بقاء له ولا اجتماع لأجزائه، حتى يقوم بشيء. ولو سلّم ، فإنما يقوم بلسانه لا بذاته، وأيضاً لما صح قولهم : الأمير يتكلم بلسان الوزير والجني يتكلم بلسان المصروع . ومن قبل الحنابلة أن المنتظم من الحروف قد لا يكون مترتب الأجزاء بل دفعياً كالقائم بنفس الحافظ ، وكالحاصل على الورقة من طابع فيه نقش الكلام. وإنما لزوم الترتيب في التلفظ والقراءة لعدم مساعدة الآلة. فالقرآن الذي هو اسم للنظم والمعنى جميعاً لا يمتنع أن يكون قديماً قائماً بذات الباري تعالى أجيب بأن كون المتكلم من قام به الكلام ثابت عرفاً ولغة ، وكون المنتظم من الحروف المسموعة مترتب الأجزاء ممتنع البقاء ثابت ضرورة، وما ذكرتم سنداً لمنعها تمويه . أما الأول : فلأن المعتبر في اسم الفاعل وجوب المعنى لابقاؤه سيما في الأعراض السيالة ، كالمتحرك والمتكلم . ولو سلم فيكفي التلبس ببعض أجزائه ولا يشترط القيام بكل جزء من أجزاء
For if an objection is raised from the Mu'tazila: that if the speaker were the one in whom speech subsists, then it would not be correct to apply it truly to the speaker of sensible speech, because it has no endurance nor gathering of its parts, so that it may subsist in something. And if it is granted, it subsists only by his tongue, not by his essence. Also, their saying: 'The prince speaks with the tongue of the vizier' and 'the jinn speaks with the tongue of the possessed' would not be correct. And from the Hanabila: that the composition of letters may not have ordered parts but may be instantaneous, like what subsists in the mind of the memorizer, and like what is obtained on paper from a stamp that has the impression of speech. The necessity of order in pronunciation and reading is only due to the lack of assistance from the instrument. So the Qur'an, which is a name for both the composition (nazm) and the meaning (ma'na), is not impossible to be pre-eternal and subsisting in the essence of God Almighty. The answer: That the speaker being the one in whom speech subsists is established customarily and linguistically, and that the composition of audible letters has ordered parts and is impossible to endure is established necessarily. What you mentioned as a proof against them is deception. As for the first [objection]: because what is considered in the active participle is the meaning, not its endurance, especially in flowing accidents (a'rad sayyala), like the mover and the speaker. And if it is granted, then being clothed with some parts is sufficient and subsistence by every part is not required.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage deals with two objections to the earlier proof that divine speech is a meaning (ma'na) rather than letters. The first objection comes from the Mu'tazila: if 'speaker' means one in whom speech subsists, then a human speaking sensible (audible) speech cannot truly be called a speaker because sensible speech has no enduring existence in the speaker—it disappears as soon as it is uttered. Also, if granted that it subsists, it would be in the tongue not the essence. They also cite examples like 'the prince speaks with the tongue of the vizier' to show that the speaker can be attributed to one who does not produce speech from himself. The second objection is from the Hanabila: they argue that a composition of letters can be instantaneous (like the mental image in a memorizer or the impression on paper from a stamp), so there is no necessary ordering of parts. The order perceived in pronunciation is due to the limitation of the instrument (the tongue). Therefore, the Qur'an, which is both the composition (nazm) and the meaning, could be pre-eternal and subsist in God's essence. The response answers that the definition of 'speaker' as one in whom speech subsists is established by custom and language, and the fact that ordered audible letters cannot endure is a necessary truth. What the objectors present as a proof is mere deception. Regarding the first objection, the key point is that in active participles (ism fa'il), what matters is the meaning, not the endurance; for flowing accidents like motion and speech, it is enough that the attribute is realized at some moment. Even if endurance were required, it would suffice that the person is 'clothed with' some parts of the speech, not every part.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الحنابلة
النظم
القدم
التلبس
الأعراض السيالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Hanbali argument about instantaneous letters is a valid refutation of the necessity of temporal sequence.
Correction: The response shows that the necessity of order is not about the letters themselves but about the meaning; flowing accidents do not require endurance.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazilite objection to defining 'speaker' as the one in whom speech subsists?
A) That speech can be instantaneous
B) That sensible speech has no endurance and cannot subsist in a person
C) That the Qur'an is both order and meaning
D) That speakers are only those who create speech

Key Takeaway
The attribute of speech is about the meaning, not the physical letters, and it does not require the letters to endure or be produced in time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you consider the meaning settling in your heart, or are you merely moving your tongue? What would change if you focused solely on the meaning today?
?
The passage argues that speech is about meaning, not the endurance of letters. In your daily conversations, do you prioritize the meaning you convey, or the form and permanence of your words? How does that reflect your understanding of God's speech?
?
If you truly believed that the Qur'an's meaning subsists in God's essence without need for sequence, how would that transform your experience of reading it slowly versus quickly? Will you test that belief in your next recitation?
