Mu'tazila Objections to Moses' Request
Sanusi presents two Mu'tazila objections and refutes the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Mu'tazila objections to the proof from Moses' request and Sanusi's refutation of the second objection.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila reinterpretations of Moses' request contradict the apparent meaning and context.

Sanusi continues his defense of the possibility of seeing Allah by addressing objections from the Mu'tazila. They claim that Moses did not actually ask for vision but for necessary knowledge or a sign. In this passage, Sanusi presents these objections and begins to refute them, focusing on the inconsistency with the Quranic response and the broader context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two objections
The first objection claims Moses did not ask for vision but for its necessary consequence (necessary knowledge). The second claims a deletion of the genitive, meaning Moses asked for a sign.
These are both attempts to avoid the apparent meaning of the verse.
3
Analyze Sanusi's refutation of the second objection
Sanusi argues that the second objection violates the apparent meaning and does not match God's answer 'You shall not see Me.' He also points out contextual problems: Moses already had signs, and the conditional on stability is inconsistent.
The refutation of the first objection is not fully given in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترضت المعتزلة بوجوه: الأول: أن موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام لم يطلب الرؤية، بل عبر بها لازمها الذي هو العلم الضروري. الثاني: أنه على حذف المضاف، والمعنى أرني آية من آياتك، أنظر إلى آياتك، وكلاهما فاسد لمخالفته الظاهر بلا ضرورة ولعدم مطابقته الجواب أعني قوله: ولن تراني لأنه نفي لرؤية الله تعالى بإجماع المعتزلة لا للعلم الضروري، ولا لرؤية الآية، والعلامة. كيف وموسى عليه الصلاة والسلام عالم بربه سمع كلامه، وجعل يناجيه ويخاطبه، واختص من عنده بآيات كثيرة؟ فما معنى طلب العلم الضروري، واندكاك الجبل أعظم آية من آياته فكيف يستقيم نفي رؤية الآية، وأيضاً الآية إنما هي عند اندكاك الجبل لا استقراره فكيف يصح تعليق رؤيتها بالاستقرار؟ وأيضاً الرؤية المقرونة بالنظر الموصول بإلى نص في الرؤية
And the Mu'tazila objected with several points: The first: that Moses, peace be upon him, did not request vision, but rather expressed by it its necessary consequence, which is necessary knowledge. The second: that it involves the deletion of the genitive, and the meaning is 'Show me a sign from Your signs, so that I may look at Your signs.' Both are invalid because they contradict the apparent meaning without necessity, and because they do not correspond to the answer, namely His saying: 'You shall not see Me,' because it is a negation of seeing Allah the Exalted by the consensus of the Mu'tazila, not of necessary knowledge, nor of seeing a sign. How? While Moses, peace be upon him, knew his Lord, heard His speech, and used to converse and speak with Him, and was singled out by Him for many signs? So what is the meaning of requesting necessary knowledge? And the mountain's being crushed is a greater sign than His signs, so how can the negation of seeing the sign be correct? Also, the sign is only at the crushing of the mountain, not at its stability, so how can the conditional of its vision upon stability be valid? Also, vision coupled with the word 'look' connected with 'to' is an explicit text about vision.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents two Mu'tazila objections to the proof from Moses' request. The first objection claims that Moses did not actually ask for vision (ru'ya), but rather used the term 'vision' to refer to its necessary consequence, which is 'necessary knowledge' ('ilm daruri). The second objection claims that the phrase 'Show me (arini)' involves a deleted genitive, so the real meaning is 'Show me a sign from Your signs, so I may look at Your signs.' The author then refutes both. The refutation of the first is implied to be invalid because the answer (God's response 'You shall not see Me') negates vision, not necessary knowledge, by consensus of the Mu'tazila themselves. The refutation of the second is given in detail: it contradicts the apparent meaning without necessity, and does not match the answer. Furthermore, Moses already knew God directly and had seen many signs, so asking for a sign is meaningless. Also, the sign mentioned is the mountain's crushing, not its stability, so the conditional on stability does not fit. The passage ends with the author asserting that the phrase 'look at the mountain' with 'to' (ila) is a clear text for vision. The refutation of the first objection is not completed in this window, and the text breaks off.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
العلم الضروري
حذف المضاف
الآية
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila objections are valid and provide an alternative interpretation of the verse.
Correction: Sanusi shows that these interpretations contradict the apparent meaning and are inconsistent with the Quranic answer and context.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is wrong with the Mu'tazila interpretation that Moses asked for a sign?
A) It requires deleting words from the text.
B) The answer negates vision, not a sign.
C) Moses already had many signs.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila reinterpretations of Moses' request fail because they violate the apparent meaning and are inconsistent with the Quranic answer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been tempted to reinterpret a clear Quranic or prophetic text to fit a comfortable philosophical assumption? What did you sacrifice in that interpretation?
?
Moses already knew Allah intimately and had witnessed many signs, yet he still yearned to see Him. Is your desire for knowledge of Allah merely intellectual, or does it include a deep longing for direct vision in the afterlife? What are you doing today to nurture that longing?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were protecting Allah's transcendence by reinterpreting His words. Have you ever 'protected' Allah by twisting His revelation? Are you willing to accept the apparent meaning even when it humbles your intellect?

First Objection: Argument Against Self-Evident Truth
First Mu'tazilite objection: proof contradicts necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazilite objection: that the Ash'arite proof contradicts self-evident knowledge of human choice.

Core Idea
Self-evident truth cannot be argued away.

The Mu'tazilites begin by challenging the very foundation of the Ash'arite proof. They claim the proof argues against what we know directly through self-evidence: that we have the power to choose and act. This lesson presents that objection and its basis in daily experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection text
Read the Arabic passage for the first objection, then its translation. Notice how it contrasts 'argumentation' with 'self-evident truth'.
The Mu'tazilites assert that we know our power and choice by necessity, not by inference.
2
Understand the basis
Identify the claim: that we know directly we can do or omit as we wish. This is the core of their objection.
3
Consider the response
Think about how an Ash'arite might respond. Does the self-evident knowledge really contradict the proof? The commentary will explore this.
The Ash'arites would argue that this self-evident knowledge is compatible with their position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمعتزلة ههنا اعتراضات أحدها أن ما ذكرتم استدلال في مقابلة الضرورة فلا يستحق الجواب، وذلك لأنا نعلم بالضرورة أن لنا مكنة واختياراً، وأنا إن شئنا الفعل فعلنا وإن شئنا الترك تركنا . وثانيها : أنه جار في فعل الباري فيلزم أن يكون موجباً لا مختاراً، وذلك لأن جميع ما لابد منه في إيجاد العالم إن كان حاصلاً في الأزل، لزم قدم العالم وصدوره عن الباري بطريق الوجوب من غير تمكن من الترك لامتناع التخلف عن تمام العلة، وإن لم يكن حاصلاً ينتقل الكلام إلى حدوث الأمر الذي لا بد منه ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي إلى أمر أزلي يلزم معه المؤثر. ويعود المحذور. وثالثها : أن ترجيح المختار أحد المتساويين جائز كما في طريقي الهارب ، وقدحي العطّشنان لأن الإرادة صفة شأنها الترجيح والتخصيص من غير احتياج إلى مرجح، وإنما المحال الترجح بلا مرجح . ورابعها: أن المرجح الذي لا يكون من العبد هو تعلق الإرادة وخلوص الداعي، ووجوب الفعل معه لا ينافي الاختيار والتمكن من الفعل والترك بالنظر إلى القدرة .
And the Mu'tazilites have here objections: first, that what you have mentioned is argumentation against a self-evident truth, so it does not deserve an answer, because we know necessarily that we have power and choice, and that if we wish to do we do, and if we wish to omit we omit. Second, that it applies to the act of God, so He would be necessitating not choosing, because all that is necessary for the existence of the world, if it was present eternally, the world would be eternal and would proceed from God by way of necessity without ability to omit due to impossibility of delay from the complete cause; and if it was not present, the discourse transfers to the temporality of the necessary thing, and there is no infinite regress, but it ends in an eternal thing with which the effect is necessary, and the same problem returns. Third, that the choice of the free agent among equals is permissible, as in the two paths of the fugitive and the two cups of the two thirsty ones, because the will is an attribute whose function is to give preponderance and specification without needing a relative weight; the impossible is only the relative weighing without a relative weight. Fourth, that the relative weight that is not from the servant is the attachment of will and sincerity of motive, and the necessity of the act with it does not negate choice and the ability to do and omit with respect to power.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage enumerates four objections raised by the Mu'tazilites against the third proof. The first objection argues that the proof contradicts self-evident knowledge: we directly know we have power and choice to act or not. The second objection turns the argument against God: if God's act required a relative weight, it would make the world eternal and God necessitated, not free. The third objection asserts that choice does not require an external relative weight; the will itself is sufficient to specify among equals, as illustrated by a fugitive choosing a path or a thirsty person choosing between two cups of water. The fourth objection concedes that the relative weight comes from the will and motive, but this does not negate choice; the ability to do or omit remains relative to power, not to the actual occurrence. These objections are classic Mu'tazilite defenses of human freedom and critique of Ash'arite determinism.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
ضرورة
مكنة
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first Mu'tazilite objection, why does the Ash'arite proof deserve no answer?
A) Because it is logically contradictory.
B) Because it argues against a self-evident truth.
C) Because it denies God's power.
D) Because it relies on invalid premises.

Key Takeaway
The first objection asserts that the Ash'arite proof is invalid because it contradicts our direct, self-evident knowledge of choice and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision—even a small one—do you ever stop to consider that your feeling of free choice might be an illusion? If the Mu'tazilite claim that we know necessarily we have power and choice resonates with your actual experience, how can you reconcile that with the Ash'arite doctrine of acquisition? Take a moment to journal about a specific choice you made today and whether your inner sense of freedom matched your theological belief.
?
The Mu'tazilites insist that argument cannot overturn self-evident truth. Is there any self-evident truth about your own moral responsibility that you have been ignoring or explaining away to fit a theological system? Name one experience of personal responsibility that you have not fully accounted for in your understanding of qadr, and commit to not dismissing it.

Second Objection: Argument Applies to God
Second objection: Ash'arite proof leads to God being necessitated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Mu'tazilite objection: that the Ash'arite argument, if valid, would make God a necessary agent and imply the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
Turn the argument against its proponent.

The Mu'tazilites cleverly apply the same reasoning used for human acts to the divine act of creation. They argue that if the Ash'arite proof is correct, it would force the conclusion that God is not a free chooser but a necessitated cause, which is unacceptable. This lesson unpacks that objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation. Note how it parallels the structure of the third proof but applied to God.
The objection considers two scenarios: whether the requirements for creation were present eternally or not.
2
Understand the two cases
In the first case, the world would be eternal. In the second, an infinite regress leads to an eternal cause that still necessitates.
Both cases lead to necessitation, denying divine free will.
3
Reflect on the challenge
This objection is a dilemma: either way, God becomes a necessary agent. How would an Ash'arite respond?
The Ash'arite would distinguish between the necessity of existence and the necessity of choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمعتزلة ههنا اعتراضات أحدها أن ما ذكرتم استدلال في مقابلة الضرورة فلا يستحق الجواب، وذلك لأنا نعلم بالضرورة أن لنا مكنة واختياراً، وأنا إن شئنا الفعل فعلنا وإن شئنا الترك تركنا . وثانيها : أنه جار في فعل الباري فيلزم أن يكون موجباً لا مختاراً، وذلك لأن جميع ما لابد منه في إيجاد العالم إن كان حاصلاً في الأزل، لزم قدم العالم وصدوره عن الباري بطريق الوجوب من غير تمكن من الترك لامتناع التخلف عن تمام العلة، وإن لم يكن حاصلاً ينتقل الكلام إلى حدوث الأمر الذي لا بد منه ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي إلى أمر أزلي يلزم معه المؤثر. ويعود المحذور. وثالثها : أن ترجيح المختار أحد المتساويين جائز كما في طريقي الهارب ، وقدحي العطّشنان لأن الإرادة صفة شأنها الترجيح والتخصيص من غير احتياج إلى مرجح، وإنما المحال الترجح بلا مرجح . ورابعها: أن المرجح الذي لا يكون من العبد هو تعلق الإرادة وخلوص الداعي، ووجوب الفعل معه لا ينافي الاختيار والتمكن من الفعل والترك بالنظر إلى القدرة .
And the Mu'tazilites have here objections: first, that what you have mentioned is argumentation against a self-evident truth, so it does not deserve an answer, because we know necessarily that we have power and choice, and that if we wish to do we do, and if we wish to omit we omit. Second, that it applies to the act of God, so He would be necessitating not choosing, because all that is necessary for the existence of the world, if it was present eternally, the world would be eternal and would proceed from God by way of necessity without ability to omit due to impossibility of delay from the complete cause; and if it was not present, the discourse transfers to the temporality of the necessary thing, and there is no infinite regress, but it ends in an eternal thing with which the effect is necessary, and the same problem returns. Third, that the choice of the free agent among equals is permissible, as in the two paths of the fugitive and the two cups of the two thirsty ones, because the will is an attribute whose function is to give preponderance and specification without needing a relative weight; the impossible is only the relative weighing without a relative weight. Fourth, that the relative weight that is not from the servant is the attachment of will and sincerity of motive, and the necessity of the act with it does not negate choice and the ability to do and omit with respect to power.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage enumerates four objections raised by the Mu'tazilites against the third proof. The first objection argues that the proof contradicts self-evident knowledge: we directly know we have power and choice to act or not. The second objection turns the argument against God: if God's act required a relative weight, it would make the world eternal and God necessitated, not free. The third objection asserts that choice does not require an external relative weight; the will itself is sufficient to specify among equals, as illustrated by a fugitive choosing a path or a thirsty person choosing between two cups of water. The fourth objection concedes that the relative weight comes from the will and motive, but this does not negate choice; the ability to do or omit remains relative to power, not to the actual occurrence. These objections are classic Mu'tazilite defenses of human freedom and critique of Ash'arite determinism.

موجب
مختار
قدم العالم
علة تامة

Checkpoint
What common outcome do both horns of the second objection share?
A) God acts without will.
B) The world is created in time.
C) God is a necessitating, not free, agent.
D) Human free will is preserved.

Key Takeaway
The second objection turns the Ash'arite argument against divine action, claiming it implies God is necessitated and the world eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe God can act without any cause or reason, even if that seems arbitrary? How does that affect your trust in His wisdom and justice?
?
If God were bound by necessity, as the objection suggests, would your prayers become mechanical requests to a system rather than pleas to a free Lord? How would that change your relationship with Him?
?
When you face dilemmas like the one in this lesson, do you feel your faith challenged or deepened? What would it take for you to accept that God's freedom surpasses your logical frameworks?

Third Objection: Will Itself Gives Preponderance
Third objection: will can specify among equals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third Mu'tazilite objection: that the will can specify one of two equals without an external preponderator, using examples like a fugitive choosing a path.

Core Idea
The will is its own weight.

The third objection cuts to the heart of the debate: does every choice need an external factor to tip the scales? The Mu'tazilites argue that the will itself, by its nature, can give preponderance to one of two equally possible alternatives. This lesson explores that claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its examples
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the examples: the fugitive with two paths, the two cups for two thirsty people.
These everyday examples illustrate that choice among equal alternatives is possible without an external factor.
2
Identify the key claim
The objection states that the will's function is to give preponderance and specification (ترجيح وتخصيص) without needing a relative weight (مرجح).
This directly challenges the Ash'arite assumption that an external factor is always needed.
3
Distinguish from the impossible
The objection clarifies that it is impossible for something to be preponderated without any preponderator, but the will itself is that preponderator.
The Mu'tazilites maintain that the will is an internal preponderator, so no external is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمعتزلة ههنا اعتراضات أحدها أن ما ذكرتم استدلال في مقابلة الضرورة فلا يستحق الجواب، وذلك لأنا نعلم بالضرورة أن لنا مكنة واختياراً، وأنا إن شئنا الفعل فعلنا وإن شئنا الترك تركنا . وثانيها : أنه جار في فعل الباري فيلزم أن يكون موجباً لا مختاراً، وذلك لأن جميع ما لابد منه في إيجاد العالم إن كان حاصلاً في الأزل، لزم قدم العالم وصدوره عن الباري بطريق الوجوب من غير تمكن من الترك لامتناع التخلف عن تمام العلة، وإن لم يكن حاصلاً ينتقل الكلام إلى حدوث الأمر الذي لا بد منه ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي إلى أمر أزلي يلزم معه المؤثر. ويعود المحذور. وثالثها : أن ترجيح المختار أحد المتساويين جائز كما في طريقي الهارب ، وقدحي العطّشنان لأن الإرادة صفة شأنها الترجيح والتخصيص من غير احتياج إلى مرجح، وإنما المحال الترجح بلا مرجح . ورابعها: أن المرجح الذي لا يكون من العبد هو تعلق الإرادة وخلوص الداعي، ووجوب الفعل معه لا ينافي الاختيار والتمكن من الفعل والترك بالنظر إلى القدرة .
And the Mu'tazilites have here objections: first, that what you have mentioned is argumentation against a self-evident truth, so it does not deserve an answer, because we know necessarily that we have power and choice, and that if we wish to do we do, and if we wish to omit we omit. Second, that it applies to the act of God, so He would be necessitating not choosing, because all that is necessary for the existence of the world, if it was present eternally, the world would be eternal and would proceed from God by way of necessity without ability to omit due to impossibility of delay from the complete cause; and if it was not present, the discourse transfers to the temporality of the necessary thing, and there is no infinite regress, but it ends in an eternal thing with which the effect is necessary, and the same problem returns. Third, that the choice of the free agent among equals is permissible, as in the two paths of the fugitive and the two cups of the two thirsty ones, because the will is an attribute whose function is to give preponderance and specification without needing a relative weight; the impossible is only the relative weighing without a relative weight. Fourth, that the relative weight that is not from the servant is the attachment of will and sincerity of motive, and the necessity of the act with it does not negate choice and the ability to do and omit with respect to power.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage enumerates four objections raised by the Mu'tazilites against the third proof. The first objection argues that the proof contradicts self-evident knowledge: we directly know we have power and choice to act or not. The second objection turns the argument against God: if God's act required a relative weight, it would make the world eternal and God necessitated, not free. The third objection asserts that choice does not require an external relative weight; the will itself is sufficient to specify among equals, as illustrated by a fugitive choosing a path or a thirsty person choosing between two cups of water. The fourth objection concedes that the relative weight comes from the will and motive, but this does not negate choice; the ability to do or omit remains relative to power, not to the actual occurrence. These objections are classic Mu'tazilite defenses of human freedom and critique of Ash'arite determinism.

Key Terms
ترجيح
إرادة
تخصيص
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What attribute of the will does the third objection emphasize?
A) It always needs an external cause.
B) It is passive.
C) It gives preponderance and specification by its nature.
D) It cannot choose among equals.

Key Takeaway
The third objection argues that the will itself is the preponderator, making external factors unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face equally valid choices, do you honestly admit that your will alone tips the balance, or do you invent external reasons to avoid responsibility?
?
If your will is sufficient to specify among equals, how can you ever claim that a sin was 'forced' upon you? What would change if you owned every choice as an act of your will?
?
The Mu'tazilites argue the will is an attribute that specifies by itself. How does this reframe your view of your daily habits—are you willfully choosing them, or drifting by inertia? What one choice today will you make with full awareness that your will is the preponderator?

Fourth Objection: Motive and Will as Relative Weight
Fourth objection: relative weight is internal; necessity compatible with choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth Mu'tazilite objection: that the relative weight (motive and will) comes from the servant, and the necessity of the act with it does not negate choice.

Core Idea
Necessity of the act does not deny choice.

The final objection concedes that there is a relative weight—the will and sincerely motive—but insists it comes from the human being, not from an external cause. Moreover, the resulting necessity of the act is compatible with free choice because the ability to do or omit remains with respect to power, not actual occurrence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice it acknowledges a relative weight but locates it in the human will and motive.
This is a moderate position that accepts the structure of the Ash'arite proof but reinterprets it.
2
Understand 'relative weight not from the servant'
The Ash'arites claimed the relative weight is external. The Mu'tazilites here say the relative weight is the servant's own will and sincere motive.
This preserves human agency.
3
Grasp the compatibility claim
The objection states that the act being necessary given the will does not negate choice, because the ability to do or omit is based on power, not on the actual outcome.
One can say 'I could have done otherwise' in terms of power, even if the will determines the specific action.

Objection: Why Does Saying 'Faith Is Created' Count as Disbelief?
An objection highlighting an apparent inconsistency in Ash'arite theology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the apparent contradiction between the Ash'arite view that faith is created by God and the legal ruling that deems saying 'faith is created' as disbelief.

Core Idea
The same phrase can be kufr or imān depending on intention and context.

This passage presents an objection directed at the Ash'arite position on the nature of faith. The objector highlights a paradox: the Ash'arites themselves affirm that faith is created by Allah, yet some legal rulings declare that whoever says 'faith is created' is a disbeliever. How can this be? The answer is not provided here, but the question itself forces us to think carefully about the difference between affirming a doctrine and misusing a phrase. By studying this objection, we sharpen our understanding of the relationship between theological statements and legal judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two parts: the Ash'arite view vs. the Mu'tazilite view, and the fatwa that declares saying 'faith is created' as kufr.
The text ends with an ellipsis; the answer is not present here.
2
Identify the paradox
The objector claims that the Ash'arites say faith is created by God, but some fatwas call that statement kufr. Is there a contradiction?
Consider that the fatwa might be directed at those who deny that faith is from God, not at the Ash'arite affirmation.
3
Reflect on intention in takfir
In Islamic jurisprudence, the same words can be kufr or not depending on the speaker's intention and context. Reflect on how this principle might resolve the paradox.
4
Formulate your own answer
Before looking for the author's answer, try to answer the objection yourself based on what you know of Ash'arite theology and legal maxims.
This prepares you to appreciate the full response when you encounter it later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فعندكم الإيمان مخلوق الله تعالى وعندهم مخلوق العبد، وقد ذكر في بعض الفتاوى أن من قال : الإيمان مخلوق كفر، فما وجهه . . . ؟
If it is said: According to you, faith is created by God Almighty, and according to them, it is created by the servant, and it has been mentioned in some fatwas that whoever says 'faith is created' is a disbeliever, so what is its explanation? ...
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents an objection raised against the Ash'arite position. The objector notes a paradox: the Ash'arites (the author's school) hold that faith is created by God, while the Mu'tazilites hold that it is created by the servant. Yet some fatwas declare that whoever says 'faith is created' is a disbeliever. The objector asks for an explanation of this ruling. The answer is not present in the given pages; the text cuts off with an ellipsis. The lesson thus consists only of the question, which challenges the consistency of the Ash'arite position with certain legal rulings.

Misconception & Correction

?

Abu al-Husayn's Self-Evidence Claim: A Tactical Retreat
Critique of Abu al-Husayn's self-evidence claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu al-Husayn's claim that human creation of actions is self-evident was a tactical move to hide his deviation from Mu'tazilism.

Core Idea
Self-evidence claimed not from truth, but from fear of exposing doctrinal retreat.

This passage critiques one of the most surprising moves in kalām: Abu al-Husayn al-Basrī, a leading Mu'tazilī, claimed that it is self-evident that humans create their own acts. The author finds this astonishing because it contradicts the very logic Abu al-Husayn himself had established. Let's delve into the passage to uncover the intellectual and psychological dynamics behind this claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Note the tone of astonishment from the author.
The author uses 'عجب' (amazement) to signal the gravity of the issue.
2
Identify the two premises from al-Razi
Al-Razi reports that Abu al-Husayn held two premises: (1) the act's dependence on a motive is self-evident, and (2) the act necessarily follows the motive. Trace how these premises lead to the denial of human creation.
These premises are the key to understanding the contradiction.
3
Understand the tactical claim
Notice that Abu al-Husayn, to avoid admitting he abandoned his school, claimed self-evidence for human creation. This is the core of the critique.
The claim is not based on evidence but on fear of his followers.
4
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Consider how this shows that even great scholars can make claims based on social pressure rather than rational necessity.
This is a reminder to separate doctrine from personal motives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعجب من أبي الحسين وهو في غاية الحذاقة، كيف اجترأ على هذه الدعوة وهي آية الوقاحة حيث نسب جميع سواه من العقلاء إلى السفسطة وإنكار الضرورة، أما السنية والجبرية فظاهر وأما القدرية فلأنهم حملوا الحكم بكون العبد مرجواً لأفعاله نظرياً لا ضرورياً وذكر الإمام في نهاية العقول أن أبا الحسين لما خالف أصحابه في قولهم القادر على الضدين لا يتوقف فعله لأحدهما دون الآخر على مرجح، وذهب إلى أن العلم بتوقف صدور الفعل على الداعي ضروري وأن حصول الفعل عقيب الداعي واجب لزمه من هاتين المقدمتين عدم كون العبد موجداً لفعله وفيه إبطال للأصول التي عليها مدار أمر الاعتزال، فخاف من تنبيه أصحابه، أنه رجع عن مذهبه، فليس الأمر عليهم، وادعى العلم الضروري بكون العبد موجداً لفعله
It is amazing of Abu al-Husayn, despite his extreme skill, how he dared to make this claim, which is a sign of impudence, as he attributed all other rational people to sophistry and denial of the self-evident. As for the Sunnis and Jabriyya, it is obvious; as for the Qadariyya, they considered the judgment that the servant is the agent of his actions to be theoretical, not self-evident. Imam al-Razi mentioned in Nihayat al-Uqul that when Abu al-Husayn disagreed with his companions in their statement that the capable of two opposites does not perform one without a preponderant, and he held that the knowledge of the act's dependence on the motive is self-evident and that the occurrence of the act subsequent to the motive is necessary, these two premises led to the conclusion that the servant is not the creator of his act, which invalidates the foundations of Mu'tazilism. So he feared that his companions would realize he had abandoned his school, so he made the matter difficult for them, and claimed self-evident knowledge that the servant is the creator of his act.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of Abu al-Husayn's claim. The author expresses astonishment at Abu al-Husayn's audacity in claiming self-evidence against all rational people. Then he quotes Imam al-Razi's explanation: Abu al-Husayn had held that the act's dependence on a motive is self-evident and that the act must occur after the motive, which leads to denying human creation, undermining Mu'tazilism. To cover his retreat, he claimed self-evident knowledge for human creation.

Key Terms
ضرورة
سفسطة
قدرة
فاعلية
داعي
مرجح
اعتزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Abu al-Husayn's claim is a genuine self-evident truth.
Correction: The author shows that the claim was a tactical move to hide his abandonment of Mu'tazilism, not a genuine self-evident judgment.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, why did Abu al-Husayn claim necessary knowledge that the servant creates his act?
A) He actually believed it was self-evident
B) He wanted to hide his abandonment of Mu'tazilism from his followers
C) He was refuting the Sunnis
D) He was forced by the Qadariyya

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Husayn's claim of self-evidence for human creation was a tactical retreat, not a rational necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you defended a position publicly despite knowing in your heart that the logic led elsewhere? What would it have cost you to admit you were wrong, and what did it cost you to maintain the facade?
?
Imagine you are Abu al-Husayn. You realize your own reasoning undermines everything your school stands for. Do you have the courage to announce your correction, or do you find a clever way to hide it? Which path have you taken when faced with a similar intellectual crisis?
?
Your beliefs about human responsibility shape how you live. If deep down you suspect your actions are not truly your own, would you rather know the truth or keep a comfortable illusion? What would change if you acted on the truth you already sense?

Critique of Mu'tazilite Notion of Obligation
Critique of Abu al-Husayn's view on obligation and refutation of a later Mu'tazilite claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite concept of obligation upon purity of motive collapses into determinism and undermines their entire school.

Core Idea
Obligation without real possibility of alternative is just determinism in disguise.

The Mu'tazilites prided themselves on a rational account of human obligation that preserved free will. But as this passage from Sharh al-Maqasid shows, their notion of 'obligation upon purity of motive' either implies a compulsion that negates free will or becomes an empty term. The author exposes this dilemma and shows that the only consistent conclusion is the Ash'arite position on divine determinism (jabr).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Read the passage and notice how the author presents two possibilities: either the pure motive necessitates the act (making refraining impossible) or it does not.
This is a classic 'either-or' argument.
2
Understand the implication of the first horn
If the pure motive necessitates the act, then the servant cannot refrain. This is determinism (jabr), which the Mu'tazilites reject but their own view leads to it.
The author equates this with the Ash'arite position.
3
Understand the implication of the second horn
If the pure motive does not necessitate the act, then calling it 'obligation' is meaningless and we have no way to know that the act will occur.
This makes the argument for human inability (e.g., Arabs' inability to produce the Qur'an) invalid.
4
See the logical structure
The passage uses inference from negation of consequent to negation of antecedent (modus tollens) to argue that knowledge of inability depends on the Mu'tazilite notion of obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من عليه فمرحبا بالوفاق، لكن يلزم منه فساد مذهب الاعتزال بالكلية لأنه لا فرق في العقل بين أن يأمر الله العبد بما يكون فعل الله تعالى وأن يأمره بفعل يجب حصوله عند فعل الله تعالى، ويمتنع حصوله عند عدمه، فإن المأمور على كلا التقديرين لا يكون متمكناً من الفعل والترك، ولا بين أن يعذب الله العبد على ما أوجده فيه، وأن يعذبه على ما يجب حصوله عند حصول ما أحدثه الله فيه ولا بين فاعل القبيح والظلم، وفاعل ما يوجب القبيح والظلم فمن اعترف بوجوب حصول الفعل عند حصول الإرادة الحادثة، انسد عليه باب الاعتزال فظهر أن أبا الحسين كان من المنكرين لمذهب الاعتزال في هذه المسألة، وأن مبالغته في دعوى الضرورة فيها كانت على سبيل التغبية والتلبيس، وزعم بعض المتأخرين من المعتزلة أن معنى الوجوب عند خلوص الداعي، أنا نعلم أن القادر بفعله مع إمكان الترك، كما نعلم أن الله يثيب الأنبياء والأولياء بالجنة ويعاقب الكفار بالنار مع إمكان تركهما نعلم أن العرب لو قدروا على مثل القرآن مع توافر الدواعي وانتفاء الموانع لأتوا بمثله، ولا وجوب الإتيان بمثله بمعنى الذي ذكرنا لما عرفنا عجزه لجواز أن يقدروا ولا يأتوا به، وفيه نظر لأنه إما أن يلزم مع خلوص الداعي صدور الفعل من القادر بحيث لا يصح منه الترك وإن كان ممكناً في نفسه وبالنظر إلى أصل قدرته وإرادته فيتم ما ذكره الإمام من وجوب الفعل لظهور أن تلك الداعية والإرادة الجازمة ليست بإرادة العبد، وهذا هو المعنى بالجبر، الذي يقول به أهل الحق، ويلزم أبا الحسين لا الجبر المغلق الذي يقول به المجبرة. وبطلانه ضروري وإما أن لا يلزم فلا معنى لتسميته بالوجوب، ولا طريق إلى العلم بالصدور، بل هو رجم بالغيب، لأن المفروض تساوي الأمرين، وإما العلم فرع اعتقاد الوجوب، ألا يرى أنه إذا قيل من أين عرفت عجز المتحدين؟ قيل لأنه خلصت دواعيهم فلو قدروا لأتوا به، وهذا معنى الوجوب لأنه استدلال بانتفاء اللازم على انتفاء الملزوم، ولهذا يستدل بنفي الفعل عند تحقق القدرة على نفي الداعية وجزم الإرادة.
From him, so welcome to agreement, but it entails the corruption of the Mu'tazilite school entirely, because there is no difference in reason between God commanding the servant with what is the act of God Almighty and commanding him with an act whose occurrence is necessary upon the occurrence of God's act and impossible upon its non-occurrence, for the commanded person in both cases is not able to act or refrain; nor between God tormenting the servant for what He originated in him and tormenting him for what must occur upon the occurrence of what God originated in him; nor between the doer of evil and injustice and the doer of what necessitates evil and injustice. Whoever acknowledges the necessity of the act's occurrence upon the occurrence of the originated will, the door of Mu'tazilism is closed to him. Thus it became clear that Abu al-Husayn was among the deniers of the Mu'tazilite school on this issue, and that his exaggeration in claiming necessity in it was a way of deception and obfuscation. And some later Mu'tazilites claimed that the meaning of obligation upon purity of motive is that we know that the capable one acts with his act despite the possibility of refraining, just as we know that God rewards prophets and saints with Paradise and punishes disbelievers with Hell despite the possibility of His refraining from both; we know that if the Arabs were capable of something like the Qur'an with motives present and obstacles absent, they would have produced something like it, and without obligation to produce its like in the sense we mentioned, we would not know their inability, because it is possible that they are capable but do not produce it. And there is consideration in it because either purity of motive necessitates the occurrence of the act from the capable such that refraining from him is not valid, although it is possible in itself and in view of his original power and will, then what the Imam mentioned of the necessity of the act is established, because it is clear that that motive and decisive will are not the servant's will, and this is the meaning of determinism (jabr) that the People of Truth affirm, and it applies to Abu al-Husayn, not the obscure determinism that the Jabriyya affirm. Its falsity is necessary. Or it does not necessitate, then there is no meaning to calling it obligation, nor way to knowledge of occurrence, but rather it is shooting in the dark, because the assumption is that the two matters are equal. And knowledge is a branch of belief in obligation. Do you not see that if it is said: 'From where did you know the inability of the challengers?' It is said: 'Because their motives were pure, so if they were capable, they would have produced it.' This is the meaning of obligation, because it is inference from the negation of the consequent to the negation of the antecedent. For this reason, one infers from the negation of the act when ability is realized to the negation of the motive and decisiveness of the will.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Mu'tazilite position on human obligation and agency. It begins by noting that if one accepts that the act necessarily follows God's creation of the will, then there is no difference between commanding an act that is directly God's act and commanding an act that necessarily occurs upon God's act; in both cases the servant lacks genuine ability to act or refrain. Similarly, there is no difference between God punishing for what He created and punishing for what must occur upon what He created, or between being the doer of evil and being the doer of what necessitates evil. The author then states that whoever admits the necessity of the act upon the originated will has closed the door to Mu'tazilism. It becomes clear that Abu al-Husayn (al-Basri) actually denied the Mu'tazilite school on this point, and his insistence on the self-evidence of obligation was deceptive. The passage then presents a later Mu'tazilite claim that obligation upon purity of motive means we know the agent can act despite possibility of refraining, analogous to God's rewarding and punishing despite possibility of refraining, or the Arabs' inability to produce the Qur'an despite capability. The author refutes this by dividing the matter: either the pure motive necessitates the act such that refraining is impossible, which leads to determinism (jabr) as held by the People of Truth (Ash'arites) and applies to Abu al-Husayn, not the crude determinism of the Jabriyya; or it does not necessitate, in which case calling it 'obligation' is meaningless and there is no way to know the occurrence of the act, thus it is mere conjecture. The passage concludes by explaining that knowledge of inability is based on belief in obligation, using the example of the Qur'anic challenge: one knows the Arabs' inability because their motives were pure and they did not produce it, which is inference from the negation of the consequent to the negation of the antecedent.

Key Terms
خلوص الداعي
الجبر
الإرادة الجازمة
مذهب الاعتزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazilites believe their notion of obligation (al-wujūb ʿinda khuṣūṣ al-dāʿī) preserves human free will and rational accountability without determinism.
Correction: The passage shows that this notion either collapses into determinism (jabr) or becomes an empty term, thus undermining the Mu'tazilite project.

Checkpoint
What does the passage argue about the Mu'tazilite notion of obligation (الوجوب عند خلوص الداعي)?
A) It successfully preserves human free will.
B) It collapses into determinism (jabr) because the pure motive necessitates the act.
C) It is the same as the Ash'arite doctrine of kasb.
D) It is based on divine decree alone.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite notion of obligation upon purity of motive either implies determinism or is an empty concept, thus refuting their school.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself disobeying Allah, do you ever subtly excuse yourself by thinking 'it was bound to happen' or 'Allah wrote it so'? How does this lesson force you to confront that your will is real and you are accountable for choosing sin?
?
This passage argues that if a pure motive truly determined your action, you would have no real choice. In your life, are there moments when you claim 'I couldn't help it' but deep down you know you could have chosen differently? Will you start taking full responsibility for those actions?
?
The Mu'tazilite notion is shown to be either determinism or meaningless. What does this mean for your own understanding of why you worship? Do you worship because you truly choose to, or because you feel compelled? How will you ensure your obedience is a conscious, willing act of submission rather than mere habit?

Refuting the Jabriyya Objection
The author presents and answers the Jabriyya's objection from Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Jabriyya's claim that the Quran becomes a proof for the disbeliever by distinguishing between apparent obstacles and hidden rational obstacles.

Core Idea
The Quran's verses refer to apparent obstacles, not hidden rational ones.

In this passage, the author presents a potent objection from the Jabriyya determinists: if God creates disbelief, then the Quran's condemnation of disbelief seems unfair. The author then provides a crisp answer that saves God's justice while acknowledging the verses. By distinguishing between two levels of obstacles, the author preserves the coherence of divine revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Ibn Abbad's quote
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note how the Jabriyya argue that God's actions make the Quran a proof for the disbeliever, and how Ibn Abbad dramatizes the contradiction.
Ibn Abbad was a Mu'tazili scholar, so his objection reflects a Qadariyya perspective.
2
Identify the core of the objection
The objection is that God's decree (creating disbelief) contradicts His command (ordering faith). This makes the Quran's condemnations seemingly unjust.
3
Understand the answer
The author answers by saying the obstacles mentioned in the Quran are apparent obstacles—those known to ignorant disbelievers—not the rational obstacles hidden from Qadariyya scholars. Thus the Quran remains a proof against them.
This answer preserves divine justice while acknowledging the verses.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters for the debate on free will and predestination. It shows that scriptural objections can be resolved by careful theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى مذهب المجبرة لهم أن يجادلوا ويقولوا إنك خلقت فينا الكفر، وعلمته ،وأردته، وأخبرت ،به وخلقت قدرة وداعية، يجب معهما الكفر، وكل هذه موانع من الإيمان، فيكون القرآن حجة للكافر، وقد أنزل ليكون حجة عليه، وإلى هذا أشار الصاحب بن عباد (۲) وكان غالياً في الرفض والاعتزال ساعياً في تربية أبي هاشم الجبائي ورفع قدره، وإعلاء ذكره حيث قال : كيف يأمر بالإيمان ولم يرده وينهى عن الكفر وأراده، ويعاقب على الباطل ويقدره، وكيف يصرف عن الإيمان ثم يقول أنى يصرفون (۳) ويخلق فيهم الإفك ثم يقول أنى يؤفكون ) (٤) وأنشأ فيهم الكفر ثم يقول كيف تكفرون (٥) وخلق فيهم لبس الحق بالباطل ثم قال لم تلبسون الحق بالباطل (٦) وصدهم عن السبيل ثم يقول لم تصدون عن سبيل الله ) (۷) وحال بينهم وبين الإيمان ثم يقول وماذا عليهم لو آمنوا) (۸) وأذهب عن الرشد ثم قال فأين تذهبون) (۱) وأضلهم عن الدين حتى أعرضوا ثم قال فما لهم عن التذكرة معرضين [؟] والجواب : أن المراد الموانع الظاهرة التي يعلمها جهال الكفرة، وهذه موانع عقلية خفيت على علماء القدرية.
And according to the doctrine of the Jabriyya, they can argue and say: You created disbelief in us, and knew it, and willed it, and informed of it, and created a power and an incitement that necessitate disbelief, and all these are obstacles to faith, so the Qur'an becomes a proof for the disbeliever, whereas it was revealed to be a proof against him. And to this al-Sahib ibn Abbad (2) pointed, who was extreme in Rafd and I'tizal, striving to nurture Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i and elevate his rank and raise his mention, saying: How can He command faith but not will it, and forbid disbelief but will it, and punish falsehood while predestining it? And how can He turn away from faith then say 'How are they turned away?' (3) and create falsehood in them then say 'How are they perverted?' (4) and originate disbelief in them then say 'How can you disbelieve?' (5) and create in them the mixing of truth with falsehood then say 'Why do you mix truth with falsehood?' (6) and bar them from the path then say 'Why do you bar from the path of God?' (7) and interpose between them and faith then say 'And what would it harm them if they believed?' (8) and remove guidance then say 'Where are you going?' (1) and lead them astray from the religion until they turn away then say 'What is the matter with them that they turn away from the reminder?' [?] And the answer: The intended obstacles are the apparent obstacles known to the ignorant disbelievers, and these are rational obstacles hidden from the scholars of the Qadariyya.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the school of the Jabriyya (compellers) and then refutes it. The author first states the objection: on the Jabriyya's determinist view, the disbeliever can argue that God created disbelief in him, willed it, and created a power and motive that necessitate disbelief. Since these are obstacles to faith, the Qur'an becomes a proof for the disbeliever, whereas its purpose is to be a proof against him. The author then quotes al-Sahib ibn Abbad, an extreme Rafidi and Mu'tazili, who raised a series of rhetorical questions highlighting the apparent contradiction in God's commands and decrees. The answer (al-jawab) distinguishes between two kinds of obstacles: the apparent obstacles that ignorant disbelievers perceive, and rational obstacles hidden even from the scholars of Qadariyya. The author asserts that the intended meaning of the verses is the apparent obstacles—so the disbeliever's argument fails because the verses refer to the disbelievers' own ignorance, not to God's decree. This answer preserves God's justice while acknowledging the verses.

Key Terms
المجبرة (al-Jabriyya)
الموانع العقلية (rational obstacles)
القدرية (Qadariyya)
الصاحب بن عباد (al-Sahib ibn Abbad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the answer denies the existence of rational obstacles altogether.
Correction: The answer actually says rational obstacles exist but are hidden from Qadariyya scholars, not that they do not exist.

Checkpoint
What is the author's answer to the Jabriyya's objection that the Quran is a proof for the disbeliever?
A) The Quran was abrogated
B) The obstacles are only apparent, not rational
C) The disbeliever's argument is based on rational obstacles hidden from Qadariyya scholars
D) The verses are metaphorical

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the Jabriyya by clarifying that the Quranic verses refer to apparent obstacles, thus preserving divine justice while acknowledging God's ultimate control.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever blamed God for your own sins, thinking that He created the obstacles to your obedience? When you are tempted to sin, do you recognize your own apparent choice to turn away, or do you rationalize by saying 'God willed it'? This lesson says the Quran holds you accountable for your manifest rejection—how will you respond to that accountability today?
?
When you read verses like 'How are they turned away?' do you see them as describing God's action or the disbeliever's action? If you have ever used God's decree as an excuse for not striving in faith, what does this lesson demand of you now? Will you accept the apparent obstacles you can remove (like laziness or doubt) rather than hiding behind hidden rational ones?
?
The author says the rational obstacles are hidden from even the scholars of Qadariyya. Do you trust that God's decree and your responsibility are not contradictory, even if you cannot fully understand the hidden obstacles? Are you willing to hold yourself accountable for your apparent actions, leaving the hidden reasons to God's wisdom? What is one area of your life where you can stop making excuses and take responsibility right now?

The Proof of Will and the Magian Challenge
The author dismisses the proof from will and recounts the Magian's challenge to Amr ibn Ubayd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school dismisses the proof from divine will and how the Magian story exposes the logical flaw in allowing events contrary to God's will.

Core Idea
Allowing evil to happen despite God's will leads to absurd dualism.

This passage tackles one of the arguments used by the Mu'tazila—the proof from will. The author dismisses it and then recounts a famous story between the Mu'tazili scholar Amr ibn Ubayd and a Magian, which dramatically illustrates the theological problem. Through this narrative, we see how permitting events contrary to God's will implies a rival power, undermining tawheed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation to understand the argument and the story.
Pay attention to the dialogue between the Magian and Amr.
2
Understand the dismissal of the proof
The author states that the proof from will is not relied upon because the Mu'tazila themselves allow events that are contrary to God's will.
This is a key theological point: if God's will can be opposed, then He is not omnipotent.
3
Follow the dialogue in the story
The Magian asks why he should submit to Islam. Amr says God wills his Islam but devils prevent it. The Magian retorts with a dualist statement.
Notice how the Magian uses Amr's own premise to justify his position.
4
Grasp the logical implication
The Magian's response points out that if devils can thwart God's will, then they are equal partners, leading to polytheism.
This exposes the absurdity of the Mu'tazili view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما دليل الإرادة فقد أورده الموافق في عدادهما فلا معول عليه عندهم، لتجويزهم وقوع خلاف مراداً الله تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً. ولهذا ألزم المجوسي عمرو بن عبید حين قال له: «لم لا تسلم فقال : لأن الله لم يرد إسلامي .فقال : إن الله يريد إسلامك لكن الشياطين لا يتركونك .فقال المجوسي : فأنا أكون مع الشريك الغالب».
And as for the proof of will, the opponent has presented it in their enumeration, so it is not relied upon according to them, because they allow the occurrence of something contrary to God's will — exalted is He above that greatly. And for this reason the Magian forced Amr ibn Ubayd when he said to him: 'Why do you not submit?' He said: 'Because God has not willed my Islam.' He said: 'God wills your Islam, but the devils do not leave you.' The Magian said: 'Then I am with the victorious partner.'
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage dismisses one of the arguments used by the Mu'tazila — the proof from divine will (dil al-irada). The author says that the proponent (presumably the Mu'tazili or the determinist) has included it in their enumeration, but it carries no weight in the Ash'ari view because the Mu'tazila themselves allow the possibility of something happening contrary to God's will. (The Mu'tazila deny that God wills evil but affirm that evil occurs; thus they admit that what occurs may not be what God wills.) To illustrate the absurdity of this position, the author recounts a story: a Magian (Zoroastrian) challenged the Mu'tazili scholar Amr ibn Ubayd. The Magian refused to convert, saying 'God has not willed my Islam.' Amr replied, 'God wills your Islam, but the devils do not leave you.' The Magian then retorted, 'Then I am with the victorious partner,' alluding to the dualist belief in two equal gods (Yazdan and Ahriman). This story shows that if God's will can be thwarted by devils, then God is not all-powerful, leading to dualism. Thus, the Mu'tazili position collapses into absurdity.

Key Terms
دليل الإرادة
المجوسي
عمرو بن عبيد
الشريك الغالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazili position that God wills good but evil happens is consistent with divine omnipotence.
Correction: The story shows that if evil occurs contrary to God's will, then something else (devils) overpowers God, making them partners in divinity, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
What was the Magian's response when Amr ibn Ubayd said God wills his Islam but devils prevent it?
A) He converted to Islam
B) He said 'Then I am with the victorious partner'
C) He argued that God is unable
D) He remained silent

Key Takeaway
The proof from will collapses because permitting events against God's will reduces Him to a powerless deity, as the Magian's claim to side with the victorious partner demonstrates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted God's power or wisdom when you see evil in your own life, subtly believing that some force can oppose His will? What would it take for you to fully trust that nothing happens outside His decree, even when you don't understand it?
?
In what areas of your daily decisions do you fear the creation more than the Creator—like fearing people's opinions or circumstances more than God's pleasure? How can you train your heart to side only with the all-powerful God rather than the 'victorious partner' of your fears?
?
The Magian chose the victorious partner because he saw God as weak. When you are disobedient, do you act as if Satan's power is greater than God's? How would your repentance change if you truly believed God's will is supreme over all, including your own failures?

The Condemnation of the Qadariyya
The author cites hadiths condemning the Qadariyya and explains their name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the authentic hadiths that condemn the Qadariyya and the reason they are called by that name.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya are cursed by seventy prophets for denying divine decree.

This passage opens with a strong condemnation of the Qadariyya sect, citing multiple prophetic hadiths. It explains how their name derives from their excessive denial of qadar (divine decree) and rejects a misleading etymology that would redirect the name to those who affirm a power of origination for humans.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadiths of condemnation
Start by reading the three hadiths cited: the curse upon seventy prophets, being the Magians of this ummah, and standing as enemies on the Day of Resurrection.
The hadiths are from the main text (matn).
2
Understand the reason for the name
The author explains that the Qadariyya are named for their extremism in denying qadar, and rejects the alternative etymology that would call them Qadariyya (with a damma) if they were affirming a creative power for humans.
The distinction in vowelization (Qadariyya vs Qadariyya) is crucial.
3
Reflect on the severity of the condemnation
The phrase 'cursed upon the tongues of seventy prophets' indicates the gravity of this deviation. Consider why denying qadar is such a serious matter in Islam.
This connects the lesson to the broader theological importance of qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذم القدرية والنصوص الدالة عليه
قال (ثم لا خلاف في ذم القدرية حتى قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لعن القدرية على لسان سبعين نبياً وسموا بذلك لافراطهم في نفيه وما يقولون من أن المثبت له أولى بالانتساب إليه مردود بقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة وقوله إذا قامت القيامة نادى مناد في أهل الجمع أين خصماء الله، فيقوم القدرية وبأن من يضيف القدر إلى نفسه أولى بهذا الام ممن يضيفه إلى ربه) .
سم
قد ورد في صحاح الأحاديث لعنت القدرية على لسان سبعين نبياً والمراد بهم القائلون بنفي كون الخير والشر كله بتقدير الله تعالى ومشيئته، سموا بذلك لمبالغتهم في نفيه، وكثرة مدافعتهم إياه، وقيل لإثباتهم للعبد قدرة الإيجاد، وليس بشيء لأن المناسب حينئذ القدري بضم القاف، وقالت المعتزلة القدرية هم القائلون بأن الخير والشر كله من الله وبتقديره ومشيئته . لأن الشائع نسبة الشخص إلى ما يثبته، ويقول به كالجبرية والحنفية والشافعية، لا إلى ما ينفيه. ورد بأنه صح عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قوله
The condemnation of the Qadariyya and the textual evidence for it. He said: (Then there is no disagreement on condemning the Qadariyya, so much so that the Prophet ﷺ said: 'The curse of the Qadariyya [is] upon the tongues of seventy prophets.' And they are called that because of their excessiveness in denying it. And their saying that the one who affirms it is more entitled to be attributed to it is refuted by his saying ﷺ: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this ummah,' and his saying: 'When the Day of Resurrection comes, a caller will call out among the gathered people: Where are the enemies of Allah? Then the Qadariyya will stand up,' and because the one who attributes qadar to himself is more deserving of this [appellation] than the one who attributes it to his Lord.) It has been narrated in authentic hadiths: 'Cursed are the Qadariyya upon the tongues of seventy prophets.' What is meant by them are those who deny that all good and evil are by Allah's decree and will. They are called that because of their exaggeration in denying it and their frequent opposition to it. And it is said [they are called that] because they affirm for the servant a power of origination, but it is nothing, because the appropriate term then would be 'Qadariyya' with a damma on the qaf. The Mu'tazila said: 'The Qadariyya are those who say that all good and evil are from Allah, by His decree and will,' because a person is usually attributed to what he affirms and believes in, like the Jabriyya, the Hanafiyya, and the Shafi'iyya, not to what he denies. This is refuted by the fact that it is authentically reported from the Prophet ﷺ that he said...
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage addresses the condemnation of the Qadariyya (those who deny divine decree). It begins with a heading: 'The condemnation of the Qadariyya and the textual evidence for it.' Then a quote (likely from the matn) states: 'Then there is no disagreement on condemning the Qadariyya,' and it provides several hadiths. The first hadith says: 'The curse of the Qadariyya is upon the tongues of seventy prophets.' The second hadith calls them 'the Magians of this ummah.' The third hadith describes them standing up as enemies of Allah on the Day of Resurrection. The quote also rebuts the claim of some that those who affirm qadar are more deserving of the name. After a brief marker ('سم'), the commentary explains that the authentic hadiths curse the Qadariyya, meaning those who deny that all good and evil are by Allah's decree. It rejects an alternative etymology that they are named for affirming a power of creation for the servant, because the correct term would then be 'Qadariyya' (with a damma on the qaf) rather than 'Qadariyya' (with a fatha). Then it reports the Mu'tazila's claim that the Qadariyya are actually those who affirm that all good and evil are from Allah, because a person is usually named after what he affirms (like Jabriyya, Hanafiyya, Shafi'iyya) not what he denies. However, the passage concludes by refuting this: 'It is refuted by the fact that it is authentically reported from the Prophet ﷺ that he said...' but the quote is cut off, implying the full hadith would clearly show that the Prophet considered the deniers as Qadariyya. Thus, the Mu'tazila's redefinition is overturned by the very texts used by the author.

الذم (al-dhamm)
نفي القدر (nafy al-qadar)
مجوس هذه الأمة (Majūs hādhihi al-ummah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think the Qadariyya are those who affirm qadar rather than deny it.
Correction: The passage shows that the Qadariyya are named for their excessive denial of qadar, and the Prophet's hadiths condemn them for that denial. An alternative etymology is rejected because it would change the vocalization.

Checkpoint

C) They are the chosen people
D) They are the scholars

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya are severely condemned in authentic hadiths for denying divine decree, and their name reflects their extremism in denial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson identifies the Qadariyya as enemies of Allah on the Day of Resurrection. Do you take the issue of qadar seriously enough to ensure your own belief is sound, or do you treat it as a minor theological point? What specific step will you take today to strengthen your conviction in Allah's complete control?
?
The name 'Qadariyya' comes from extremism in denial. Is there any area of your life where you resist submitting to Allah's decree—perhaps in your plans, your health, or your relationships? What would true acceptance of qadar look like for you right now?

The Mu'tazila's Redefinition Refuted
The author rebuts the Mu'tazila's definition of Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's claim that the Qadariyya are those who affirm qadar, and how the author refutes it by appealing to the prophetic hadiths.

Core Idea
The Prophet's condemnation clearly targets the deniers of qadar, not the affirmers.

In this passage, the author reports the Mu'tazila's alternative definition of the Qadariyya: that they are those who affirm that all good and evil are from Allah. The Mu'tazila argue that people are named after what they affirm, not what they deny. The author refutes this by pointing to the very hadiths that curse the deniers, showing that the Prophet considered the deniers as Qadariyya.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's claim
Identify their definition: 'The Qadariyya are those who say that all good and evil are from Allah, by His decree and will.'
This is a direct reversal of the common understanding.
2
Understand their reasoning
The Mu'tazila argue that a person is attributed to what he affirms, not to what he denies, giving examples like Jabriyya, Hanafiyya, Shafi'iyya.
They think that because the affirmers of qadar are called Qadariyya by this logic.
3
See the refutation
The author refutes by saying it is authentically reported from the Prophet that he said... (the hadiths that condemn the deniers). The implication is that the Prophet's own usage defines the term, not the Mu'tazila's linguistic reasoning.
The passage is cut off, but the point is clear: the hadiths show the Qadariyya are the deniers.

Qadariyya as the Magians of This Nation
The author cites two prophetic hadiths equating Qadariyya with Magians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Prophet ﷺ equated the Qadariyya with the Magians and how both groups misattribute qadar.

Core Idea
To misuse qadar to justify sin is to follow the Magians' error.

This lesson examines two hadiths that the author cites to show the severe condemnation of the Qadariyya. The first hadith describes them as Allah's antagonists at the resurrection, while the second warns that some Muslims will use qadar as an excuse for grave sins—just as the Magians did. Together, these traditions reveal the danger of misattributing qadar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the author presents two distinct hadiths with his own commentary.
The passage is dense; take your time to understand each part.
2
Identify the two hadiths
Find the first hadith about the resurrection and the second about the Persian man. What is the key message of each?
3
Reflect on the link to Magians
Consider why the Prophet ﷺ compared these people to Magians. How does misattributing qadar lead to sin?
The author's commentary explains the similarity.

القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة» وقوله إذا قامت القيامة نادى مناد في أهل الجمع أين خصماء الله ..؟ فيقوم القدرية ولا خفاء في أن المجوس هم الذين ينسبون الخير إلى الله ، والشر إلى الشيطان ويسمونهما ، يزدان، وإهرمن، وأن لا من يفوض الأمور كلها إلى الله ويعترض لبعضها فينسبه إلى نفسه يكون هو المخاصم الله تعالى وأيضاً من يضيف القدر إلى نفسه ويدعي كونه الفاعل والمقدر أولى باسم القدري ممن يضيفه إلى ربه. فإن قيل روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال لرجل قدم عليه من فارس أخبرني بأعجب شيء رأيت» فقال رأيت أقواماً ينكحون أمهاتهم وبناتهم وأخواتهم. فإن قيل لهم لم تفعلون ذلك. قالوا قضاء الله علينا وقدره . فقال عليه السلام) سيكون في آخر أمتي أقوام يقولون مثل مقالتهم، أولئك مجوس أمتي»
The Qadariyya are the Magians of this nation. And His saying: When the resurrection occurs, a caller will call out among the gathered people: 'Where are the antagonists of Allah?' Then the Qadariyya will stand up. And it is no secret that the Magians are those who attribute good to Allah and evil to Satan and name them Yazdan and Ahriman, and that whoever does not delegate all matters to Allah and objects to some of them, attributing them to himself, is the one who disputes with Allah. Also, whoever attributes qadar (قدر) to himself and claims to be the doer and determiner is more deserving of the name Qadari (قدري) than one who attributes it to his Lord. For it is said: It is narrated from the Prophet ﷺ that he said to a man who came to him from Persia: 'Tell me of the most amazing thing you have seen.' He said: 'I saw people marrying their mothers, daughters, and sisters. When asked why they did that, they said: "It is the decree (قضاء) of Allah upon us and His predestination (قدر)."' He ﷺ said: 'There will be in the later times of my nation people who say similar to their saying; those are the Magians of my nation.'
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents two prophetic traditions as evidence that the Qadariyya are likened to the Magians. The first hadith speaks of the resurrection where a caller will summon the antagonists of Allah, and the Qadariyya will stand up. The author then explains the similarity: Magians attribute good to Allah and evil to Satan, while Qadariyya dispute with Allah by not delegating all matters to Him. Moreover, the true Qadari is one who ascribes qadar to himself rather than to Allah. The second hadith relates an encounter with a Persian man who witnessed incest justified by divine decree. The Prophet prophesies that some of his nation will use similar reasoning, and those are the Magians of his nation.

Qada and Qadar as Allah's Command and Judgment
Ali defines qada and qadar as Allah's command and judgment, citing the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of qada and qadar as given by Ali ibn Abi Talib, rooted in the Quranic concept of divine command and judgment.

Core Idea
Qada and qadar are Allah's command and judgment, not blind fate.

This lesson presents the foundational definition of divine decree and predestination as taught by Ali ibn Abi Talib. Through his response to a question, we learn that qada and qadar refer to Allah's command and judgment, and this is supported by the Quranic verse about worshiping only Him. This sets the stage for understanding the Islamic view that divine decree is consistent with human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the flow of the conversation.
Notice that the man asks specifically about the qada and qadar 'by which we only marched'.
2
Identify the definition given by Ali
Ali responds with a clear definition: qada and qadar are 'the command of Allah and His judgment.' This is a concise theological statement.
3
Connect to the Quranic verse
Ali then recites the verse 'And your Lord has decreed that you worship none but Him.' The verb 'qada' here means 'commanded,' reinforcing the definition.
The verse shows that qada is an act of commanding, not a blind fate.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider that defining qada/qadar as command and judgment means they are rooted in divine wisdom and purpose, not arbitrary.
This definition is the basis for later discussions on divine justice and human freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال الشيخ وما القضاء والقدر اللذان ما سرنا إلا بهما ؟ قال : هو الأمر من الله والحكم ثم تلا قوله تعالى وقضى ربك ألا تعبدوا إلا إياه وعن الحسن بعث الله تعالى محمداً إلى العرب وهم قدرية يحملون ذنوبهم على الله وتصديقه قوله تعالى و إذا فعلوا فاحشة قالوا وجدنا عليها أباءنا والله أمرنا بها قلنا ما ذكر لا يدل إلا على أن القول بأن فعل العبد إذا كان بقضاء الله تعالى وقدره وخلقه وإرادته يجوز للعبد الإقدام عليه ويبطل اختياره فيه، واستحقاقه للثواب والعقاب والمدح والذم عليه قول المجوس فلينظر أن هذا قول المعتزلة أم المجبرة ولكن من لم يجعل الله له نوراً فما له من نور.
The man said: 'And what are the decree (قضاء) and predestination (قدر) by which we only marched?' He said: 'It is the command of Allah and His judgment.' Then he recited the saying of Allah: 'And your Lord has decreed (قضى) that you worship none but Him.' And from al-Hasan: Allah sent Muhammad to the Arabs while they were Qadariyya (قدرية), placing their sins on Allah, and the confirmation of it is His saying: 'And when they commit an immorality, they say: We found our fathers upon it, and Allah has commanded us to do it.' We say: What has been mentioned only indicates that the statement that the action of the servant, if it is by the decree of Allah, His predestination, creation, and will, it is permissible for the servant to proceed with it and it nullifies his choice in it, and his deserving reward and punishment, praise and blame for it, is the saying of the Magians. Then look: is this the saying of the Mu'tazila (المعتزلة) or the Jabriyya (المجبرة)? But whoever Allah has not given light, there is no light for him.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage continues the story: the man asks for the definition of qada and qadar, and Ali answers that they are the command and judgment of Allah, citing the verse about worshiping only Him. The author then brings a quote from al-Hasan al-Basri that the pre-Islamic Arabs were Qadariyya, as they attributed their sins to Allah, supported by a Quranic verse. Then the author gives his own conclusion: the narrations prove that the deterministic view (that actions being by divine decree nullify human choice and accountability) is the view of the Magians. He poses a rhetorical question asking whether this is the view of the Mu'tazila (who deny predestination) or the Jabriyya (who affirm compulsion), implying that such a view is actually that of the Jabriyya. The passage ends with a Quranic metaphor about light.

Key Terms
القضاء
القدر
الأمر
الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Blind fate and destiny
B) Allah's command and judgment
C) Human actions and choices
D) Natural laws

Key Takeaway
Qada and qadar are defined as Allah's command and judgment, not as a random or oppressive fate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficulty, do you see it as a random event or as a decree from Allah that carries His command and wisdom? What would change if you truly believed it is His judgment?
?
The verse 'Your Lord has decreed that you worship none but Him' is used to define qada. How does your daily life reflect that decree? Are there people or things you obey more than Allah?
?
Ali defined qada and qadar as Allah's command and judgment. How does this understanding affect your attitude toward what happens to you? Do you submit to His judgment with contentment, or do you resist, as if it is unfair?

Refuting Determinism: Actions by Divine Decree Do Not Negate Human Choice
The author refutes determinism by equating the view that divine decree nullifies choice with Magian doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that claiming divine decree nullifies human accountability is a Magian view, and how this refutes deterministic theology.

Core Idea
Determinism, which denies human choice, is the view of the Magians, not true Islam.

In this passage, the author builds on the earlier definition to refute a common misunderstanding: that if all actions are by God's decree, then humans have no choice and are not accountable. He traces this idea back to the pre-Islamic Arabs and ultimately to the Magians (Zoroastrians), and challenges the reader to consider which sect—Mu'tazila or Jabriyya—actually holds this view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the historical reference to the Arabs and the Quranic verse.
The passage starts with 'And from al-Hasan...'
2
Understand the deductive conclusion
The author states that the logical consequence of the deterministic view—that it permits sin and nullifies choice—is the saying of the Magians.
This labels determinism as a non-Islamic, Magian belief.
3
Analyze the rhetorical question
The author asks: 'Is this the saying of the Mu'tazila or the Jabriyya?' This implies that the deterministic view belongs to the Jabriyya.
The Mu'tazila affirm free will, while the Jabriyya deny it.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this refutes determinism: true Islam teaches that divine decree and human choice coexist.
The final verse 'But whoever Allah has not given light...' warns against such misguided views.
