فقال الشيخ وما القضاء والقدر اللذان ما سرنا إلا بهما ؟ قال : هو الأمر من الله والحكم ثم تلا قوله تعالى وقضى ربك ألا تعبدوا إلا إياه وعن الحسن بعث الله تعالى محمداً إلى العرب وهم قدرية يحملون ذنوبهم على الله وتصديقه قوله تعالى و إذا فعلوا فاحشة قالوا وجدنا عليها أباءنا والله أمرنا بها قلنا ما ذكر لا يدل إلا على أن القول بأن فعل العبد إذا كان بقضاء الله تعالى وقدره وخلقه وإرادته يجوز للعبد الإقدام عليه ويبطل اختياره فيه، واستحقاقه للثواب والعقاب والمدح والذم عليه قول المجوس فلينظر أن هذا قول المعتزلة أم المجبرة ولكن من لم يجعل الله له نوراً فما له من نور.
The man said: 'And what are the decree (قضاء) and predestination (قدر) by which we only marched?' He said: 'It is the command of Allah and His judgment.' Then he recited the saying of Allah: 'And your Lord has decreed (قضى) that you worship none but Him.' And from al-Hasan: Allah sent Muhammad to the Arabs while they were Qadariyya (قدرية), placing their sins on Allah, and the confirmation of it is His saying: 'And when they commit an immorality, they say: We found our fathers upon it, and Allah has commanded us to do it.' We say: What has been mentioned only indicates that the statement that the action of the servant, if it is by the decree of Allah, His predestination, creation, and will, it is permissible for the servant to proceed with it and it nullifies his choice in it, and his deserving reward and punishment, praise and blame for it, is the saying of the Magians. Then look: is this the saying of the Mu'tazila (المعتزلة) or the Jabriyya (المجبرة)? But whoever Allah has not given light, there is no light for him.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage continues the story: the man asks for the definition of qada and qadar, and Ali answers that they are the command and judgment of Allah, citing the verse about worshiping only Him. The author then brings a quote from al-Hasan al-Basri that the pre-Islamic Arabs were Qadariyya, as they attributed their sins to Allah, supported by a Quranic verse. Then the author gives his own conclusion: the narrations prove that the deterministic view (that actions being by divine decree nullify human choice and accountability) is the view of the Magians. He poses a rhetorical question asking whether this is the view of the Mu'tazila (who deny predestination) or the Jabriyya (who affirm compulsion), implying that such a view is actually that of the Jabriyya. The passage ends with a Quranic metaphor about light.

Key Terms
القدرية
المجوس
المعتزلة
المجبرة
الاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Determinism (jabr) is a valid Islamic doctrine that denies human choice.
Correction: The author shows that the view that divine decree removes human accountability is the saying of the Magians, not from Islamic sources.

Checkpoint
According to the author, whose view is it that actions being by divine decree nullifies human choice and reward?
A) The Mu'tazila
B) The Qadariyya
C) The Magians
D) The Jabriyya

Key Takeaway
The determinist view that divine decree removes human choice is not Islamic but Magian, and it is the position of the Jabriyya, not the Mu'tazila.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall into sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'It was written for me' or 'Allah decreed it,' as a way to avoid true repentance? How does this passage expose that evasion as closer to Magian doctrine than to Islam? What will you do the next time that thought arises?
?
The author ends with a stark warning: 'Whoever Allah has not given light, there is no light for him.' Are you actively seeking that light through knowledge and reflection, or are you passively assuming your fate? What one change will you make today to pursue guidance rather than blame your circumstances?
?
This lesson forces you to choose: will you live as someone accountable for your actions, or will you hide behind divine decree? In which area of your life are you most tempted to surrender responsibility? Write down a specific decision you will own fully this week, without attributing it to fate.

The Mu'tazila Argument for Generated Acts
Mu'tazila argue generated acts are human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's reasoning for attributing generated acts to human agents based on intention and moral responsibility.

Core Idea
Generated acts are human because they follow intention and invite praise or blame.

The Mu'tazila argue that generated acts, like writing or killing, are clearly human because they align with the agent's intention and are subject to moral judgment. This passage presents their key evidence, contrasting with direct acts, and shows why they consider such acts attributable to the slave.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila argument
Read the Arabic passage from the start to 'والجواب : مثل ما مرَّ' and its translation. Notice how the argument is built on two main criteria: intention and moral praise/blame.
The argument also includes examples like writing and craftsmanship.
2
Identify the two criteria
The text says generated acts occur 'on accordance with intention and motive' and are subject to 'praise and blame, command and prohibition'. These are the two pillars of the argument.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the examples: writing, craftsmanship, composing speech, pushing, pulling, killing, war. Why are these called 'generated acts'? They are indirect but clearly related to human agency.
Direct acts are like hand movements; generated acts are their intended consequences.
4
Contrast with direct acts
The passage states that praise and blame are more apparent for generated acts than for direct acts. Why might that be? Generated acts have visible, socially meaningful outcomes.
This contrast strengthens the Mu'tazila case.
5
Note the brief response
The answer given is 'similar to what has passed'. This refers to an earlier discussion. It is not elaborated here, but note that the author does not accept the argument outright.
The response is a placeholder, not a refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسكوا في كون المتولد فعلاً للعبد سواء تولد من فعله المباشر أو فعله المتولد كحركة الآلة، وحركة المتحرك بالآلة بمثل ما ذكروا في مسألة خلق الأعمال من وقوعه على وفق القصد والداعية ومن حسن المدح والذم والأمر والنهي، بل استحسان المدح والذم على الأفعال المتولدة كالكتابة والصناعة (۱) وإنشاء الكلام، والدفع والجذب والقتل والحرب، أظهر عند العقلاء من المدح والذم على المباشرات لأنها لا تظهر ظهور المتولدات، وكذا الوقوع بحسب الدواعي أظهر فيها، لأن أكثر الدواعي إنما يكون إلى المتولدات . والجواب : مثل ما مرَّ وذهب النظام من المعتزلة إلى أنه لا فعل للعبد إلا ما يوجد في محل قدرته والباقي بطبع المحل . وقال معمر لا فعل للعبد إلا الإرادة وما يحدث بعدها : إنما هو بطبع المحل وقيل لا فعل للعبد إلا الفكر
They adhered [to the view] that the generated (al-mutawallad) is an act of the slave, whether it is generated from his direct act or from his generated act, like the movement of a tool and the movement of the mover by the tool, by similar [arguments] to what they mentioned in the issue of the creation of acts: its occurrence in accordance with intention and motive, and the goodness of praise and blame, command and prohibition. Rather, the approval of praise and blame for generated acts like writing, craftsmanship, composing speech, pushing, pulling, killing, and war is more apparent to rational people than praise and blame for direct acts (al-mubāsharāt), because the latter do not appear as generated acts do. Similarly, occurrence according to motives is more apparent in them, because most motives are directed toward generated acts. The answer is: similar to what has passed. And al-Naẓẓām from the Muʿtazila went to [the view] that there is no act for the slave except what occurs in the locus of his power, and the rest is by the nature of the locus. And Muʿammar said: there is no act for the slave except the will, and what occurs after it is only by the nature of the locus. And it was said: there is no act for the slave except thought.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents the Muʿtazila's initial argument that generated acts (al-mutawallad) are attributable to the human slave. They base this on empirical observations: generated acts occur in accordance with the agent's intention (qaṣd) and motive (dāʿiya), and they are subject to praise and blame, command and prohibition in a way that direct acts (al-mubāsharāt) are not. For example, acts like writing, craftsmanship, and causing death are clearly praised or blamed, while direct bodily movements are less noticeably praised. The response given is very brief: 'like what has passed,' referring to an earlier discussion (presumably in the context of 'creation of acts'). Then the passage reports three distinct Muʿtazilī positions: al-Naẓẓām holds that only acts occurring in the locus of the slave's power are his acts; Muʿammar restricts the slave's act to will alone; and another view limits it to thought. These are not refuted here but merely stated as alternative views within the school.

Key Terms
المتولد
المباشر
الداعية
القصد
المدح والذم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That generated acts are not truly human acts but merely automatic consequences.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue that generated acts are human because they follow intention and are subject to moral judgment, making them attributable to the agent.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila argument presented, what two main criteria indicate that generated acts are human acts?
A) Occurrence in the locus of power and being created by Allah
B) Occurrence in accordance with intention and being subject to praise/blame
C) Being direct and immediate
D) Being caused by nature and necessity

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila consider generated acts as human because they are intentionally driven and morally accountable, contrasting with direct acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you excused yourself from responsibility by saying 'I didn't directly do it' or 'it was an indirect consequence.' After reading this lesson, how do you now view that excuse? What generated act are you willing to take full ownership of starting today?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that praise and blame are more apparent for generated acts like writing, craftsmanship, or war. Which generated act in your own life—one that ripples out beyond your direct control—do you most fear being called to account for? Why does that fear reveal something about your heart?
?
If you truly believed that every indirect effect of your actions—every word that echoes, every email that spirals—is intentional and morally charged, what would you change about how you live tomorrow morning? Be specific: what one generated act will you consciously intend or avoid?

Internal Mu'tazili Disagreements on Human Acts
Three internal Mu'tazili views on human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three internal Mu'tazili views on which acts are truly attributable to humans.

Core Idea
Not all Mu'tazila agree: some limit human acts to direct power, will, or thought.

After presenting the standard Mu'tazila argument for generated acts, the text notes that leading figures within the school held divergent views. Al-Nazzam, Mu'ammar, and an unnamed source each restrict human agency to a minimal category, showing that the school was not monolithic on this issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the three views
Read from 'وذهب النظام' to the end. Identify each position: al-Nazzam (acts in locus of power), Mu'ammar (will only), and the third view (thought only).
2
Understand al-Nazzam's view
Al-Nazzam says only acts that occur in the locus of the slave's power are his acts; everything else is by the nature of the locus. This is a very restrictive view.
This limits human action to direct physical movements.
3
Understand Mu'ammar's view
Mu'ammar limits the slave's act to the will (irāda) alone; everything after that is by the nature of the locus. So even bodily movements are not truly human.
This is even more restrictive.
4
Understand the third view
Another view restricts the slave's act to thought (fikr) alone, going even further.
These views show a spectrum of opinions within Mu'tazila.
5
Compare and contrast
Notice how each view reduces the scope of human agency. Ask: why would these scholars adopt such restrictive positions? They likely want to preserve Divine Omnipotence.
These are not refuted here, just reported.

Source Text

Objection: Generated Acts Must Align with Intentions
Objection and answer on alignment of generated acts with intentions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili objection that generated acts must align with intentions if they are human acts, and the Ash'ari response that obstacles can prevent alignment.

Core Idea
Aligning with intention requires both cause and absence of obstacles.

The Mu'tazila argued that if generated acts were truly our own, they would always occur according to our intentions, just like direct acts. But this seems contradicted by experience. In this passage, the author presents their objection and then answers it by introducing the crucial concept of obstacles (mawāniʿ). Let's follow the argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the two arguments the Mu'tazila present.
The first argument uses craftsmen undoing their work as evidence.
2
Identify the two sub-arguments
The objection contains two distinct claims: (1) generated acts would always match intentions if they were ours, but they often don't; (2) if they were ours, we could refrain after the cause, but we can't (like an arrow).
3
Read the answer (al-jawāb)
The answer addresses each objection by invoking obstacles (mawāniʿ). Note how error in causes is a type of obstacle, and the complete cause (al-sabab al-tāmm) can remove the ability to refrain.
The answer does not deny that generated acts are our acts; it explains the apparent contradiction.
4
Understand the key concept: obstacles
Obstacles prevent alignment or ability to refrain. They are external factors that do not negate the act being the agent's own.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
This discussion shows how Ash'ari theology reconciles human responsibility with the fact that we cannot always control outcomes. Generated acts remain ours despite obstacles.
This links to the larger question of qadar (divine decree) and human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا لو كان المتولد فعلنا لم يقع إلا بحسب دواعينا كالمباشرة (۲) واللازم باطل، لأن كثيراً من أرباب الصناعات ينقضون أعمالهم لعدم موافقتها دواعيهم وأغراضهم. وأيضاً لو كان فعلنا لصح منا أن لا نفعله بعد وجود السبب لأن شأن القادر صحة أن يفعل وأن لا يفعل، واللازم ظاهر البطلان كما في السهم المرسل من القوس . والجواب : أن عدم الموافقة للفرض كمانع مثل الخطأ في تهيئة الأسباب، وكذا عدم التمكن من ترك الفعل لمانع مثل إحداث السبب التام لا ينافي كونه فعل الفاعل فإن موافقة الفرض وتمكن القادر من الترك والفعل إما يكونان عند وجود الأسباب وانتفاء الموانع
They said: If the generated were our act, it would only occur according to our motives like the direct act, but the consequent is false, because many craftsmen undo their works due to their not matching their motives and aims. Also, if it were our act, it would be valid for us not to do it after the cause exists, because the condition of the capable (al-qādir) is the validity to do and not to do, but the consequent is manifestly false, as in the arrow released from the bow. The answer is: that non-alignment with the intention is like an obstacle, such as error in preparing the causes; likewise, the inability to leave the act due to an obstacle, like causing the complete cause, does not negate its being an act of the agent, for alignment with intention and the ability of the capable to leave or do occur only when causes exist and obstacles are absent.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage contains a specific objection from the Muʿtazila against the view that generated acts are human acts. They argue: if generated acts were our own acts, they would always occur according to our motives (dawāʿīnā), just like direct acts. But this is false because craftsmen often undo their own work when it doesn't match their intentions. Moreover, if it were our act, we should be able to refrain from it even after the cause exists (since a capable agent can do or not do), but this is clearly false, e.g., an arrow once released from a bow cannot be undone. The answer (al-jawāb) refutes this by introducing the concept of obstacles (mawāniʿ). Non-alignment with intention is due to an obstacle like error in preparing causes, and the inability to leave the act is due to another obstacle like the complete cause having been set in motion. These do not negate the act being the agent's own, because alignment and ability to leave occur only when causes are present and obstacles are absent.

Key Terms
المتولد (al-mutawallad)
المباشرة (al-mubāshara)
الدواعي (al-dawāʿī)
المانع (al-māniʿ)
السبب التام (al-sabab al-tāmm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a generated act does not match my intention, it cannot be my act.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that obstacles (like error or a complete cause already set) can prevent alignment or the ability to refrain, yet the act remains attributable to the agent.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the Mu'tazila in this objection?
A) Generated acts are caused by Allah
B) Generated acts always match intentions if they are our acts
C) Generated acts are not subject to praise/blame
D) Generated acts are only possible through divine power

Key Takeaway
Generated acts are our acts despite occasional mismatch because obstacles (like error or complete cause) can prevent alignment or ability to refrain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Recall a time when you acted with good intention but the outcome was wrong—perhaps you gave advice that backfired or tried to help and made things worse. How does this lesson's explanation of obstacles (like error in causes) challenge you to reconsider your responsibility for that outcome? Are you still accountable for what was generated from your act? What would it mean to truly own that result before Allah?
?
Think of a habit or sin you keep falling into even though you resolve not to. The arrow once released cannot be taken back—once you set the cause in motion, the consequence follows. In what areas of your life have you surrendered to the feeling that 'the arrow is already shot' and you cannot change? How does the concept of the complete cause remind you that you are still the agent of that act, and what would repentance look like if you fully accepted that responsibility?
?
This lesson teaches that alignment with intention and ability to refrain require both causes and absence of obstacles. When you find yourself making excuses for your failures—'I didn't mean for it to happen' or 'I couldn't stop it'—are you hiding behind obstacles that you yourself helped create? Examine one specific area where you blame circumstances. According to this lesson, does that excuse remove your agency, or does it merely reveal an obstacle that you must still account for?

First Proof: Body Between Two Agents
First proof against generated acts being human: body between two agents; objection follows.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof from a body between two agents and the opponent's objection that weakens it.

Core Idea
The dilemma of two agents pulling one body exposes the limits of human power over generated acts.

This passage presents a key Ash'ari proof against generated acts being within human power. Using the analogy of a body held by two agents, they pose a dilemma. However, the text also includes a critical consideration that shows the proof is not airtight. Let us examine the logic and the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source text carefully, then the translation, to get the sense of the argument.
Notice the technical terms: al-jism al-multaziq, al-qādirān, al-ʿilla al-mustaqilla.
2
Identify the three logical possibilities
The body's movement, when pulled and pushed simultaneously, can only be caused by three alternatives: both powers combined, one power alone, or neither. List them.
Each possibility leads to a different conclusion.
3
Understand why two independent causes on one effect is impossible
In Ash'ari causality, two independent causes cannot produce a single effect. This refutes the first possibility.
This is a key principle in their theology.
4
Understand why preference without preponderant is impossible
If only one power caused the movement, there must be a reason why that power prevailed, but no such preponderant exists. This refutes the second possibility.
This logical principle is called tarajjuḥ bilā murjīḥ.
5
Recognize the opponent's objection
The opponent argues that each power is not independent in producing the specific movement that occurs jointly; they are only independent in general movement. This undermines the dilemma.
The proof is therefore not decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج أصحابنا بوجوه الأول : أن الجسم الملتزق طرفاه بيدي قادرين إذا جذبه أحدهما ودفعه الآخر معاً فحركته إما أن تقع بمجموع القدرتين فيلزم اجتماع العلتين المستقلتين على معلول واحد أو بإحداهما فيلزم الترجح بلا مرجح أو لا بهما وهو المطلوب، وفيه نظر إذ للخصم أن يمنع استقلال كل من القوتين بإحداث الحركة على الوجه الذي وقع باجتماعهما غاية الأمر أنها تستقل بإحداث حركة ذلك الجسم في الجملة
And our scholars (aṣḥābunā) argued with proofs. The first: that a body whose two ends are held by the hands of two capable agents—if one pulls it and the other pushes it together—then its movement either occurs by the combination of the two powers, necessitating the concurrence of two independent causes on one effect; or by one of them, necessitating preference without a preponderant; or by neither, which is the desired conclusion. And there is a consideration: for the opponent can deny the independence of each of the two powers in producing the movement in the manner that occurred by their conjunction; at most, each is independent in producing the movement of that body in general.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents the first of several proofs by the Ashʿari scholars (aṣḥābunā) against the view that generated acts are in the power of the slave. The proof is a logical dilemma. Consider a body held by two agents: one pulls, the other pushes simultaneously. The resulting movement can only be attributed to: (a) the combination of both powers – which would mean two independent causes (ʿillatān mustaqillatān) producing one effect, an impossibility in Ashʿari causality; (b) one of the powers alone – which would require a preponderant (murjīḥ) to decide which one, without any such preponderant; or (c) neither of them – which is what the Ashʿaris want to prove (that the movement is not from the slave's power at all). However, the text immediately adds a critique (wa fīhi naẓar): the opponent can deny that each power is truly independent in producing the movement exactly as it occurred jointly; rather, each is independent only in producing the body's movement in general, not the specific movement. So the proof is not conclusive.

Key Terms
الجسم الملتزق (al-jism al-multaziq)
القادران (al-qādirān)
العلة المستقلة (al-ʿilla al-mustaqilla)
الترجح بلا مرجح (al-tarajjuḥ bilā murjīḥ)
الفعل المباشر (al-fiʿl al-mubāshir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this proof definitively refutes human power over generated acts.
Correction: The 'consideration' shows the opponent can object, so the proof is not conclusive.

Checkpoint
What is the desired conclusion (al-maṭlūb) in the first proof?
A) The movement occurs by both powers together
B) The movement occurs by one power alone
C) The movement occurs by neither power
D) The movement occurs by divine power only

Key Takeaway
The proof from two agents pulling a body is a clever dilemma, but it fails because the opponent can deny that each agent's power is independent in producing the specific movement that results from their joint action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something you worked hard for, do you ever catch yourself assuming that your effort alone was the independent cause? How does this proof's objection—that no agent's power is independent in producing the specific outcome—challenge that assumption? Will you pause today to acknowledge the countless factors beyond your control that contributed to your success?
?
The opponent in this proof denies that each power is independent 'in the manner that occurred by their conjunction'. In your relationships, when conflict arises, do you assume your perspective is the independent and sufficient cause of your feelings? What would change if you admitted that your reaction is not solely your own but also shaped by the other person's actions? Can you think of a recent disagreement where this realization could soften your heart?
?
This lesson reveals that even a seemingly airtight proof has a 'consideration' that prevents it from being conclusive. How often do you hold your theological convictions with absolute certainty, dismissing objections without genuine reflection? When was the last time you sincerely entertained a counterargument to a belief you cherish? What would it cost you to remain open to the possibility that your understanding might be incomplete?

Second Proof: Analogy with Divine Power
Taftazani's second proof by analogy with divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy with divine power demonstrates that generated acts are not truly within human power.

Core Idea
True power is uncaused; human power requires causes.

This lesson presents the second proof from Taftazani's 'Sharh al-Maqasid' against the view that humans generate their own acts. The argument draws a sharp contrast between divine and human power: if our power were genuine, we would be able to produce effects without their usual causes. Since we cannot, our power is not real.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic sentence once, then the translation. Notice its concise structure: a conditional statement followed by a comparison.
The passage is only one line but packs a complete argument.
2
Understand the analogy
The proof compares human power to divine power. Allah can create acts without any cause (e.g., creating life directly). If humans had real power, they should be able to do the same without the usual means. Since they cannot, their 'power' is not genuine.
3
Reflect on the implication
If the generated act required a cause (like a weapon for killing), then the act is not solely produced by the human's power. The cause is a necessary condition, showing that the act is not fully in human control.
This argument reinforces the Ash'ari position that all acts are ultimately from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثاني : أنه لو كان مقدوراً للعبد الجاز وقوعه بلا توسط السبب كما في حق الباري تعالى
Second: that if it were in the power of the slave, it would be permissible for it to occur without the mediation of the cause, as in the case of the Exalted Creator.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This is a very concise second proof. It argues that if the generated act were truly within the power of the human slave (abd), then just as Allah (al-Bārī) can bring about acts without any mediating cause, so too should the slave be able to produce the generated act without its usual cause (sabab). Since the slave cannot do this—e.g., one cannot produce a killing without a weapon—the generated act is not in the slave's power. The proof relies on the principle that genuine power (qudra) does not depend on causes; only contingent created power does.

Key Terms
مقدور (maqdūr)
توسط السبب (tawassuṭ al-sabab)
الباري (al-Bārī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans can produce acts without any intermediate cause because they possess real power.
Correction: The proof shows that since humans cannot produce generated acts without their usual causes (e.g., killing without a weapon), the act is not truly in their power; real power is uncaused as with Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the second proof?
A) The impossibility of two independent causes
B) The analogy with divine power which does not require a cause
C) The fact that generated acts are subject to praise and blame
D) The alignment of generated acts with intentions

Key Takeaway
The second proof uses the analogy with divine power to show that human power, which depends on causes, cannot be the real agent of generated acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you perform—from writing a word to earning a living—is only realized by Allah's power, or do you secretly rely on your own ability and the means you have? Take a recent success: can you honestly say it was Allah who created it, not your skill or effort? How would your daily attitude change if you lived in constant awareness that without His uncaused power, nothing you attempt would come to be?
?
Think of a time when you had all the necessary means (money, health, knowledge) yet still failed to achieve something. In that moment, did you feel the limitation of your own power? How did that experience strip away your illusion of self-sufficiency? What would it mean to never forget that dependence on Allah is not weakness but truth?
?
The lesson defines true power as that which acts without any cause. Is there any area of your life where you act as if you are the uncaused cause—where you ignore your dependence on Allah and claim ownership of outcomes? What would it look like to repent from that subtle idolatry and return to the reality that 'there is no power nor strength except with Allah'?

Third Proof: Cause Necessitates Effect
Third proof against Mu'tazilite causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof against the Mu'tazilite view of causality, which shows that if cause necessitates effect, then the direct act would be independent in producing the generated act, making divine power superfluous.

Core Idea
If cause necessitates effect, human power becomes irrelevant.

This lesson examines a key argument against the Mu'tazilite view that causes necessitate their effects. The proof shows that if a cause directly produces its effect without any other influence, then the human act that generates the effect would have no need for the slave's power to produce it. This has significant implications for the relationship between God's power and human action.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
Recall that Mu'tazilites believe causes necessitate their effects in the absence of obstacles. This sets the stage for the critique.
This is their core claim about causality.
2
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the argument sink in.
The passage is short but dense.
3
Identify the logical consequence
The passage states that if cause necessitates effect, then the direct act (al-fi'l al-mubāshir) would independently necessitate the generated act. Identify this inference.
Focus on the phrase 'istqilālan bi-ījāb al-mutawallad'.
4
Reflect on the implication for divine power
Consider the conclusion: 'without any influence of power in it'. This means the generated act does not need the slave's power, contradicting Mu'tazilite belief in human agency.
This undermines their doctrine of human free will.
5
Analyze how the proof refutes the view
Discuss why this is a problem for the Mu'tazilites. The proof uses their own principle (cause necessitates effect) to show that it eliminates the role of power.
It is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالث : أن السبب عندهم موجب للمسبب عند عدم المانع ، فيلزم أن يكون الفعل المباشر مستقلاً بإيجاب المتولد من غير تأثير للقدرة فيه
Third: that the cause, according to them, necessitates the effect when there is no obstacle, so it would follow that the direct act is independent in necessitating the generated act, without any influence of power in it.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This third proof targets the Muʿtazilī view that a cause (sabab) necessitates its effect (musabbab) in the absence of obstacles. If that were true, then the direct act (al-fiʿl al-mubāshir)—the act that immediately produces the generated act—would independently necessitate the generated act, leaving no role for the slave's power (qudra). Thus, the generated act cannot be attributed to the slave's power; it is entirely due to the cause.

Key Terms
السبب
المسبب
الموجب
المانع
الفعل المباشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite view of causality does not undermine human agency.
Correction: This proof shows that if cause necessitates effect, then the direct act would be independent in producing the generated act, leaving no role for the slave's power, thus actually denying human agency.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what would be the consequence if a cause necessitates its effect?
A) The direct act would independently necessitate the generated act, leaving no role for power.
B) The generated act would not occur.
C) The obstacle would be irrelevant.
D) The power of the slave would be independent.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari argument shows that if causes necessitate effects, then generated acts are independent of human power, contradicting the Mu'tazilite view of human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent action you performed that you feel proud of—perhaps a good deed or an achievement. Did you attribute that success to your own ability (qudra) or to Allah? This lesson argues that if causes necessitate effects, your power is irrelevant. How does that challenge your sense of ownership over your actions? What would it mean for you to sincerely attribute every outcome solely to Allah?
?
The Mu'tazilites believed that human power is effective in generating acts. But this proof shows that their own causal principle actually eliminates the role of power. When you find yourself relying on your own strength or planning, do you ever stop to consider that true causation lies only with Allah? How can you train yourself to see His hand in every 'natural' cause and effect in your daily life?
?
If the direct act necessitates the generated act without any influence of power, then your 'choices' are merely placeholders. Does this realization frighten you or liberate you? Reflect on a moment when you felt trapped by your own decisions. How does the Ash'ari perspective—that all acts are directly from Allah—offer a way to release the burden of self-reliance and instead embrace dependence on Him? What concrete step can you take today to live this truth?

Fourth Proof: Disappearance of Power and Ash'ari Position
Fourth proof: if generated act were in slave's power, it would cease with power; objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof against generated acts being in human power, the objection and response, and the decisive Ash'ari doctrine.

Core Idea
If an act persists after the agent's power ends, it cannot be in that agent's power.

This lesson presents the fourth proof from the Ash'ari tradition against the idea that generated acts (like wounds from an arrow) are within human power. The proof hinges on the observation that such acts can occur after the agent has died, which would be impossible if they were truly in his power. The passage also includes an important objection and the final Ash'ari stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's structure.
Pay attention to the logical flow: premise, consequence, counterexample.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The proof states a conditional: if generated acts were in the slave's power, they would not exist when his power perishes. The consequent is shown false by the archer example.
Notice how the example is used as a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the objection
The objection suggests that conditions for originated power may differ from eternal power, and that power might act on the cause rather than the effect.
This objection challenges the premise that the effect must be directly in power.
4
Understand the final Ash'ari position
The passage concludes with the definitive doctrine: whatever occurs in a locus distinct from the agent's body cannot be an object of his power at all.
This clarifies the scope of human power: it only extends to immediate acts, not remote effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابع : أنه لو كان بقدرة العبد لزم أن لا يوجد عند فناء قدرة العبد واللازم باطل فيما إذا رمى الإنسان سهماً ومات. قيل إن أصاب السهم حياً فجرحه وأفضى إلى زهوق روحه بعد شهور وأعوام فهذه السرايات والآلام أفعال (۱) حدثت (۲) بعدما صار الرامي عظاماً رمياً. واعترض بأنه يجوز أن يشترط في تأثير القدرة الحادثة ما لا يشترط في القديمة، وبأن معنى كون (۳) المتولد بقدرة العبد تأثيرها في السبب الموجب له . واعلم أن مذهب أصحابنا أن ما يقع مبايناً لمحل القدرة الحادثة لا يكون مقدوراً لها أصلاً.
Fourth: that if it were in the power of the slave, it would necessitate that it would not exist when the slave's power perishes, but the consequent is false in the case where a person shoots an arrow and dies. It is said: if the arrow hits a living being, wounds him, and leads to the expiration of his soul after months and years, then these continuations and pains are acts that occurred after the shooter became decayed bones. And it was objected that it is possible for conditions to be stipulated in the influence of the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) that are not stipulated in the eternal power, and that the meaning of the generated being by the slave's power is its influence in the cause that necessitates it. And know that the doctrine of our scholars (aṣḥābunā) is that whatever occurs distinct from the locus of the originated power cannot be an object of power for it at all.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof against generated acts being in the slave's power, along with an objection and the final Ashʿari position. The proof argues: if a generated act (e.g., the wound caused by an arrow) were truly in the power of the slave, it would not exist once the slave's power ceases (i.e., when he dies). But this is false: a man can shoot an arrow, die, and the arrow later hits someone, causing death after the shooter has become bones. So the generated act persists after the agent's power has perished, showing it is not an object of his power. An objection is then raised: it is possible that the conditions for the influence of originated power (qudra ḥāditha) differ from those of eternal power (al-qudra al-qadīma), and that the meaning of 'the generated act being by the slave's power' is that the slave's power influences the cause that necessitates the effect, not the effect directly. The passage concludes with the definitive Ashʿari doctrine: whatever occurs in a locus distinct from the locus of the originated power (i.e., outside the agent's body) cannot be an object of that power at all.

Key Terms
فناء القدرة (fanā' al-qudra)
السهم (al-sahm)
السرايات (al-sarāyāt)
القدرة الحادثة (al-qudra al-ḥāditha)
القدرة القديمة (al-qudra al-qadīma)
السبب الموجب (al-sabab al-mūjib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The archer example shows humans have no power over any act.
Correction: The example only shows that remote effects cannot be directly in human power; power may influence the immediate cause.

Checkpoint
What is the consequent deemed false in the fourth proof?
A) The generated act would occur only by divine power
B) The generated act would not exist when the slave's power perishes
C) The archer would not die
D) The arrow would not hit

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position is that human power cannot directly produce effects in a locus distinct from the agent's own body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a decision you made long ago that had consequences far beyond what you could control. How does this passage challenge your assumption that you own those distant outcomes? What would it mean to truly surrender that illusion of control to Allah?
?
You have likely felt responsible for harm that came from your actions but was beyond your immediate power. In light of this lesson, what do you need to release from the burden of false responsibility? What does this demand you trust in Allah's justice instead?
?
If the archer is not held accountable for the remote death of his victim, where do you draw the line between what is truly in your power and what is not? Examine a recurring sin in your life: are you claiming power over its consequences that you do not have, and how might that keep you from repentance?

Mu'tazilite Arguments on Evil and Divine Will
The author presents and refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that Allah does not will evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite arguments against Allah willing evil and the author's refutation that they are based on invalid analogies.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites project human morality onto Allah, but divine will transcends human categories.

The Mu'tazilites argued that Allah cannot will evil because that would be evil, unjust, foolish, and imply approval of evil. The author of this text counters that their reasoning is corrupt and their proof texts are misapplied. This lesson examines their claims and the refutation, highlighting the danger of anthropomorphic analogies in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how the Mu'tazilite position is presented followed by their four arguments.
2
Identify the four Mu'tazilite arguments
The passage lists four reasons why they think Allah cannot will evil. Find each one and summarize it in your own words.
They are: willing evil is evil, punishment for what is willed is unjust, commanding what is not willed is foolish, and will implies approval.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author says all these arguments are corrupt and their Quranic evidence does not support them. Consider why human analogies fail when talking about Allah's attributes.
This refutation is brief; the author does not elaborate further here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة لم يكتفوا بقطع إرادته عن القبائح، بل جزموا بأنها متعلقة بأضدادها. فجعلوا أكثر ما يجري في ملكه خلاف مراده، تمسكاً بأن إرادة القبيح قبيحة. وأن العقاب على ما أريد ظلم، وأن الأمر بما لا يراد والنهي عما يراد سفه، وأن الإرادة تستلزم الأمر والرضاء والمحبة والكل فاسد ولا تمسك لهم بمثل قوله تعالى : ( وما الله يريد ظلماً للعباد ) (۳) قل إن الله لا يأمر بالفحشاء (٣) و ولا يرضى لعباده الكفر () والله لا يحب الفساد (ه) .
The Mu'tazilites were not content to cut off His will from evil deeds, but they were certain that it is attached to their opposites. So they made most of what occurs in His kingdom contrary to His will, clinging to the argument that willing evil is evil, and that punishment for what is willed is injustice, and that commanding what is not willed and prohibiting what is willed is foolish, and that will necessitates command, contentment, and love—all of this is corrupt, and they have no support from such verses as 'And Allah does not will injustice for His servants' (40:31) and 'Say: Allah does not command immorality' (7:28) and 'And He is not pleased with disbelief for His servants' (39:7) and 'And Allah does not love corruption' (2:205).
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite position that Allah's will is not related to evil deeds; rather, it is related to their opposites (i.e., good). Consequently, they claim that most events in the universe happen against Allah's will. They support this with four arguments: (1) willing evil is evil; (2) punishing what one has willed is unjust; (3) commanding what one does not will and forbidding what one does will is foolish; (4) will necessarily entails command, contentment, and love, so evil cannot be willed. The author refutes these by noting that the Mu'tazilites themselves rely on certain verses that seem to support their view, but these verses do not actually prove their case—they simply misinterpret them. The author does not elaborate further here but indicates the argument is flawed.

Key Terms
قبائح
إرادة القبيح
ظلم
سفه
أمر
رضاء
محبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah cannot will evil because willing evil is evil and would make Allah unjust.
Correction: Divine will is not bound by human moral categories; evil exists by Allah's will as part of a wise plan that ultimately serves good.

Checkpoint
What is one of the Mu'tazilite arguments mentioned against Allah's willing evil?
A) Willing evil would make Allah evil
B) Punishing what is willed is unjust
C) Allah cannot will both good and evil
D) Evil is not real

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite arguments against Allah willing evil are based on anthropomorphic analogies that the author dismisses as corrupt and unsupported by the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil in the world—whether your own sins or the suffering of others—do you secretly doubt that Allah's will is behind it all? How does this passage challenge you to submit to His comprehensive will even when it troubles your human sense of justice?
?
The Mu'tazilites were driven by a desire to protect Allah's perfection from association with evil. Have you ever tried to 'defend' Allah by explaining away His will or limiting His control over certain events? What would it cost you to simply say, 'Allah wills whatever He wills, and it is all wise'?
?
If you truly believed that every evil you experience is willed by Allah for a purpose beyond your understanding, what would change about how you react to your next trial or temptation? Are you ready to accept that will and let it transform your response?

The Comprehensive Will of Allah in Sunni Doctrine
The author states the doctrine of Ahl al-Haqq on divine will and contrasts with Mu'tazilite view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that Allah's will encompasses all existence and the rationale for avoiding explicit attribution of evil to His will.

Core Idea
Allah's will extends to everything that exists, but we do not describe it in detail for evil things.

This passage establishes the foundational Sunni belief about divine will: it covers all that occurs. The author emphasizes that while we affirm Allah creates all, we avoid saying He wills specific evils, just as we avoid saying He creates filth. Understanding this nuance prevents misunderstanding the absolute nature of divine will.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim about Allah's will
Read the first part of the passage where the author states that Allah's will is related to every existent thing. Notice the supporting hadith.
The hadith 'What Allah wills occurs...' is a key proof.
2
Understand the caution about detail
The author notes that some scholars avoid saying Allah wills unbelief, just as we avoid saying He creates filth, even though He is Creator of all. This is a matter of respect and following the Salaf.
3
Contrast with the Mu'tazilite view
The Mu'tazilites claim Allah wills faith from the disbeliever even if it does not occur, and does not will the actual unbelief. The author finds this absurd, comparing it to a village chief who would not tolerate such defiance.
Consider why this position seems untenable to the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذهب أهل الحق أن إرادة الله تعالى متعلقة بكل كائن غير متعلقة بما ليس بكائن، على ما اشتهر من السلف، وروي مرفوعاً إلى النبي عليه السلام «إن ما شاء كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن لكن منهم من منع التفصيل بأن يقال إنه يريد الكفر والظلم والفسق كما في الخلق يقال إنه خالق الكل، ولا يقال : خالق القاذورات والقردة والخنازير، وخالفت المعتزلة في الشرور والقبائح، فزعموا أنه يريد من الكافر الإيمان ، وإن لم يقع لا الكفر وإن وقع، وكذا يريد من الفاسق الطاعة لا الفسق، حتى أن أكثر ما يقع من العباد خلاف مراده . والظاهر أنه لا يصبر على ذلك رئيس قرية من عباده . حكى أنه دخل القاضي عبد الجبار () داراً للصاحب بن عباد فرأى الأستاذ أبي إسحق الاسفرائني . فقال سبحان من تنزه عن الفحشاء. فقال الأستاذ على الفور : سبحان من لا يجري في ملكه إلا ما يشاء والتقصي عن ذلك، بأنه أراد من العباد الإيمان والطاعة برغبتهم واختيارهم، فلا عجز ولا نقيصة ولا مغلوبية له في عدم
The doctrine of Ahl al-Haqq is that the will of Allah is related to every existing thing and is not related to what is not existing, as has been transmitted from the Salaf, and it has been narrated as a marfū‘ hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'What Allah wills occurs and what He does not will does not occur.' However, some of them forbade the detail that it should be said that He wills unbelief, injustice, and sin, just as in creation it is said that He is the Creator of all, but it is not said: 'Creator of filth, monkeys, and pigs.' The Mu'tazilites differed concerning evils and wicked deeds, claiming that He wills from the disbeliever faith, even if it does not occur, not unbelief even if it occurs, and likewise He wills from the sinner obedience, not sin, so that most of what occurs from servants is contrary to His will. It seems that a village chief among his servants would not tolerate that. It is related that Qadi Abd al-Jabbar entered a house of al-Sahib ibn Abbad and saw the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. He said, 'Glory be to Him who is transcendent above obscenity.' The master immediately replied, 'Glory be to Him from whose kingdom nothing occurs except what He wills.' And the thorough investigation of that is that He willed from the servants faith and obedience willingly and by their choice, so there is no incapacity, deficiency, or defeat for Him in the non-
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage reaffirms the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth) that Allah's will encompasses all beings. It notes that some scholars avoid saying 'He wills unbelief' explicitly, as one avoids saying 'He creates filth' even though He is the Creator of all. The Mu'tazilites hold the opposite: Allah wills faith from the disbeliever even if it does not occur, and does not will the actual unbelief. The author finds this absurd—even a village chief would not tolerate such a situation. He then recounts a famous encounter: the Mu'tazilite Qadi Abd al-Jabbar praises Allah's transcendence, and the Ash‘ari Abu Ishaq counter-praises with the principle that nothing occurs in His kingdom except what He wills. The passage ends by explaining that when Allah wills faith from the servant through their own choice and desire, there is no deficiency in Him if they choose otherwise—He still wills the actual occurrence (their choice) but the phrase breaks off.

Key Terms
أهل الحق
إرادة متعلقة بكل كائن
تفصيل
قاذورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if Allah wills everything, then He directly wills evil actions like unbelief.
Correction: The Sunni position affirms that Allah's will encompasses all, but we do not explicitly say He wills unbelief, following the Salaf's cautious language, analogously to avoiding saying He creates filth.

Checkpoint
What is the position of Ahl al-Haqq regarding Allah's will?
A) It is related only to good things.
B) It is related to every existing thing.
C) It is not related to human actions.
D) It is only related to what He commands.

Key Takeaway
Allah's will covers all existence, but we follow the Salaf in not detailing it for evil actions, affirming His absolute sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a trial, do you truly accept that it is part of Allah's will, or do you secretly wish it had not happened? How can you train your heart to embrace whatever Allah wills, even when it is painful?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that most of what happens contradicts Allah's will, implying weakness. Is there any area in your life where you act as if your own will can override Allah's? What does that reveal about your tawheed?
?
If a harmful event occurs, do you find yourself questioning Allah's justice? How can you use the teaching of this lesson—that Allah's will is comprehensive and we do not detail it for evil—to guard your faith against such doubts?

The Two Scholars' Exchange: A Lesson in Divine Will
The author recounts the anecdote and explains its theological significance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the anecdote between Qadi Abd al-Jabbar and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini illustrates the difference between Mu'tazilite and Ash'ari understandings of divine will.

Core Idea
The rebuttal of transcendent purity with all-encompassing will.

This historical anecdote captures the heart of the debate. The Mu'tazilite Qadi Abd al-Jabbar praises Allah's transcendence from evil, and the Ash'ari master Abu Ishaq counters by affirming that nothing occurs outside Allah's will. The author then explains that Allah wills through human choice, so no deficiency arises.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote and the two statements
Read the exchange between Qadi Abd al-Jabbar and Abu Ishaq. Note the exact phrasing of each statement.
The first statement emphasizes God's transcendence from evil; the second emphasizes God's absolute will over all that occurs.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite intention
Qadi Abd al-Jabbar's praise of transcendence is typical of Mu'tazilite theology, which denies that Allah wills evil. His statement implies that evil is not from Allah's will.
3
See how the Ash'ari response reasserts divine sovereignty
Abu Ishaq's immediate reply redirects focus back to Allah's all-encompassing will. The author explains that when Allah wills faith and obedience through the servant's own choice, there is no incapacity or deficiency if the servant chooses otherwise.
The passage cuts off, but the idea is that Allah's will is still fully effective.

The Mu'tazilite View on the Purpose of Moral Obligation and Its Refutation
Outlines and refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that taklif is for exposing to reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that the purpose of moral obligation (taklif) is exposure to reward, and the Ash'ari refutation based on divine grace and alternative purposes.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites insisted on deserved reward; the Ash'aris replied that reward can be pure divine grace.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents a critical theological debate regarding why God imposes moral obligations on humans. The Mu'tazilites argue that the sole purpose is to expose humans to reward, which can only be good if deserved through hardship. The refutation challenges the necessity of such desert, offering other purposes and emphasizing that reward is ultimately a divine favor. This discussion highlights key differences between Mu'tazilite rationalism and Ash'ari theocentrism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the concise style of kalam arguments.
2
Identify the three Mu'tazilite proofs
The Mu'tazilites use a scriptural proof (Quranic verse), a consensus proof (no other purpose), and a moral proof (obligation without desert is injustice). Find each in the text.
The moral proof is the most philosophical.
3
Understand the main refutation points
The refutation argues that reward may be pure favor, rejects consensus, proposes alternative purposes, and questions the necessity of any purpose in divine acts.
Focus on how the refutation shifts from necessity to possibility.
4
Reflect on the concluding argument
The passage ends by acknowledging that difficult acts do have effect in establishing desert, citing Quranic verses, but this does not prove the Mu'tazilite claim.
Consider how the author balances both perspectives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (خاتمة) ذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن الغرض من التكليف هو التعريض للثواب فإنه لا يحسن بدون الاستحقاق الحاصل بالمشاق ويدل عليه وجوه: فتعين. الأول: مثل قوله تعالى ومن يطع الله ورسوله يدخله جنات (٦) الآية. الثاني: أنه لا غرض سواء إجماعاً لأنهم لا يثبتون الغرض ونحن ننفي غيره. الثالث: أن التكليف بالمشاق إضرار وهو بدون استحقاق، ولا منفعة ظلم فيكون التعريض للمنفعة هو الجهة المحسنة. ورد بأن المترتب قد يكون فضلاً من الله تعالى لا أثراً لما ترتب عليه، وكيف يعقل استحقاق النعيم الدائم بمجرد كلمة وتصديق فيمن آمن فمات. ولا نسلم الإجماع على أنه لا غرض سواه، فقيل الابتلاء، وقيل الشكر، وقيل حفظ النظام، وقيل أمر لا طريق إليه للعقل. ولو سلم فلا يفيد كونه الغرض إلا بعد ثبوت لزوم الغرض ولم يثبت. ولو بالنسبة إلى من مات على الكفر أو الفسق هو التعريض للثواب أعني منافع كثيرة دائمة خالصة السرور والتعظيم، فإن ذلك لا يحسن بدون الاستحقاق مع ولا خفاء في أن للأفعال والتروك الشاقة تأثيراً في إثبات الاستحقاق بشهادة الآيات والأحاديث الدالة على ترتب الثواب. استحقاق التعظيم على تلك الأفعال والتروك، ومن يطع الله ورسوله يدخله جنات تجري من تحتها الأنهار (۳) ثواباً من عند الله (٤) ومن عمل صالحاً من ذكر أو أنثى وهو مؤمن فلنحيينه حياة طيبة ولنجزينهم
He said (Conclusion): The Mu'tazilites held that the purpose of moral obligation (taklif) is exposure to reward, for it is not good without deserving (istihqaq) attained through hardships. Evidence for this: First, like His saying, 'Whoever obeys Allah and the Messenger, He will admit him to gardens' (4:13) verse. Second, that there is no other purpose of taklif by consensus, because they (the Asha'ira?) do not affirm purpose, and we deny any other. Third, that obligation involving hardship is harmful, and without desert, and no benefit, it is injustice; so exposing to benefit is the aspect that makes it good. It was replied that what results (reward) may be a favor from God, not an effect of what it is based on. How can eternal bliss be deserved by a mere word and belief from one who believed and died? And we do not accept the consensus that there is no other purpose; it is said: trial, gratitude, preservation of order, or something beyond reason. Even if accepted, it does not prove it to be the purpose unless the necessity of purpose is established, and it is not established. And with regard to one who dies in disbelief or sin, exposure to reward means many permanent pleasures free of sorrow and with exaltation; that is not good without desert. There is no doubt that difficult acts and abstentions have an effect in establishing desert, as evidenced by verses and hadiths indicating reward as a consequence. Deserving exaltation for those acts and abstentions. And 'Whoever obeys Allah and the Messenger, He will admit him to gardens beneath which rivers flow' (48:17) 'as a reward from Allah' (3:195), and 'Whoever does righteousness, male or female, while a believer, We will surely give him a good life and We will surely reward them' (16:97).
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and refutation of the Mu'tazilite position on the purpose of taklif (moral obligation). The author begins by stating the Mu'tazilite view: the purpose of taklif is to expose the servant to reward, and this exposure is only good when it is deserved (istihqaq) through the hardships of obligation. They offer three proofs: (1) a Quranic verse linking obedience to entering gardens; (2) a claimed consensus that there is no other purpose; (3) a moral argument that obligation without desert would be harmful and unjust, so the only justifying aspect is the benefit of reward. The refutation follows: first, the reward may be a pure favor (fadl) from God, not a necessary effect of the obligation; one cannot conceive how eternal bliss is deserved by a mere word and faith. Second, the claimed consensus is rejected; others propose alternative purposes such as trial, gratitude, preservation of order, or something unknowable. Third, even if consensus were accepted, it still requires proving that purpose is necessary in God's acts, which has not been established. The author also argues that exposure to reward is not good without desert, and that difficult acts do indeed have an effect in establishing desert, citing Quranic verses that show reward is tied to deeds. The passage ends with citations stressing that reward is from God as a result of obedience.

Key Terms
تكليف
تعريض للثواب
استحقاق
مشاق
غرض
فضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The misunderstanding that reward is always necessarily deserved; the author argues it may be a pure divine favor.
Correction: The refutation emphasizes that reward can be a divine grace (fadl) rather than a necessary consequence of obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the third proof of the Mu'tazilites for their view on the purpose of taklif?
A) A Quranic verse about entering gardens.
B) Consensus that there is no other purpose.
C) The harm of obligation without desert would be injustice.
D) The necessity of purpose in all divine acts.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite argument that taklif must be for deserved reward is refuted by the Ash'ari emphasis on divine grace and the possibility of alternative purposes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that your prayers or charity entitle you to a specific reward from Allah? How does the Ash'ari teaching that reward is pure grace challenge that sense of entitlement? What would change in your worship if you truly believed every blessing is an unmerited gift?
?
When you face hardship in obeying Allah—like waking for Fajr or avoiding a sin—do you secretly expect Allah to 'owe' you something in return? How would the claim that obligation is for trial and gratitude, not just reward, reshape your response to that hardship?
?
If Allah were to treat you with pure justice based on your deeds, what would you deserve? After reading this passage, can you honestly say you still believe your good deeds obligate Allah? What would it mean for your relationship with Him to let go of that expectation and accept everything as fadl?

Definition of Rizq and First Refutation
The author defines rizq and rebuts the first Mu'tazila objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definitions of rizq and how the Ash'arite refutes the Mu'tazila claim that haram cannot be provision.

Core Idea
Rizq is defined by divine attribution, not by moral permissibility.

This passage begins by clarifying the definition of rizq (provision) through linguistic and technical lenses, addressing ambiguities and the concept of ownership. It then presents the Mu'tazila objection that forbidden things cannot be considered rizq due to their ugliness (qabih), and the Ash'arite refutation using a Qur'anic verse and a logical counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author moves from definitions to argument.
2
Identify the definitions discussed
The passage discusses various interpretations of rizq: ownership, benefit, and the verbal noun form. Identify each and note why some are problematic.
The author prefers a definition that retains relation to the Provider.
3
Find the Mu'tazila objection and the refutation
Locate the Mu'tazila claim that haram cannot be rizq because of its ugliness, and then the author's proof from the Qur'an and the counterexample.
The counterexample of a person who eats only haram shows the absurdity of their position.
4
Understand the 'answer' to the potential rebuttal
The author anticipates a possible answer from the Mu'tazila and shows it is invalid by a case of death without any food.
The phrase 'your answer is our answer' is a rhetorical closure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حيث يقال: رزقه الله ولداً صالحاً. وأراد بالعبد ما يشمل البهائم تغليباً، وتفسيره بالملك ليس بمطرد ولا منعكس لدخول ملك الله تعالى وخروج رزق الدواب، بل العبيد والإماء مع الاختلال بما في مفهومه من الإضافة إلى الرازق، اللهم إلا أن يقال المراد المملوك أي المجعول ملكاً بمعنى الإذن في التصرف الشرعي وفيه معنى الإضافة، ولا يشمل ملك الله تعالى ويدخل رزق غير الإنسان بطريق هذا التغليب . لكن لا بد مع من قيد الانتفاع وحينئذ فخروج ملك الله تعالى ظاهر، ومن فسره بالانتفاع أراد المنتفع به، أو أخذ الرزق مصدراً من المبني للمفعول، أي الارتزاق، ولما كان الرزق مضافاً إلى الرازق وهو الله تعالى وحده ، لم يكن الحرام المنتفع به رزقاً عند المعتزلة لقبحه. وقد عرفت فساد أصلهم ، ولزمهم أن من لم يأكل طول عمره إلا الحرام لم يرزقه الله تعالى وهو باطل لقوله تعالى وما من دابة في الأرض إلا على الله رزقها ) . وأجيب بأنه تعالى قد ساق إليه كثيراً من المباح إلا أنه أعرض عنه لسوء اختياره على أنه منقوض بمن مات ولم يأكل حلالاً ولا حراماً، فجوابكم جوابنا. قالوا لو كان الحرام رزقاً لما جاز دفعه عنه ، ولا الذم ولا العقاب عليه . قلنا : ممنوع وإنما يصح لو لم يكن مرتكباً للمنهي عنه مكتسباً للقبح من الفعل سيما في مباشرة الأسباب لأن السعي في تحصيل الرزق قد يجب وذلك عند الحاجة، وقد يستحب، وذلك عند قصد التوسعة على نفسه وعياله، وقد يباح وذلك عند قصد التكثير من غير ارتكاب منهي ، وقد يحرم وذلك عند ارتكاب المنهي كالغصب والسرقة والربا
Where it is said: 'May Allah bless him with a righteous child.' And by 'servant' he intended inclusively of animals by way of predominance, and its interpretation as ownership is neither universal nor reversible because it includes the ownership of Allah the Exalted and excludes the provision of beasts. Rather, male and female slaves are included, with the defect in its concept of relation to the Provider, unless it is said that what is meant is the possessed, i.e., that which is made property in the sense of permission for legal disposal, and in it is the meaning of relation, and it does not include the ownership of Allah the Exalted, and it includes the provision of non-humans through this predominance. But it is necessary with whoever restricted it to benefit, and then the exclusion of Allah's ownership is apparent. And whoever interpreted it as benefit intended the thing benefited from, or took 'rizq' as a verbal noun from the passive construction, i.e., being provided. And since rizq is attributed to the Provider, who is Allah alone, the forbidden thing benefited from is not rizq according to the Mu'tazila because of its ugliness. And you have known the corruption of their principle, and it necessarily follows from them that whoever eats nothing but forbidden food his entire life has not been provided by Allah, and that is false due to His saying: 'There is no creature on earth but that upon Allah is its provision' (Hud 6). And it was answered that He the Exalted may have brought to him much permissible food, but he turned away from it due to his evil choice, and moreover it is refuted by one who died without eating either permissible or forbidden; so your answer is our answer. They said: If the forbidden were provision, then it would not be permissible to prevent it from him, nor blame nor punishment upon him. We said: It is denied; and it would only be correct if he were not committing the prohibited act, acquiring ugliness from the action, especially in directly engaging in the causes, because striving to obtain provision may be obligatory, that is at times of need, and may be recommended, that is when intending to expand upon oneself and one's family, and may be permissible, that is when intending increase without committing a prohibited act, and may be prohibited, that is when committing the prohibited act such as usurpation, theft, and usury.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument concerning the definition of rizq (provision) and whether it includes haram (forbidden) food. The author begins by discussing the linguistic and technical definitions of rizq, noting that the Mu'tazila hold that haram cannot be rizq because it is qabih (ugly). He then refutes their position by stating that their principle is corrupt, and demonstrates a logical consequence: if a person eats only haram his whole life, then according to them Allah has not provided for him, but the Qur'an says every creature's provision is from Allah. Next, he presents an answer to a potential counter: Allah may have brought much halal to him, but he turned away—yet this is refuted by the case of one who dies without eating anything. Then the Mu'tazila raise a second objection: if haram were rizq, then it would not be permissible to prevent it from him, nor to blame or punish him for it. The author responds by denying the premise; rather, blame and punishment are for committing the prohibited act (the means of acquiring the rizq), not for the rizq itself. He explains that seeking rizq can be obligatory, recommended, permissible, or forbidden depending on the means, and if one uses forbidden means, he is blameworthy. Thus, the answer distinguishes between the rizq as a divine provision and the moral ruling on the act of acquisition.

Key Terms
رزق
حرام
معتزلة
قبح
ارتزاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That rizq is defined solely by benefit or ownership, and that the Ash'ari view ignores moral distinctions.
Correction: The passage clarifies that rizq is defined by divine attribution and permission for legal disposal, not by benefit, and that the Ash'ari view still holds the Mu'tazila accountable for their flawed principle.

Checkpoint
Which Qur'anic verse does the author use to refute the Mu'tazila claim that a person eating only haram has not been provided by Allah?
A) 'There is no creature on earth but that upon Allah is its provision' (Hud 6)
B) 'And whoever fears Allah, He will make for him a way out' (Talaq 2)
C) 'And eat of the good things We have provided you' (Baqara 172)
D) 'And He provides for him from where he does not expect' (Talaq 3)

Key Takeaway
Rizq is defined by its attribution to Allah, and the Mu'tazila objection fails because the Qur'an affirms all creatures' provision from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive something haram, do you attribute it to Allah's provision or to your own misdeeds? How does this lesson change your response?
?
If you were to die without ever having consumed any halal food, would you still believe that Allah provided for you? What does that say about your reliance on His provision?
?
Is there a forbidden benefit in your life that you justify as 'not provision' because it is haram? How does the Ash'ari definition of rizq challenge that justification and call you to trust Allah's halal provision instead?

Second Objection and Rulings on Seeking Rizq
The author responds to the second Mu'tazila objection and outlines the rulings on seeking rizq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite response to the Mu'tazila claim that if haram were rizq, then preventing it and punishing for it would be unjust, and how the rulings on seeking rizq depend on the means.

Core Idea
Blame and punishment are for the forbidden act of acquisition, not for the provision itself.

The Mu'tazila raise a second objection: if forbidden things are truly provision from Allah, then it would be wrong to prevent them or to punish the one who consumes them. The Ash'arite response distinguishes between the provision and the means by which it is acquired, clarifying that moral rulings apply to the act, not the thing. The passage then outlines the five rulings on seeking rizq based on intention and means.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Mu'tazila objection and the Ash'ari response (denial). Notice the logic: They argue that if haram is rizq, then it cannot be prevented or punished. The response denies the connection.
The key is the distinction between the rizq itself and the act of acquisition.
2
Understand the distinction
The author explains that blame and punishment are only valid if the person is committing a prohibited act. The rizq as provision is not blameworthy; the means matter.
3
Examine the five rulings on seeking rizq
The passage lists rulings: obligatory (need), recommended (expanding), permissible (increase without prohibition), and prohibited (via forbidden means). Identify each case.
The prohibited case includes ghasp (usurpation), sariqa (theft), and riba (usury).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حيث يقال: رزقه الله ولداً صالحاً. وأراد بالعبد ما يشمل البهائم تغليباً، وتفسيره بالملك ليس بمطرد ولا منعكس لدخول ملك الله تعالى وخروج رزق الدواب، بل العبيد والإماء مع الاختلال بما في مفهومه من الإضافة إلى الرازق، اللهم إلا أن يقال المراد المملوك أي المجعول ملكاً بمعنى الإذن في التصرف الشرعي وفيه معنى الإضافة، ولا يشمل ملك الله تعالى ويدخل رزق غير الإنسان بطريق هذا التغليب . لكن لا بد مع من قيد الانتفاع وحينئذ فخروج ملك الله تعالى ظاهر، ومن فسره بالانتفاع أراد المنتفع به، أو أخذ الرزق مصدراً من المبني للمفعول، أي الارتزاق، ولما كان الرزق مضافاً إلى الرازق وهو الله تعالى وحده ، لم يكن الحرام المنتفع به رزقاً عند المعتزلة لقبحه. وقد عرفت فساد أصلهم ، ولزمهم أن من لم يأكل طول عمره إلا الحرام لم يرزقه الله تعالى وهو باطل لقوله تعالى وما من دابة في الأرض إلا على الله رزقها ) . وأجيب بأنه تعالى قد ساق إليه كثيراً من المباح إلا أنه أعرض عنه لسوء اختياره على أنه منقوض بمن مات ولم يأكل حلالاً ولا حراماً، فجوابكم جوابنا. قالوا لو كان الحرام رزقاً لما جاز دفعه عنه ، ولا الذم ولا العقاب عليه . قلنا : ممنوع وإنما يصح لو لم يكن مرتكباً للمنهي عنه مكتسباً للقبح من الفعل سيما في مباشرة الأسباب لأن السعي في تحصيل الرزق قد يجب وذلك عند الحاجة، وقد يستحب، وذلك عند قصد التوسعة على نفسه وعياله، وقد يباح وذلك عند قصد التكثير من غير ارتكاب منهي ، وقد يحرم وذلك عند ارتكاب المنهي كالغصب والسرقة والربا
Where it is said: 'May Allah bless him with a righteous child.' And by 'servant' he intended inclusively of animals by way of predominance, and its interpretation as ownership is neither universal nor reversible because it includes the ownership of Allah the Exalted and excludes the provision of beasts. Rather, male and female slaves are included, with the defect in its concept of relation to the Provider, unless it is said that what is meant is the possessed, i.e., that which is made property in the sense of permission for legal disposal, and in it is the meaning of relation, and it does not include the ownership of Allah the Exalted, and it includes the provision of non-humans through this predominance. But it is necessary with whoever restricted it to benefit, and then the exclusion of Allah's ownership is apparent. And whoever interpreted it as benefit intended the thing benefited from, or took 'rizq' as a verbal noun from the passive construction, i.e., being provided.
