Definition of Si'r and the Ash'ari Position on Pricing
Definition and theological argument on pricing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of si'r, its causes, and the Ash'ari theological stance that pricing is ultimately from Allah alone, in contrast to the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Pricing is ultimately from Allah, not from human acts.

This passage from al-Maqasid defines the term si'r (price) and addresses a key theological dispute with the Mu'tazila. The author argues that while human actions can influence prices, the ultimate determinant is Allah. We will examine the definition, the types of causes, and the supporting hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of si'r
Start by reading the Arabic definition and translation. Note the key terms.
2
Identify the two categories of causes
The passage distinguishes between causes beyond human choice and those involving choice. See how both ultimately refer to Allah.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila argument and refutation
The Mu'tazila claim pricing can come from human acts via tawallud or direct agreement. The author rejects this.
4
Reflect on the hadith evidence
The Prophet's hadith reinforces the Ash'ari position. Consider why he refused to fix prices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس
السعر تقدير ما يباع به الشيء
قال المبحث الخامس السعر تقدير ما يباع به الشيء).
السعر تقدير ما يباع به الشيء ويكون غلاء ورخصاً بأسباب من الله تعالى ولو كان البعض من اكتساب العباد ، فالمسعر هو الله تعالى وحده خلافاً للمعتزلة . طعاماً كان أو غيره ، ويكون غلاء ورخصاً باعتبار الزيادة على المقدار الغالب ذلك المكان والأوان والنقصان عنه ، ويكونان بما لا اختيار فيه للعبد كتقليل ذلك الجنس، وتكثير الرغبات فيه، وبالعكس وبما له فيه اختيار كإخافة السيل، ومنع التبايع ، وادخار الأجناس ومرجعه أيضاً إلى الله تعالى، فالمسعره) هو الله وحده (1) خلافاً للمعتزلة زعماً منهم أنه قد يكون من أفعال العباد تولداً كما مرّ ومباشرة كالمواضعة على تقدير الأثمان .
(أخرج ابن ماجه في كتاب التجارات ٢٧ باب من كره أن يسعر ۲۲۰۰ حدثنا حجاج ثنا حماد بن سلمه عن قتادة، وحميد وثابت عن أنس بن مالك قال : « غلا السعر على عهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فقالوا : يا رسول الله قد علا السعر فسعر لنا . فقال : إن الله هو المسعر القابض، الباسط، الرزاق، إني لأرجو أن ألقى وبي وليس أحد يطلبني بمظلمة في دم ولا مال . . . . .
The Fifth Section: The price is the estimation of what a thing is sold for. He said: 'The fifth section: the price is the estimation of what a thing is sold for.' The price is the estimation of what a thing is sold for, and it is high and low due to causes from Allah the Exalted, even if some of it is from the acquisition of servants. So the one who sets the price (al-musa'ir) is Allah alone, contrary to the Mu'tazila. Whether it be food or other. And it is high and low in consideration of the increase over the usual measure of that place and time and the decrease from it. And they occur by what involves no choice for the servant, such as the reduction of that type (of goods) and the increase of desires for it, and vice versa, and by what involves choice for him, such as frightening with flood, preventing trade, and hoarding types (of goods). And their ultimate reference is also to Allah the Exalted. So the one who sets the price is Allah alone, contrary to the Mu'tazila, in their claim that it may be from the acts of servants by way of generated acts (tawallud) as previously mentioned, or directly (mubashara) like mutual agreement on determining prices. (Ibn Majah narrated in the Book of Trade, chapter 27: Whoever disliked to fix prices, no. 2200: Hajjaj narrated to us, Hammad ibn Salamah narrated to us from Qatada, and Humayd and Thabit from Anas ibn Malik who said: 'Prices became high in the time of the Messenger of Allah, so they said: O Messenger of Allah, prices have become high, so fix prices for us. He said: Indeed Allah is the one who sets prices, the Restrainer, the Expander, the Provider. I hope that I meet Allah with none of you claiming injustice from me regarding blood or wealth...')
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a doctrinal argument concerning si'r (price/pricing). The author first defines si'r as the estimation of what a thing is sold for. He then states that high and low prices occur due to causes from Allah the Exalted, even if some causes involve human acquisition. Therefore, the one who truly sets the price (al-musa'ir) is Allah alone, contrary to the Mu'tazila who claim that pricing can result from human acts. He elaborates that price variations (ghala' and rukhsa) are relative to the usual measure in a given place and time. He distinguishes between causes where the servant has no choice (e.g., scarcity of goods or increased demand) and causes where the servant has choice (e.g., scaring travelers, prohibiting trade, hoarding). Nonetheless, the ultimate reference of all these causes is to Allah. The Mu'tazila argue that pricing can come from human acts either by tawallud (generated acts, as previously discussed) or directly (mubashara) such as mutual agreement on prices. The author rejects this. Finally, he supports his position with a hadith where the Prophet refrained from fixing prices and stated that Allah is the one who sets prices, emphasizing that he wants to meet Allah without any claim of injustice.

Key Terms
سعر
مسعر
غلاء
رخص
تولد
مواضعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari position completely denies any human role in pricing.
Correction: The Ash'aris acknowledge human choice in some causes but affirm that the ultimate determination of prices is from Allah alone.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the main point of disagreement with the Mu'tazila regarding si'r?
A) Whether pricing can be high or low.
B) Whether Allah alone is the one who sets prices.
C) Whether human beings have any choice in pricing causes.
D) Whether the hadith about pricing is authentic.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position is that while human actions can influence prices, the true determiner of pricing is Allah alone, as supported by the Prophet's hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face financial hardship or rising prices, do you truly believe that Allah alone is al-Musa'ir, or do you secretly feel that it's all about market forces and human decisions? What would change in your heart if you fully internalized this truth?
?
The Prophet (ﷺ) refused to fix prices even when asked, fearing injustice. Are there ways you manipulate prices or hoard goods in your own dealings, thinking it's your right to profit regardless of others' needs? How can you repent and trust Allah's provision instead?
?
If every price increase and decrease is ultimately from Allah, then what does your anxiety about money reveal about your tawakkul? Will you commit today to recite 'Allah is al-Musa'ir' whenever price worries strike?

Divine Determination of Price
Al-Taftazani defines price and argues that God alone determines it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani asserts that Allah alone is the ultimate determiner of prices, contrary to the Mu'tazila, and how human actions relate to price fluctuations.

Core Idea
Allah alone is the Price-setter; human actions are secondary causes.

In this passage, al-Taftazani tackles the theological question of price determination. He defines price, explains its fluctuations, and confronts the Mu'tazilite view that humans can independently influence prices. By grounding price in divine causation and citing the Prophet's refusal to fix prices, al-Taftazani reinforces the Ash'ari position on causality and divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the full argument.
Notice the clear structure: definition → explanation → refutation → hadith.
2
Identify the definition of price
Find how al-Taftazani defines price (si'r). Note that it is 'the estimation of what a thing is sold with' and that he links it to divine causes.
The definition is concise and sets the stage.
3
Understand the causes of price fluctuation
The text lists factors within and beyond human control. Distinguish between what the servant has choice in and what he does not. Note that even factors with human choice ultimately return to Allah.
This shows the Ash'ari principle of occasionalism.
4
Examine the refutation of the Mu'tazila
Al-Taftazani explicitly rejects the Mu'tazilite view. Note their two ways price could be from human actions: tawallud (generation) and mubasharah (direct action).
The refutation is concise but clear.
5
Reflect on the hadith evidence
Read the hadith from Ibn Majah. The Prophet refuses to fix prices, declaring Allah alone is the Price-setter. Consider how this serves as a proof for al-Taftazani's point.
The hadith grounds the theological claim in prophetic authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس
السعر تقدير ما يباع به الشيء
السعر تقدير ما يباع به الشيء ويكون غلاء ورخصاً بأسباب من الله تعالى ولو كان البعض من اكتساب العباد، فالمسعر هو الله تعالى وحده خلافاً للمعتزلة. طعاماً كان أو غيره، ويكون غلاء ورخصاً باعتبار الزيادة على المقدار الغالب ذلك المكان والأوان والنقصان عنه، ويكونان بما لا اختيار فيه للعبد كتقليل ذلك الجنس، وتكثير الرغبات فيه، وبالعكس وبما له فيه اختيار كإخافة السيل، ومنع التبايع، وادخار الأجناس ومرجعه أيضاً إلى الله تعالى، فالمسعر هو الله وحده خلافاً للمعتزلة زعماً منهم أنه قد يكون من أفعال العباد تولداً كما مرّ ومباشرة كالمواضعة على تقدير الأثمان.
أخرج ابن ماجه في كتاب التجارات باب من كره أن يسعر حدثنا حجاج ثنا حماد بن سلمه عن قتادة، وحميد وثابت عن أنس بن مالك قال: غلا السعر على عهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، فقالوا: يا رسول الله قد علا السعر فسعر لنا. فقال: إن الله هو المسعر القابض، الباسط، الرزاق، إني لأرجو أن ألقى وبي وليس أحد يطلبني بمظلمة في دم ولا مال.
The fifth topic: Price is the estimation of what a thing is sold with. Price is the estimation of what a thing is sold with, and it becomes high and low due to causes from Allah, even if some of it is from the acquisition of servants. So the Price-setter is Allah alone, contrary to the Mu'tazila. Whether it be food or other than it, and it becomes high and low according to the increase over the usual amount of that place and time and the decrease from it, and they occur by what the servant has no choice in, like the reduction of that species, and the increase of desires for it, and the opposite, and by what he has choice in, like making the path fearful, and preventing mutual trade, and hoarding goods, and its final reference is also to Allah. So the Price-setter is Allah alone, contrary to the Mu'tazila, in their claim that it may be from the actions of the servants by generation (tawallud) as previously mentioned, or directly (mubasharah) like collusion on determining prices. Ibn Majah narrated in the Book of Transactions: Chapter on whoever disliked to fix prices: Hajjaj narrated to us, Hammad ibn Salamah narrated to us from Qatadah, and Humayd, and Thabit, from Anas ibn Malik who said: 'Prices became high during the time of the Messenger of Allah peace be upon him, and they said: O Messenger of Allah, prices have become high, so fix them for us. He said: Indeed Allah is the Price-setter, the Withholder, the Expander, the Provider. I hope that I meet Allah while no one is seeking recompense from me for injustice in blood or wealth.'
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument. Al-Taftazani defines price (si'r) as the estimation of what a thing is sold with. He states that price fluctuations (high and low) occur due to causes from God, even if some human acquisition is involved. Thus, the real price-setter (musi'r) is God alone, contra the Mu'tazila who say price can be from human actions either by generation (tawallud) or directly (mubasharah) like collusion on pricing. The author then elaborates that price variation is relative to the usual amount of the place and time, and can be due to factors within the servant's control (like creating fear on the path, preventing trade, hoarding) and factors beyond (like reduction of the commodity and increase of desires). All ultimately go back to God. He then cites the hadith of Anas where the Prophet refused to fix prices, declaring that only God is the Price-setter, and noting that he wished to meet God with no one seeking recourse for injustice in blood or wealth.

Key Terms
سعر
مسعر
غلاء
رخص
تقدير
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that human market forces alone determine prices independently of God.
Correction: Al-Taftazani asserts that while human actions (such as hoarding or creating fear) can influence prices, these are secondary causes that ultimately depend on Allah. The Price-setter (مُسعِّر) is Allah alone.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, what is the primary cause of price fluctuations?
A) Human supply and demand
B) The actions of merchants
C) Causes from Allah
D) The decree of the ruler

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani establishes that Allah alone is the ultimate determiner of prices, while human actions are merely secondary causes that ultimately depend on divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a sudden price hike, do you truly believe that Allah is the Price-setter, or do you find yourself blaming shopkeepers, the government, or the economy? How does this affect your reliance on Allah alone?
?
The Prophet (peace be upon him) refused to fix prices despite the people's request. Is there an area in your life where you are trying to force a solution or control an outcome, rather than trusting Allah's wisdom and provision? What step can you take today to surrender that control?
?
The text says price fluctuations occur by causes from Allah, even if human actions are involved. Think of a recent financial worry. Did you see Allah’s hand in it, or did you only see people? How can you train your heart to look beyond secondary causes to the First Cause?

Mu'tazila Doctrine of Obligatory Grace (Lutf) and Its Refutation
Al-Taftazani expounds and refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God must provide lutf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila concept of lutf, their proofs that God must provide it, and the Sunni refutation as presented by al-Taftazani.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila claimed God must grant grace (lutf) to guide humans, but this leads to contradictions with divine predestination.

This lesson presents the Mu'tazila doctrine of obligatory grace (lutf) and the refutation by al-Taftazani. Lutf is defined as an action that draws the servant closer to obedience without compulsion. The Mu'tazila offered several proofs for its obligation, but these are countered with strong objections from within their own principles and from the reality of divine decree. By the end, you'll see why the Sunni position rejects any obligation upon God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage starts with the Mu'tazila claim, then gives proofs, then refutation, then a comment.
2
Understand the definition of lutf
Identify how al-Taftazani defines lutf: an act that draws near to obedience or brings it about, without compulsion. Also note the two types: muqarrib and muhassil.
Examples include provisions, intellect, proofs.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila proofs
The passage lists three proofs: (1) preventing what draws near would contradict divine will, (2) preventing lutf violates purpose, (3) the obligatory cannot be completed without it. See how each one is constructed.
These proofs are based on considering God's actions as purposive.
4
Analyze the refutation and Sunni stance
Al-Taftazani dismisses the proofs as weak and offers two objections: if lutf were obligatory to the fullest, no disbeliever would exist; also, God's predestination would be undermined. Then he notes that Sunnis reject any obligation on God, sparing lengthy debates.
The refutation uses the Mu'tazila's own principles against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس
ما يجب على الله تعالى في رأى المعتزلة
ذهب المعتزلة إلى أنه يجب على الله تعالى أمور:
الأول : اللطف وهو فعل يقرب إلى الطاعة أو يحصلها، ويبعده عن المعصية لا إلى حد الإلجاء، واستدلوا بأنه لو جاز منع ما يقرب إلى المأمور به لكان غير مراد وهو تناقض وبأن منع اللطف نقض للغرض، وتقريب أو تحصيل فيقبح، و بأن الواجب لا يتم إلا بما يحصله أو يقرب منه فيجب، والكل ضعيف ومعارض بأنه من قواعدكم أن أقصى اللطف واجب . فيلزم أن لا يبقى كافر ولا فاسق، وبأنه لو وجب لما أخبر الله تعالى بسعادة البعض وشقاوة البعض لكونه إقناطاً وإغراء ولما خلا عصر من الأنبياء والأولياء والخلفاء.
لما لم تقل بوجوب شيء على الله كفينا مؤونة كثير من تطويلات المعتزلة القائلين بوجوب أشياء على الله تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً. وقد أكثروا الكلام في تفاصيلها ولنعد منها عدة :
الأول : اللطف وهو فعل يقرب العبد إلى الطاعة ويبعده عن المعصية لا إلى حد الإلجاء، ويسمى اللطف المقرب، أو يحصل الطاعة فيه، ويسمى المحصل، وذلك كالأرزاق والآجال والقوى والآلات وإكمال العقل، ونصب الأدلة، وما يشبه ذلك
وفسروا الوجوب عليه بأنه لا بد أن يفعله لقيام الداعي، وانتفاء الصارف وتارة بأن لتركه مدخلاً في استحقاق الذم وقد عرفت ما فيه، واستدلوا على الوجوب بوجوه :
The sixth topic: What is obligatory upon Allah in the view of the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila hold that matters are obligatory upon Allah: The first: al-lutf (grace/kindness), which is an action that draws closer to obedience or brings it about, and distances from disobedience, but not to the point of compulsion (ilja'). They argued that if it were permissible to prevent what draws closer to the commanded, then it would be not willed, which is a contradiction; and that preventing lutf is a violation of purpose, and drawing closer or causing to obtain is good; and that the obligatory is not completed except by what brings it about or draws close to it, so it is obligatory. All of this is weak and is opposed: by the fact that according to your principles, the utmost lutf is obligatory, then it would follow that no disbeliever or sinner remains; and that if it were obligatory, Allah would not have informed of the happiness of some and the misery of some, because that would be discouraging and inciting, and no era would be free of Prophets, saints, and caliphs. Since you (the author says) do not assert the obligation of anything upon Allah, we are spared the burden of many of the lengthy arguments of the Mu'tazila who assert the obligation of things upon Allah—exalted is He high above that. They spoke extensively about its details, so let us count several: The first: al-lutf, which is an action that draws the servant closer to obedience and distances from disobedience not to the point of compulsion, and it is called al-lutf al-muqarrib (drawing-near grace), or it brings about obedience therein, and is called al-muhassil (effecting), such as provisions, terms, powers, instruments, completion of intellect, establishing proofs, and the like. They interpreted the obligation upon Him as that He must do it due to the presence of the motive (al-da'i) and the absence of the deterrent (al-sarif), and sometimes as that leaving it has a role in deserving blame—and you already know what is in that. And they argued for the obligation with aspects:
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila doctrine of lutf and refutes it. Al-Taftazani first states that the Mu'tazila assert that certain things are obligatory upon God, the first being lutf. He defines lutf as an act that brings the servant closer to obedience without compulsion (ilja'), and provides their proofs: if preventing lutf were allowed, God would not be willing it, which is inconsistency; preventing it violates purpose; the obligatory cannot be completed without it. Then the author dismisses all these as weak and counters with two objections: if lutf were obligatory in full (the utmost lutf), then no disbeliever would exist; also, if it were obligatory, God would not inform of some people's predestination to happiness or misery (as that would be discouraging), and there would always be prophets and saints in every era. Then the author remarks that since Ahl al-Sunna do not hold any obligation upon God, they are spared these lengthy debates. Nonetheless, he elaborates the Mu'tazila view: lutf is of two types: muqarrib (drawing near) and muhassil (effecting), with examples such as provisions, terms, powers, intellect, proofs. They interpret obligation either as necessitated by motive (da'i) and absence of deterrent (sarif) or as leaving it entails blame. The text ends with them beginning to present their proofs.

Key Terms
لطف
وجوب
إلجاء
تقريب
تحصيل
داع
صارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God must treat all creatures equally by providing grace (lutf) to guide everyone to obedience.
Correction: The refutation shows that such an obligation would eliminate free will and contradict divine predestination (qadar), since in reality not all people believe.

Checkpoint
What is the first thing the Mu'tazila claim is obligatory upon God?
A) Al-Ilja' (compulsion)
B) Al-Lutf (grace)
C) Al-Ajal (term)
D) Al-Mas'uliyya

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila doctrine of obligatory grace (lutf) is refuted on the grounds that it would contradict divine decree and the reality of human disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever take God's guidance for granted, assuming He must make faith easy for you, just as the Mu'tazila argued He must provide grace? Have you thanked Him for the small openings He has given you to obey, or do you demand more?
?
If you truly believed that the existence of disbelievers is a direct refutation of any supposed 'obligation' on God to guide everyone, would that change how you view the disobedience in your own life? Are you making excuses for your sins by expecting God to 'force' you to obey?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed God's motive (da'i) obligates Him to act. In what areas of your life are you acting as if God owes you something? Will you today surrender the expectation that God must treat you a certain way, and instead humbly accept His decree for you?

Mu'tazilite Disagreements on Compensations
Lists disagreements on compensations and states the obligation principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key points of disagreement among Mu'tazilite scholars regarding compensations (a'wad) and the rational principle that makes compensation obligatory.

Core Idea
Compensation is obligatory because omitting it would be unjust.

The Mu'tazilites were known for their rational theology, but they did not always agree on every detail. One area of internal debate was the nature and necessity of compensations (a'wad) that God provides for harms suffered by His creatures. This passage lists several points of disagreement among them, then grounds the entire discussion in a fundamental principle: compensation must be given because withholding it would be injustice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the list of disagreements and the concluding principle.
The passage uses a very compact style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the points of disagreement
Go through the text and list each point of disagreement mentioned. There are several items. Pay attention to phrases like 'واختلفوا في' and 'وفي' that introduce new topics.
The disagreements cover perpetuity, knowledge, location of compensation, nullification by sins, etc.
3
Understand the main principle
After the list, the passage states a general rule: compensation is deserved for pains caused by God, and its omission would be ugly (qabih) because it is injustice (zulm). Grasp this reasoning.
This principle is what makes compensation obligatory according to the Mu'tazilites.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about how this principle might shape Mu'tazilite theology. Why would they consider it unjust for God not to compensate? How does this relate to their concept of divine justice?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه، واختلفوا في لزوم دوام العوض، وفي لزوم العلم عند الإبقاء بكونه حقه، وفي جواز التفضل بقضاء عوض المظلوم عن الظالم وفي وجوب كون العوض في الآخرة، وفي حبوطه بالذنوب، وفي جواز التفضل بمثل الأعواض من غير سبق الألم واضطربوا في أن أعواض آلام الكفار والفساق وغير العاقل كالصبيان والبهائم تكون في الدنيا أم في الآخرة وفي أن البهائم هل تدخل الجنة، وهل يخلق فيها العلم؟). يجري يستحق في مقابلة ما يفعل الله تعالى بالعبد من الأسقام والالام وما مجرى ذلك فيخرج الأجر والثواب لكونهما للتعظيم في مقابلة فعل العبد، وكذا النفع المتفضل به لكونه غير مستحق، ووجه وجوبه على الإطلاق أن تركه قبيح لكونه ظلماً، فيجب فعله.
And they disagreed regarding the necessity of perpetuity of the compensation, and regarding the necessity of knowledge at the time of preservation that it is his right, and regarding the permissibility of bestowal by paying the compensation of the oppressed from the oppressor, and regarding the obligation that the compensation be in the Hereafter, and regarding its nullification by sins, and regarding the permissibility of bestowal of the like of compensations without prior pain. And they disagreed concerning whether the compensations for the pains of disbelievers, sinners, and non-rational beings like children and animals are in this world or the Hereafter, and concerning whether animals enter Paradise, and whether knowledge is created in them? (It runs:?) It is deserved in exchange for what God the Exalted does to the servant of illnesses and pains and the like of that, so reward and recompense are excluded because they are for honor in exchange for the servant's action, and likewise the benefit bestowed gratuitously because it is not deserved. And the aspect of its obligation absolutely is that its omission is ugly because it is injustice, so it must be done.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of points of disagreement among the Mu'tazilites regarding compensations (a'wad) and a statement of the general principle that compensation is obligatory. The text lists several disagreements: whether compensation must be perpetual, whether knowledge of it being one's right is necessary, whether it is permissible for God to bestow by paying the oppressed's compensation from the oppressor, whether compensation is obligatory in the Hereafter, whether it is nullified by sins, and whether compensations can be given without prior pain. It also notes disagreement on whether compensations for pains of disbelievers, sinners, children, and animals occur in this world or the Hereafter, and whether animals enter Paradise and if knowledge is created in them. Then the text explains that compensation is deserved in exchange for what God does to the servant, such as illnesses and pains, but reward and recompense are excluded because they are for honor in exchange for the servant's action, and gratuitous benefit is excluded because it is not deserved. The reason compensation is absolutely obligatory is that leaving it would be ugly (qabih) because it would be injustice (zulm), so it must be done.

Key Terms
عوض
ظلم
ألم
ثواب
قبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all Mu'tazilites held identical views on compensations.
Correction: In fact, they had many detailed disagreements as listed in the passage.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is compensation absolutely obligatory?
A) Because it is a command of God.
B) Because leaving it would be unjust.
C) Because the servant deserves it inherently.
D) Because it is for honor.

Key Takeaway
Compensation for harms caused by God is obligatory because omitting it would be injustice (zulm), which is rationally ugly (qabih).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face illness or pain, do you ever feel that God owes you something in return? If so, why? Does this lesson challenge you to examine whether your expectation is based on a sense of entitlement rather than gratitude?
?
The Mu'tazilites insisted that God must compensate to avoid injustice. In contrast, the Ash'aris emphasize God's absolute sovereignty. Which view aligns more with your heart when you suffer? Are you comfortable with a God who is not obligated to recompense you?
?
Think of a time you were harmed by someone. Did you demand compensation? How does your standard for humans compare to your expectation from God? Is there a double standard that reveals something about your understanding of divine justice?

Grounds for Claiming Compensation from God
Lists five grounds for compensation from God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific grounds on which Mu'tazilites hold that compensation is due from God, including pains, loss of benefits, griefs, commands to harm, and enabling harm.

Core Idea
Compensation from God is due in five specific cases, each with distinct conditions.

The Mu'tazilites developed a detailed theology of divine justice, including the idea that God owes compensation for certain harms. In this passage, they enumerate the situations where compensation is deserved from God. Each case is carefully defined and contrasted with cases where compensation falls on humans.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five grounds
Read the passage to pick out each ground listed. Notice that they range from direct infliction of pain to enabling non-rational beings.
2
Understand the role of knowledge
Note the distinction between grief based on knowledge (divine cause) and grief based on compound ignorance (human cause).
3
Differentiate natural from legal causation
Examine the distinction between pain from natural custom (e.g., fire) where compensation is on the human agent, and pain from false testimony where it is on the witnesses.
This shows that divine causation is not always directly involved.
4
Reflect on enabling oppression
Consider why enabling an oppressor is a ground for compensation from God, and why retaliation is obligatory on Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا ويستحق على الله تعالى بإنزاله الالام على العبد، وبتفويته المنافع عليه لمصلحة الغير عليه كالزكاة وبإنزاله الغموم التي لا تستند إلى فعل العبد كالغم المستند إلى علم ضروري أو مكتسب أو ظن بوصول مضرة أو فوات منفعة، بخلاف المستند إلى جهل مركب لأنه من العبد وبأمر العباد بالمضار كالذبح لمثل الهدي والنذر أو إباحته إياها كالصيد أو تمكينه غير العاقل كالوحوش والسباع من غير إضرار العباد لا بمثل ألم الاحتراق حين ألقي في النار ، وألم القتل بشهادة الزور. لأن الأول مما وجب طبعاً بخلق الله تعالى ذلك العادة، فالعوض على الملقى، والثاني مما وجب شرعاً بفعل صبي في بطريق جري الشهود فعليهم العوض، وأما في تمكين الظالم من الظلم فالعوض على الله تعالى فإن الانتصاف واجب عليه قالوا فإن كان المظلوم من أهل الجنة فرق الله تعالى أعواضه الموازنة بظلم الظالم على الأوقات المتتالية على وجه لا يتبين انقطاعها، كي لا يتألم أو يتفضل الله عليه بمثل تلك الأعواض عقيب انقطاعها، فلا يتألم . وإن كان من أهل النار أسقط الله تعالى بأعواضه جزءاً من عقابه بحيث لا يظهر له التخفيف، وذلك بأن يفرق القدر المسقط على الأوقات المتتالية لئلا ينقطع ألمه .
They said: And compensation is deserved from God the Exalted by His sending down pains upon the servant, and by His causing the loss of benefits upon him for the benefit of another, like the zakat, and by His sending down griefs that are not based on the servant's action, like the grief based on necessary or acquired knowledge or conjecture of the arrival of harm or loss of benefit, contrary to that based on compound ignorance because it is from the servant, and by His commanding the servants with harms like slaughter for the likes of the sacrificial animal and votive offering, or permitting them like hunting, or enabling non-rational beings like wild animals and beasts without harming the servants, not like the pain of burning when thrown into the fire, and the pain of killing by false testimony. For the first is something necessitated naturally by God's creation of that custom, so the compensation is upon the one who threw, and the second is something necessitated legally by the action of a child in the way of the testimony's course, so upon them is the compensation. As for enabling the oppressor to oppress, the compensation is upon God the Exalted, for retaliation is obligatory upon Him. They said: If the oppressed is among the people of Paradise, God the Exalted distributes his compensations equivalent to the oppression of the oppressor over successive times in a manner that their interruption is not perceived, so that he does not suffer, or God bestows upon him the like of those compensations after their interruption, so he does not suffer. And if he is among the people of Hellfire, God the Exalted drops by his compensations a part of his punishment such that alleviation does not appear to him, and that is by distributing the dropped amount over successive times so that his pain does not cease.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage details the grounds on which the Mu'tazilites hold that compensation is deserved from God. It lists several cases: (1) When God directly inflicts pains on the servant. (2) When God causes loss of benefits for the sake of another, like zakat. (3) When God sends griefs that are not due to the servant's action—specifically grief based on certain knowledge or conjecture of harm or loss, as opposed to compound ignorance which is from the servant. (4) When God commands servants to cause harm, like slaughter for sacrifice, or permits it, like hunting. (5) When God enables non-rational beings like wild animals without harming humans. The text distinguishes these from cases where the pain is due to a natural custom (like burning from fire) where compensation is on the thrower, or legal necessity (like a child's action in testimony) where compensation is on the witnesses. In the case of enabling an oppressor, compensation is on God because retaliation is obligatory. The passage then explains how compensations are distributed in the afterlife: for the oppressed in Paradise, God distributes the compensations over time so that the suffering is not perceived, or bestows like compensations after that; for the oppressed in Hellfire, God reduces a portion of punishment by distributing the reduction over time so that the pain does not cease entirely but is alleviated.

Key Terms
عوض
غم
تمكين
انتصاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Compensation is only due for physical pain.
Correction: The passage includes grief, loss of benefits, and enabling harm as grounds for compensation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a ground for compensation from God?
A) Inflicting pains
B) Causing loss of benefits
C) Sending griefs based on conjecture
D) Enabling the sun to shine

Key Takeaway
Compensation from God is due for five specific types of harm, with clear distinctions between divine and human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a deep grief or pain, do you immediately assume it is from God and expect compensation, or do you search for your own role in causing it? This lesson distinguishes griefs from God from those arising from your own ignorance—where do your sufferings fall?
?
The passage says compensation for enabling an oppressor falls on God. Have you ever stood by while someone was wronged? According to this, God bears the compensation, but what is your obligation toward that wrong? Does knowing God will compensate relieve you of responsibility?
?
If you were the one who caused harm to another, like the witnesses in false testimony, this lesson places the compensation on you. Are there harms you have caused that you have not yet sought to rectify? How does this teaching about human-compensated harms change your view of your own actions?

Distribution of Compensations in the Afterlife
Explains distribution of compensations in Paradise and Hellfire

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how compensations are distributed in the afterlife for the people of Paradise and the people of Hellfire, according to the Mu'tazilites.

Core Idea
To avoid continued suffering, God distributes compensations over time or uses them to reduce punishment without full alleviation.

After establishing the grounds for compensation, the Mu'tazilites explain how God fulfills these obligations in the afterlife. The method differs for believers and disbelievers, ensuring that justice is done without causing additional pain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on distribution
Carefully read the part describing how compensation is given in Paradise and Hellfire.
2
Understand the Paradise scenario
For the people of Paradise, God spreads compensations over time so that no interruption is perceived, or grants additional favors after their cessation.
This prevents the psychological pain of loss.
3
Understand the Hellfire scenario
For the people of Hellfire, God uses compensations to reduce a portion of punishment, but distributes that reduction over time so that the person does not feel relief and his pain continues.
4
Compare the two mechanisms
Notice that in both cases, the distribution over time is key to avoiding the perception of change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا ويستحق على الله تعالى بإنزاله الالام على العبد، وبتفويته المنافع عليه لمصلحة الغير عليه كالزكاة وبإنزاله الغموم التي لا تستند إلى فعل العبد كالغم المستند إلى علم ضروري أو مكتسب أو ظن بوصول مضرة أو فوات منفعة، بخلاف المستند إلى جهل مركب لأنه من العبد وبأمر العباد بالمضار كالذبح لمثل الهدي والنذر أو إباحته إياها كالصيد أو تمكينه غير العاقل كالوحوش والسباع من غير إضرار العباد لا بمثل ألم الاحتراق حين ألقي في النار ، وألم القتل بشهادة الزور. لأن الأول مما وجب طبعاً بخلق الله تعالى ذلك العادة، فالعوض على الملقى، والثاني مما وجب شرعاً بفعل صبي في بطريق جري الشهود فعليهم العوض، وأما في تمكين الظالم من الظلم فالعوض على الله تعالى فإن الانتصاف واجب عليه قالوا فإن كان المظلوم من أهل الجنة فرق الله تعالى أعواضه الموازنة بظلم الظالم على الأوقات المتتالية على وجه لا يتبين انقطاعها، كي لا يتألم أو يتفضل الله عليه بمثل تلك الأعواض عقيب انقطاعها، فلا يتألم . وإن كان من أهل النار أسقط الله تعالى بأعواضه جزءاً من عقابه بحيث لا يظهر له التخفيف، وذلك بأن يفرق القدر المسقط على الأوقات المتتالية لئلا ينقطع ألمه .
They said: And compensation is deserved from God the Exalted by His sending down pains upon the servant, and by His causing the loss of benefits upon him for the benefit of another, like the zakat, and by His sending down griefs that are not based on the servant's action, like the grief based on necessary or acquired knowledge or conjecture of the arrival of harm or loss of benefit, contrary to that based on compound ignorance because it is from the servant, and by His commanding the servants with harms like slaughter for the likes of the sacrificial animal and votive offering, or permitting them like hunting, or enabling non-rational beings like wild animals and beasts without harming the servants, not like the pain of burning when thrown into the fire, and the pain of killing by false testimony. For the first is something necessitated naturally by God's creation of that custom, so the compensation is upon the one who threw, and the second is something necessitated legally by the action of a child in the way of the testimony's course, so upon them is the compensation. As for enabling the oppressor to oppress, the compensation is upon God the Exalted, for retaliation is obligatory upon Him. They said: If the oppressed is among the people of Paradise, God the Exalted distributes his compensations equivalent to the oppression of the oppressor over successive times in a manner that their interruption is not perceived, so that he does not suffer, or God bestows upon him the like of those compensations after their interruption, so he does not suffer. And if he is among the people of Hellfire, God the Exalted drops by his compensations a part of his punishment such that alleviation does not appear to him, and that is by distributing the dropped amount over successive times so that his pain does not cease.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage details the grounds on which the Mu'tazilites hold that compensation is deserved from God. It lists several cases: (1) When God directly inflicts pains on the servant. (2) When God causes loss of benefits for the sake of another, like zakat. (3) When God sends griefs that are not due to the servant's action—specifically grief based on certain knowledge or conjecture of harm or loss, as opposed to compound ignorance which is from the servant. (4) When God commands servants to cause harm, like slaughter for sacrifice, or permits it, like hunting. (5) When God enables non-rational beings like wild animals without harming humans. The text distinguishes these from cases where the pain is due to a natural custom (like burning from fire) where compensation is on the thrower, or legal necessity (like a child's action in testimony) where compensation is on the witnesses. In the case of enabling an oppressor, compensation is on God because retaliation is obligatory. The passage then explains how compensations are distributed in the afterlife: for the oppressed in Paradise, God distributes the compensations over time so that the suffering is not perceived, or bestows like compensations after that; for the oppressed in Hellfire, God reduces a portion of punishment by distributing the reduction over time so that the pain does not cease entirely but is alleviated.

Key Terms
عوض
انتصاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Compensations are given as a lump sum at once.
Correction: The passage states they are distributed over successive times to avoid perceived interruption.

Checkpoint
For a person destined for Hellfire, how does God handle compensation?
A) He gives full compensation after punishment ends.
B) He reduces punishment by distributing compensation over time so that pain continues.
C) He cancels all punishment.
D) He gives compensation in this world.

Key Takeaway
God distributes compensations over time to prevent perceiving the end of suffering or to reduce punishment without full alleviation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God's justice was slow or incomplete, expecting immediate compensation for a wrong done to you? How does the distribution of compensations over successive times call you to trust His perfect timing, even when you cannot see the end? What is one specific grievance you need to surrender to His wisdom today?
?
If you are among the people of Paradise, God's mercy ensures you never perceive the end of His blessings. Are you living now with a constant awareness that every moment of ease is a continuous gift from Him, or do you take it for granted? What would change in your daily gratitude if you truly believed no good thing ever ceases for the believer?
?
For the people of Hellfire, the reduction of punishment is made imperceptible so that pain does not cease. Does this sobering aspect of divine justice make you more aware of your need for repentance? In what specific sin are you presuming upon God's mercy, forgetting that even a reduced punishment involves perpetual suffering? What step will you take today to turn back to Him with sincerity?

The Mu'tazilite Definition of Injustice
Mu'tazilite definition of injustice with excluded cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite definition of injustice (zulm) and the key exceptions that distinguish it from all pain.

Core Idea
Injustice is undeserved, non-beneficial, non-customary harm.

The Mu'tazilites developed a precise definition of injustice to clarify what actions are morally evil and attributable to God. This passage lays out the criteria that must be met for an act to be considered unjust, and lists several examples of pain that are excluded. By understanding this definition, you will grasp a key principle of Mu'tazilite theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage that defines zulm. Notice the three conditions: harm must be undeserved, without benefit or repulsion of harm, and not customary.
The definition uses negation: 'لا يشتمل على نفع... ولا يكون دفعه نفسه...'
2
Identify the excluded cases
The passage lists five specific types of pain that are not zulm. Find each one and understand why it is excluded according to the definition.
For example, punishment is excluded because it is deserved.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that pain meeting any of the exception criteria is not injustice but is good and permissible from God without compensation.
This conclusion is important for theodicy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفسروا الظلم بضرر غير مستحق لا يشتمل على نفع أو دفع ضرر معلوم أو مظنون، ولا يكون دفعه نفسه ولا مفعولاً بطريق جري العادة، فخرج العقاب ومشقة السفر والحجامة ودفع الصائل، وإحراق الله تعالى الصبي الملقى في النار. فإن الإيلام إذا كان مستحقاً أو مشتملاً على نفع أو دفع ضر، وعادياً لا يكون ظلماً ، بل يكون حسناً يجوز صدوره عن الله تعالى من غير عوض عليه . ثم للمعتزلة في بحث الآلام والأعواض فروع واختلافات لا بأس بذكر بعضها منها : أن الألم إذا وقع جزاء لسيئة فهي عقوبة لا عوض عليها، وإن لم يقع فإن كان من الله تعالى وجب العوض عليه، وإن كان مكلفاً فإن كان له حسنات أخذ الله حسناته ، وأعطاها المؤلم عوضاً لإيلامه، وإن لم يكن له حسنات فعلى الله العوض من عنده حيث مكن الظالم ولم يصرفه عن الإيلام. فالواجب قبل الوقوع إما الصرف وإما التزام العوض، وإن كان من غير عاقل كالأطفال والوحوش والسباع . فإن كان ملجأ إليه بسبب من الله تعالى كجوع وخوف ونحوهما : فالعوض على الله تعالى. وإلا فعلى المؤلم عند القاضي، وعلى الله تعالى عند أبي علي لأن التمكين وعدم المنع بعلم أو نهي إغراء على إيصال تلك المضار. فأخذ العوض منها يكون ظلماً بمنزلة من ألقى الطعام إلى كلب فأكله، ثم أخذ يضربه، وللقاضي ما ورد في الحديث من أنه : «يأخذ للجماء من القرناء » وما ثبت في الشرع من وجوب منعها
And they interpreted injustice as an undeserved harm that does not include benefit or repulsion of known or conjectured harm, and is not its own repulsion nor an effect by way of custom. Thus excluded are punishment, hardship of travel, cupping, repelling an assailant, and God's burning of the child thrown into the fire. For inflicting pain, if it is deserved or includes benefit or repulsion of harm, and is customary, is not injustice, but is good, permissible to occur from God the Exalted without compensation upon Him. Then for the Mu'tazilites, in the discussion of pains and compensations, there are branches and disagreements, and it is not objectionable to mention some of them: That pain, if it occurs as a recompense for an evil deed, then it is punishment without compensation. If it does not occur, then if it is from God the Exalted, compensation is obligatory upon Him. If it is from a morally responsible being, then if he has good deeds, God takes his good deeds and gives them to the one who inflicted pain as compensation for his infliction. If he has no good deeds, then compensation is upon God from His own, since He enabled the oppressor and did not prevent him from inflicting pain. So the obligation before occurrence is either prevention or undertaking compensation. And if the pain is from a non-rational being like children, wild animals, and beasts, then if it is compelled by a cause from God like hunger or fear and the like, compensation is upon God. Otherwise, it is upon the one who inflicts pain according to al-Qadi, and upon God according to Abu Ali, because enabling and not preventing by knowledge or prohibition is incitement to deliver those harms. So taking compensation from them would be injustice, like one who throws food to a dog and it eats it, then he beats it. And for al-Qadi, there is what is reported in the hadith: 'He takes retribution for the hornless from the horned,' and what is established in the law of the obligation to prevent them.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage defines injustice (zulm) according to the Mu'tazilites and then presents further branches of their discussion on pains and compensations. The definition: injustice is a harm that is undeserved, does not include benefit or repulsion of known or conjectured harm, and is not itself a repulsion of harm nor an effect by way of custom. This excludes punishment (since it is deserved), hardship of travel and cupping (since they involve benefit), repelling an assailant (since it is repulsion of harm), and God's burning of the child thrown into the fire (since it is a customary effect). Thus inflicting pain, if it is deserved or involves benefit or repulsion of harm, or is customary, is not injustice but is good and permissible from God without compensation. Then the text lists branches: (1) If pain occurs as punishment for an evil deed, there is no compensation. (2) If it occurs from God directly, compensation is obligatory upon Him. (3) If the inflicter is a morally responsible being, then if he has good deeds, God takes those and gives them as compensation to the sufferer; if not, compensation is upon God because He enabled the oppressor. Therefore, before the pain occurs, the obligation is either prevention or undertaking compensation. (4) If the inflicter is non-rational (children, animals), then if the action is compelled by a cause from God (e.g., hunger, fear), compensation is on God; otherwise, according to al-Qadi, it is on the inflicter, but according to Abu Ali, on God because enabling is incitement. Taking compensation from the non-rational being would be unjust, analogous to feeding a dog and then beating it. Al-Qadi supports his view with the hadith about retribution for the hornless from the horned and the legal obligation to prevent harm.

Key Terms
ظلم
ضرر
إيلام
عوض
حسن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All pain is considered unjust and evil.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites exclude deserved punishment, beneficial pain, pain that repels harm, and customary effects from the definition of injustice. Such pain is not unjust but good.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite definition in this passage, which of the following is NOT considered injustice (zulm)?
A) An undeserved harm with no benefit.
B) Punishment for an evil deed.
C) Harm inflicted by a non-rational being without cause from God.
D) Harm that does not include benefit or repulsion of harm.

Key Takeaway
Injustice is a specific type of harm: undeserved, without benefit or repulsion of harm, and not customary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you suffered deeply and felt God was unjust. According to the Mu'tazilite definition, what if that pain was actually beneficial or a natural consequence? Would you be willing to re-examine that experience and see it as good rather than unjust? What would that require you to let go of?
?
When you face a hardship like the 'child thrown into the fire'—a painful but ordinary event—do you instinctively blame God or call it unfair? Are you ready to accept that such pain is not injustice but simply the way the world works under God's decree? What would change in your relationship with God if you did?
?
Reflect on a deserved punishment you received. Did you resent it or accept it as just? This lesson says deserved harm is not injustice but good. How does this challenge your pride and sense of entitlement? Will you now thank God for the discipline, even if it hurt?

Branches on Pains and Compensations
Mu'tazilite branches on pains and compensations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite branches regarding when compensation for pain is obligatory and on whom responsibility falls.

Core Idea
Compensation for pain depends on its source: punishment, divine action, moral agent, or non-rational being.

After defining injustice, the Mu'tazilites explored various scenarios involving pain and compensation. This passage outlines several branches: pain as punishment, pain from God, from a morally responsible being, and from non-rational beings. Each has different rulings on compensation, and there is a notable disagreement between al-Qadi and Abu Ali regarding pain from non-rational beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the branches list
Read the passage that enumerates the branches. Notice the different categories: pain as punishment, pain from God, pain from a mukallaf, and pain from non-rational beings.
The passage uses conditional statements: 'إذا وقع... وإن لم يقع...'
2
Analyze each branch
For each category, determine whether compensation is required and on whom it falls.
For pain as punishment, no compensation. For pain from God, compensation on God. For pain from a mukallaf, compensation may be from his good deeds or from God. For non-rational beings, there is a difference of opinion.
3
Understand the dispute
Focus on the last branch: pain from non-rational beings. Compare the views of al-Qadi and Abu Ali, and understand their arguments.
Al-Qadi says compensation on the inflicter; Abu Ali says on God because enabling is incitement.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The passage gives an analogy of feeding a dog and then beating it. Consider how this illustrates why taking compensation from non-rational beings would be unjust.
The hadith about retribution for the hornless is also cited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception: All pain deserves compensation from the one who caused it.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite branches in this passage, what is the ruling when pain is inflicted by a non-rational being and is not compelled by a cause from God?
A) Compensation is always upon God.
B) Compensation is upon the inflicter according to al-Qadi, and upon God according to Abu Ali.
C) No compensation is required.
D) Compensation is taken from the non-rational being itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that if you have good deeds, God can take them to compensate someone you have harmed. Have you ever considered that your good deeds might be transferred to others because of your sins? How does this make you feel about the weight of your actions? Will you apologize to someone you have wronged today, even if you think your good deeds might cover it?
?
This passage states that God enables oppressors and does not prevent them, and that He then compensates the victim. When you see injustice in the world, do you find it hard to believe God is just? How does this Mu'tazilite framework help you trust that God will make things right? What can you do to actively stand against oppression rather than passively wait for divine compensation?
?
The hadith mentioned says God will take retribution for the hornless animal from the horned on the Day of Judgment. Do you believe that God's justice extends to animals? If so, does this change how you treat animals in your daily life—for example, what you eat, how you dispose of waste, or how you interact with stray animals? Write one concrete action you will take this week to treat animals with more care.

Pain Caused by God Must Be Compensated
First branch on compensation for pain caused by God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why pain caused by God (e.g., using beasts, slaughtering, or allowing oppression) must be compensated by God due to His transcendence above injustice.

Core Idea
God's justice demands compensation for any pain He commands.

This lesson begins the Mu'tazilite discussion on compensations. The first branch deals with pain that occurs by God's direct command, permission, or enabling. Since God is transcendent above injustice, He must compensate the one who suffers such pain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the claim that compensation is upon God due to His transcendence above injustice.
2
Identify the types of pain
Notice the three scenarios: pain by command, by permission, and by enabling with delay of retribution.
3
Understand the rationale
The basis is God's transcendence (ta'ālī) above injustice. Therefore, He must provide compensation (ʿiwaḍ) for the pain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن الإيلام بأمر الله كما في استعمال البهائم أو بإباحته كما في ذبحها أو بتمكينه مع تأخير الانتصاف إلى دار الجزاء كما في المظلوم عوضه على الله تعالى لتعاليه عن الظلم ومنها أنه إذا استوى لذة وألم في كونهما لطفاً، فالجمهور على أنه يتعين اللذة، ويقبح الألم. لأنه إنما يحسن إذا تعين طريقاً للعوض واللطف وقال أبو هاشم: بل يتخير منهما كما بين المنفعتين لأن الإيلام بكونه عوضاً ولطفاً قد خرج عن كونه عبثاً وظلماً، ومنها أن العوض يستحق دائماً عند أبي علي كالثواب. إذ لو انقطع لاغتم بانقطاعه فثبت عوض آخر وهلم جراً، ومنقطعاً عند أبي هاشم إذ لو شرط الدوام لما حسن بدونه واللازم باطل لأن العقلاء قد يستحسنون الالام لمنافع منقطعة، ومنها أنهم اختلفوا في أنه هل يشترط عند إبقاء العوض علم المعوض بأنه حقه كالثواب أم لا بناء على أن العوض منه مجرد اللذة والمنفعة. وفي الثواب قصد التعظيم به بما لا يثبت بدون علمه بذلك، ومنها أنه هل يجوز التفضل بقضاء عوض المظلوم عن الظالم؟ بناء على أن حقه في الأعواض المقابلة بالمضار وقد وصلت أم لا بناء على أن حقه في الأعواض الواجبة، ولم تصل وأنه لو جاز التفضل العوضه لجاز ترك الانتصاف من الظالم وهو باطل.
And among them: that causing pain by the command of God, as in the use of beasts, or by His permission as in slaughtering them, or by His enabling with delay of retribution until the abode of recompense as in the oppressed, his compensation is upon God because He is exalted above injustice. And among them: that if pleasure and pain are equal in being a grace, the majority holds that pleasure is determined and pain is ugly, because it is only good if it is determined as a way to compensation and grace. Abu Hashim said: rather, one may choose between them as between two benefits, because causing pain, being compensation and grace, has exited from being vain and unjust. And among them: that compensation is perpetually deserved according to Abi Ali, like reward; for if it ceased, he would be grieved at its cessation, so another compensation is established, and so on. And according to Abu Hashim, it is temporary; for if perpetuity were stipulated, it would not be good without it, and the necessary consequence is false because rational people may approve of pains for temporary benefits. And among them: that they differed about whether knowledge of the compensated person that it is his right is required for the continuation of compensation, like reward, or not, based on the fact that the essence of compensation is mere pleasure and benefit. In reward, there is the intention of glorification which is not established without his knowledge of it. And among them: whether it is permissible to voluntarily pay the compensation of the oppressed on behalf of the oppressor? Based on whether his right is in the compensations in exchange for harms and they have been delivered, or not, based on whether his right is in the obligatory compensations and they have not been delivered. And that if voluntary payment of his compensation were permissible, then it would be permissible to leave retribution from the oppressor, which is false.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage presents four additional branches (sub-issues) of the Mu'tazilite discussion on compensations. The first branch concerns pain caused by God's command or permission: since God is transcendent above injustice, such pain is compensated by God. The second branch deals with the case where pleasure and pain are both equally beneficial (luṭf). The majority (jumhūr) say pleasure is necessarily chosen, and pain is repugnant because it is only good when it serves as a means to compensation and benefit. Abu Hashim argues one can choose either, because pain, being both compensation and grace, is no longer vain or unjust. The third branch concerns the perpetuity of compensation: Abi Ali says compensation is perpetual like reward, because if it stopped, the person would be grieved, so another compensation follows. Abu Hashim says it is temporary, because rational people approve of pains for temporary benefits. The fourth branch asks whether knowledge of the recipient that the compensation is his right is necessary for its continuation, in analogy to reward (thawāb) which requires knowledge for its purpose of glorification. The fifth branch (included here) asks whether it is permissible to voluntarily pay the compensation of the oppressed on behalf of the oppressor. The argument turns on whether the right is in the compensations themselves (as exchange for harms) and they have already been received, or whether the right is in the obligatory compensations that have not been delivered. If voluntary payment were allowed, it would imply that leaving retribution against the oppressor is permissible, which is false. The passage ends with this point.

Key Terms
الإيلام
الانتصاف
العوض
الظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if God causes pain, it cannot be compensated because God is above obligations.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites argue that God's justice requires compensation; He is not above justice but embodies it.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is compensation for pain caused by God upon God?
A) Because God directly causes all pain
B) Because God is exalted above injustice
C) Because the human agent is not responsible
D) Because pain is always unjust

Key Takeaway
Pain caused by God's command, permission, or enabling is compensated by God due to His transcendence above injustice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the pain you have caused to others—even unintentionally—does this lesson remind you that ultimate justice and compensation belong to God? Are you willing to release your need to make it right and trust His compensation?
?
Think of a moment when you felt deeply wronged by someone and saw no justice in this world. According to this passage, God's enabling of that harm comes with guaranteed compensation. Can you truly believe this and let go of your bitterness?
?
Every time you eat meat, you are benefiting from an animal's pain permitted by God. How does the truth that God compensates that animal affect your gratitude for the provision? Does it change how you treat the blessings you receive?

Choosing Between Pleasure and Pain as Grace
Second branch on grace equivalence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute between the majority and Abu Hashim on whether pleasure or pain is chosen when both serve as grace.

Core Idea
The majority favors pleasure, but Abu Hashim allows choice.

The second branch examines a theoretical scenario where both pleasure (ladhdha) and pain (alam) are equally beneficial as a grace (luṭf) from God. The majority holds that pleasure must be chosen and pain is repugnant, while Abu Hashim argues one can choose either because pain, when serving as compensation and grace, is no longer vain or unjust.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two positions.
2
Understand the majority view
The majority says pain is only good when it is determined as a way to compensation and grace.
They prioritize pleasure.
3
Understand Abu Hashim's view
Abu Hashim says causing pain as compensation and grace removes it from being vain and unjust, so choice is permissible.
He equates it to choosing between two benefits (manfa'atayn).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن الإيلام بأمر الله كما في استعمال البهائم أو بإباحته كما في ذبحها أو بتمكينه مع تأخير الانتصاف إلى دار الجزاء كما في المظلوم عوضه على الله تعالى لتعاليه عن الظلم ومنها أنه إذا استوى لذة وألم في كونهما لطفاً، فالجمهور على أنه يتعين اللذة، ويقبح الألم. لأنه إنما يحسن إذا تعين طريقاً للعوض واللطف وقال أبو هاشم: بل يتخير منهما كما بين المنفعتين لأن الإيلام بكونه عوضاً ولطفاً قد خرج عن كونه عبثاً وظلماً، ومنها أن العوض يستحق دائماً عند أبي علي كالثواب. إذ لو انقطع لاغتم بانقطاعه فثبت عوض آخر وهلم جراً، ومنقطعاً عند أبي هاشم إذ لو شرط الدوام لما حسن بدونه واللازم باطل لأن العقلاء قد يستحسنون الالام لمنافع منقطعة، ومنها أنهم اختلفوا في أنه هل يشترط عند إبقاء العوض علم المعوض بأنه حقه كالثواب أم لا بناء على أن العوض منه مجرد اللذة والمنفعة. وفي الثواب قصد التعظيم به بما لا يثبت بدون علمه بذلك، ومنها أنه هل يجوز التفضل بقضاء عوض المظلوم عن الظالم؟ بناء على أن حقه في الأعواض المقابلة بالمضار وقد وصلت أم لا بناء على أن حقه في الأعواض الواجبة، ولم تصل وأنه لو جاز التفضل العوضه لجاز ترك الانتصاف من الظالم وهو باطل.
And among them: that causing pain by the command of God, as in the use of beasts, or by His permission as in slaughtering them, or by His enabling with delay of retribution until the abode of recompense as in the oppressed, his compensation is upon God because He is exalted above injustice. And among them: that if pleasure and pain are equal in being a grace, the majority holds that pleasure is determined and pain is ugly, because it is only good if it is determined as a way to compensation and grace. Abu Hashim said: rather, one may choose between them as between two benefits, because causing pain, being compensation and grace, has exited from being vain and unjust. And among them: that compensation is perpetually deserved according to Abi Ali, like reward; for if it ceased, he would be grieved at its cessation, so another compensation is established, and so on. And according to Abu Hashim, it is temporary; for if perpetuity were stipulated, it would not be good without it, and the necessary consequence is false because rational people may approve of pains for temporary benefits. And among them: that they differed about whether knowledge of the compensated person that it is his right is required for the continuation of compensation, like reward, or not, based on the fact that the essence of compensation is mere pleasure and benefit. In reward, there is the intention of glorification which is not established without his knowledge of it. And among them: whether it is permissible to voluntarily pay the compensation of the oppressed on behalf of the oppressor? Based on whether his right is in the compensations in exchange for harms and they have been delivered, or not, based on whether his right is in the obligatory compensations and they have not been delivered. And that if voluntary payment of his compensation were permissible, then it would be permissible to leave retribution from the oppressor, which is false.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage presents four additional branches (sub-issues) of the Mu'tazilite discussion on compensations. The first branch concerns pain caused by God's command or permission: since God is transcendent above injustice, such pain is compensated by God. The second branch deals with the case where pleasure and pain are both equally beneficial (luṭf). The majority (jumhūr) say pleasure is necessarily chosen, and pain is repugnant because it is only good when it serves as a means to compensation and benefit. Abu Hashim argues one can choose either, because pain, being both compensation and grace, is no longer vain or unjust. The third branch concerns the perpetuity of compensation: Abi Ali says compensation is perpetual like reward, because if it stopped, the person would be grieved, so another compensation follows. Abu Hashim says it is temporary, because rational people approve of pains for temporary benefits. The fourth branch asks whether knowledge of the recipient that the compensation is his right is necessary for its continuation, in analogy to reward (thawāb) which requires knowledge for its purpose of glorification. The fifth branch (included here) asks whether it is permissible to voluntarily pay the compensation of the oppressed on behalf of the oppressor. The argument turns on whether the right is in the compensations themselves (as exchange for harms) and they have already been received, or whether the right is in the obligatory compensations that have not been delivered. If voluntary payment were allowed, it would imply that leaving retribution against the oppressor is permissible, which is false. The passage ends with this point.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for Abu Hashim allowing a choice between pleasure and pain when both are graceful?
A) Pain is always ugly
B) Pleasure is always better
C) Pain as compensation and grace is no longer vain or unjust
D) The majority holds the same view

Key Takeaway
When pleasure and pain are equally graceful, the majority chooses pleasure, but Abu Hashim allows a choice because pain as compensation is not unjust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a decision where both options lead to good outcomes, do you automatically choose the one that feels pleasant? How does this lesson challenge the assumption that pain is always a sign of God's displeasure? What would it mean for you to accept a painful situation as a possible grace from God?
?
Think of a time when you experienced pain or hardship that later brought you closer to God or taught you something valuable. How does Abu Hashim’s reasoning help you see that pain as not being vain or unjust? Does this change how you view other painful experiences in your life?
?
If God’s grace can come through pain, what does that require of you when you are suffering? How can you train yourself to trust that His actions are never pointless, even when they hurt? What practical step will you take this week to remind yourself of this truth when you face difficulty?

Perpetuity of Compensation: Abi Ali vs. Abu Hashim
Third branch on perpetuity of compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement between Abi Ali and Abu Hashim on whether compensation must be perpetual or can be temporary.

Core Idea
Compensation may be perpetual or finite depending on the scholar's view.

The third branch addresses whether compensation (ʿiwaḍ) for harms is perpetual or temporary. Abi Ali al-Jubbā'ī argues it must be perpetual like reward (thawāb), because if it ceased, the recipient would be grieved, necessitating further compensation. Abu Hashim argues it is temporary, because rational people accept pains for temporary benefits.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the two contrasting positions.
2
Analyze Abi Ali's argument
He says if compensation were to end, the person would be grieved, so another compensation would be needed, leading to an infinite regress (halam jurrā). Thus it must be perpetual.
This parallels his view on reward.
3
Analyze Abu Hashim's argument
He says perpetuity is not required because rational people approve of pains for temporary benefits. The necessary consequence of perpetuity is false.
He gives an example of temporary benefits.

Source Text

Knowledge Condition for Continuation of Compensation
Fourth branch on knowledge condition for compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute over whether the recipient must know that compensation is his right for it to continue, similar to the requirement of knowledge in reward.

Core Idea
Knowledge of the compensation's right is necessary for its purpose, just as in reward.

The fourth branch deals with a condition for the continuation of compensation: must the recipient know that the compensation is his right? The passage draws an analogy to reward (thawāb), where knowledge of the recipient that the reward is a glorification (taʿẓīm) is essential for its purpose. Some argue that compensation, being mere pleasure and benefit, does not require such knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the differing views on the necessity of knowledge.
2
Understand the analogy to reward
Reward requires knowledge because its purpose is glorification (taʿẓīm). The question is whether compensation similarly requires knowledge.
Compensation is only pleasure and benefit.
3
Identify the two positions
Some say knowledge is required because compensation is like reward; others say not because compensation is merely pleasure.
The passage does not name the proponents but presents the basis.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you see your acts of worship as mere mechanical transactions (compensation) that you perform to get benefits, or as acts that require your conscious recognition that they are from God and for His glorification? Which view dominates your daily prayer? How might shifting to glorification change your experience?
?
If God gave you a reward without your knowing it was from Him, would it feel like a reward? The passage says knowledge is necessary for glorification. Examine your own heart: Are you content with receiving pleasures from God without caring that they come from Him, or do you long for the honor of knowing He is the Giver? Commit to thanking God specifically for one blessing today, acknowledging it as His right upon you.
?
In what areas of your life do you treat God's blessings as mere compensations—things you deserve or consume without thought—rather than as gracious gifts intended to honor you? This lesson challenges you to see His favors as requiring your grateful awareness. Write down one blessing you have taken for granted and decide to consciously acknowledge it as from God today.
