intermediate
5 min
Voluntary Payment of Compensation Is Not Permissible
Fifth branch on voluntary compensation payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument against voluntarily paying the oppressed's compensation on behalf of the oppressor, based on the necessity of retribution.

Core Idea
Voluntarily paying compensation does not replace the need for retribution against the oppressor.

The fifth branch examines whether it is permissible for someone (e.g., a third party) to voluntarily pay the compensation due to the oppressed on behalf of the oppressor. The argument presented is that this is not permissible because the oppressed's right is in the compensations in exchange for harms, and those compensations have not been delivered. If voluntary payment were allowed, it would imply that leaving retribution (intiṣāf) from the oppressor is permissible, which is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the issue and the argument against voluntary payment.
2
Understand the basis
The right of the oppressed is in the compensations that are owed in exchange for harms. If the compensations have not been delivered, the right remains.
3
Analyze the consequence
If voluntary payment were allowed, it would mean retribution against the oppressor can be abandoned, which is false.
Therefore, voluntary payment is not permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن الإيلام بأمر الله كما في استعمال البهائم أو بإباحته كما في ذبحها أو بتمكينه مع تأخير الانتصاف إلى دار الجزاء كما في المظلوم عوضه على الله تعالى لتعاليه عن الظلم ومنها أنه إذا استوى لذة وألم في كونهما لطفاً، فالجمهور على أنه يتعين اللذة، ويقبح الألم. لأنه إنما يحسن إذا تعين طريقاً للعوض واللطف وقال أبو هاشم: بل يتخير منهما كما بين المنفعتين لأن الإيلام بكونه عوضاً ولطفاً قد خرج عن كونه عبثاً وظلماً، ومنها أن العوض يستحق دائماً عند أبي علي كالثواب. إذ لو انقطع لاغتم بانقطاعه فثبت عوض آخر وهلم جراً، ومنقطعاً عند أبي هاشم إذ لو شرط الدوام لما حسن بدونه واللازم باطل لأن العقلاء قد يستحسنون الالام لمنافع منقطعة، ومنها أنهم اختلفوا في أنه هل يشترط عند إبقاء العوض علم المعوض بأنه حقه كالثواب أم لا بناء على أن العوض منه مجرد اللذة والمنفعة. وفي الثواب قصد التعظيم به بما لا يثبت بدون علمه بذلك، ومنها أنه هل يجوز التفضل بقضاء عوض المظلوم عن الظالم؟ بناء على أن حقه في الأعواض المقابلة بالمضار وقد وصلت أم لا بناء على أن حقه في الأعواض الواجبة، ولم تصل وأنه لو جاز التفضل العوضه لجاز ترك الانتصاف من الظالم وهو باطل.
And among them: that causing pain by the command of God, as in the use of beasts, or by His permission as in slaughtering them, or by His enabling with delay of retribution until the abode of recompense as in the oppressed, his compensation is upon God because He is exalted above injustice. And among them: that if pleasure and pain are equal in being a grace, the majority holds that pleasure is determined and pain is ugly, because it is only good if it is determined as a way to compensation and grace. Abu Hashim said: rather, one may choose between them as between two benefits, because causing pain, being compensation and grace, has exited from being vain and unjust. And among them: that compensation is perpetually deserved according to Abi Ali, like reward; for if it ceased, he would be grieved at its cessation, so another compensation is established, and so on. And according to Abu Hashim, it is temporary; for if perpetuity were stipulated, it would not be good without it, and the necessary consequence is false because rational people may approve of pains for temporary benefits. And among them: that they differed about whether knowledge of the compensated person that it is his right is required for the continuation of compensation, like reward, or not, based on the fact that the essence of compensation is mere pleasure and benefit. In reward, there is the intention of glorification which is not established without his knowledge of it. And among them: whether it is permissible to voluntarily pay the compensation of the oppressed on behalf of the oppressor? Based on whether his right is in the compensations in exchange for harms and they have been delivered, or not, based on whether his right is in the obligatory compensations and they have not been delivered. And that if voluntary payment of his compensation were permissible, then it would be permissible to leave retribution from the oppressor, which is false.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage presents four additional branches (sub-issues) of the Mu'tazilite discussion on compensations. The first branch concerns pain caused by God's command or permission: since God is transcendent above injustice, such pain is compensated by God. The second branch deals with the case where pleasure and pain are both equally beneficial (luṭf). The majority (jumhūr) say pleasure is necessarily chosen, and pain is repugnant because it is only good when it serves as a means to compensation and benefit. Abu Hashim argues one can choose either, because pain, being both compensation and grace, is no longer vain or unjust. The third branch concerns the perpetuity of compensation: Abi Ali says compensation is perpetual like reward, because if it stopped, the person would be grieved, so another compensation follows. Abu Hashim says it is temporary, because rational people approve of pains for temporary benefits. The fourth branch asks whether knowledge of the recipient that the compensation is his right is necessary for its continuation, in analogy to reward (thawāb) which requires knowledge for its purpose of glorification. The fifth branch (included here) asks whether it is permissible to voluntarily pay the compensation of the oppressed on behalf of the oppressor. The argument turns on whether the right is in the compensations themselves (as exchange for harms) and they have already been received, or whether the right is in the obligatory compensations that have not been delivered. If voluntary payment were allowed, it would imply that leaving retribution against the oppressor is permissible, which is false. The passage ends with this point.

Key Terms
فضل
عوض
انتصاف
ظالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that voluntary payment of compensation can fully satisfy justice.
Correction: The passage argues that it cannot replace retribution against the oppressor, which is still required.

Checkpoint
Why is voluntary payment of the oppressed's compensation on behalf of the oppressor considered impermissible according to the passage?
A) Because compensation must be given by the oppressor himself
B) Because it would imply leaving retribution from the oppressor, which is false
C) Because God does not accept voluntary payments
D) Because the oppressed has already received compensation

Key Takeaway
Voluntary payment of compensation on behalf of an oppressor is not permissible because it would legitimize leaving retribution, which is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have been wronged, do you find it easier to accept compensation and let go of the need for justice against the oppressor? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to seek peace at the expense of true retribution?
?
Reflect on a time when you saw an injustice and settled for a compromise that let the wrongdoer off easily. What were the consequences for your own sense of justice? How does this lesson call you to act differently?
?
If you were the oppressed, would you accept a voluntary payment from someone else on behalf of the oppressor, knowing it might let the oppressor evade punishment? What does your answer reveal about your understanding of justice and accountability before Allah?

The Mu'tazilite Positions on al-Aslah and Their Methodological Critique
States the two Mu'tazilite schools on al-aslah and critiques their analogical method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Mu'tazilite schools' doctrines on al-Aslah and the epistemological critique of their reliance on analogy from the seen to the unseen.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's proof from analogy is their weakness, not their strength.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents the two main Mu'tazilite positions on al-Aslah (the most beneficial) and then delivers a sharp critique of their method. Before we can evaluate their doctrine, we need to see exactly what they claim and how they attempt to prove it. This lesson will lay out both the claims and the fundamental flaw in their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the structure: first the two views, then their points of agreement, then the critique.
Notice that the critique comes at the end and uses strong language.
2
Identify the two positions
Find where the Baghdadis and Basrans disagree on the scope of al-aslah. One says it covers both religion and world, the other only religion. Also note their different definitions of al-aslah.
The Baghdadis define al-aslah as 'in wisdom and management', the Basrans as 'the more beneficial'.
3
Locate the common ground
The passage says both schools agreed on several points. List them: obligation of decrees and enabling, doing the utmost possible good for each person, and that there is no act that would force all disbelievers to believe.
This agreement shows they share a rationalist framework.
4
Understand the critique
The author says their ultimate proof is qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy from the seen to the unseen). He criticizes this as stemming from deficient insight into divine matters and error about God's attributes.
This is a methodological refutation, not just a doctrinal disagreement.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does the author call their approach 'deficient'? What does this tell you about the Sunni position on using reason in theology?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب البغداديون من المعتزلة إلى أنه يجب على الله تعالى ما هو أصلح لعباده في الدين والدنيا، وقال البصريون بل في الدين فقط فيعنون بالأصلح الأنفع ، والبغداديون الأصلح في الحكمة والتدبير. واتفق الفريقان على وجوب الاقدار والتمكين، وأقصى ما يمكن في معلوم الله تعالى مما يؤمن عنده المكلف، ويطيع وأنه فعل لكل أحد غاية مقدورة من الأصلح، وليس في مقدوره لطف لو فعل بالكفار لأمنوا جميعاً، وإلا لكان تركه بخلاً وسفها، وعمدتهم القصوى قياس الغائب على الشاهد لقصور نظرهم في المعارف الإلهية ، واللطائف الخفية الربانية، ووفور غلطهم في صفات الواجب الحق وأفعال الغنى المطلق .
The Baghdadis of the Mu'tazila held that God Almighty must do what is most beneficial for His servants in religion and the world, while the Basrans said rather only in religion; they mean by al-aslah the more beneficial, and the Baghdadis [mean] al-aslah in wisdom and management. Both parties agreed on the obligation of decrees and enabling, and the utmost possible within God's knowledge of what would cause the morally responsible person to believe and obey, and that He does for everyone the utmost possible in al-aslah, and that there is no kindness within His power that if He did to the disbelievers they would all believe; otherwise His leaving it would be miserliness and foolishness. Their ultimate proof is the analogy of the unseen from the witnessed due to the deficiency of their insight into divine knowledge, the hidden subtle divine realities, and the abundance of their error concerning the attributes of the Necessary Truth and the acts of the Absolutely Self-Sufficient.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents the two Mu'tazilite schools' positions on al-aslah. The Baghdadis hold that God must do what is most beneficial in both religion and worldly life, while the Basrans limit it to religion, defining al-aslah as the more beneficial. The Baghdadis interpret al-aslah in terms of wisdom and management. Both schools agree on certain points: that God must decree and enable the best, that He does the utmost possible good for each person, and that there is no act of kindness within His power that would cause all disbelievers to believe—otherwise, refraining would be miserly. The author then critiques their ultimate proof: qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy from the seen to the unseen). He attributes this to their deficient insight into divine matters, hidden subtleties, and errors concerning God's attributes and actions as the Self-Sufficient. The passage thus states the doctrine and then undermines its epistemological foundation.

Key Terms
البغداديون
البصريون
قياس الغائب على الشاهد
المعتزلة
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazilites agree that God must do what is most beneficial in both religion and the world.
Correction: The Baghdadis and Basrans disagree on the scope: Baghdadis include both religion and world, Basrans limit it to religion. They also define al-aslah differently.

Checkpoint
What is the ultimate proof of the Mu'tazila according to the author?
A) Scripture
B) Qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid
C) Consensus of the community
D) Intuition

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's doctrine of al-Aslah is not monolithic—the Baghdadis and Basrans differ on its scope—and their method of proving it by analogy from human experience is epistemologically flawed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may have thought that God owes you certain benefits in life. The Mu'tazila argued that God must do what is most beneficial for you. But this passage reveals a deeper problem: their proof for this claim is flawed because they measure God by human standards. Where in your own heart have you judged God's actions using the scale of your own limited understanding? What would it mean for you to surrender that scale and trust that God's wisdom is beyond your comprehension, even when His plan does not appear 'most beneficial' to you? Will you commit to that surrender starting now?
?
The author accuses the Mu'tazila of 'abundant error concerning the attributes of the Necessary Truth'. When you reflect on your own conception of God's attributes—His justice, His mercy, His wisdom—are you certain that your understanding is not tainted by human analogies? What is one specific way you have been thinking of God that may actually be an error in need of correction? Right now, resolve to purify that concept by seeking knowledge from revelation rather than your own reasoning.
?
Both schools of Mu'tazila agreed that there is no kindness (lutf) within God's power that would make all disbelievers believe—otherwise God's failure to use it would be miserly. Consider someone you know who does not believe or who persists in sin. If you think God could and should have done something to compel them to change, you have fallen into the same error. Are you willing to accept that God's wisdom is greater than your demands for a certain outcome? Can you, in this moment, entrust that person to God's wisdom without dictating what He must do? If not, what will you do to let go of that demand?

Mu'tazilite Doctrine of Al-Aslah: Baghdadi and Basran Views
Sanusi enumerates the two Mu'tazilite positions on al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah and the distinction between the Baghdadi and Basran schools on its scope.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila disagreed: must God do what is best in both religion and world, or only in religion?

The Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial) is a key point of controversy. In this lesson, we examine the positions of the Baghdadi and Basran schools, which differed on whether God is obligated to do what is best for His servants in matters of religion alone or also in worldly affairs. This distinction sets the stage for their broader argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text describing the two views. Notice the terms 'Baghdadiyyun' and 'Basriyyun' and their definitions.
2
Identify the schools
Distinguish between the Baghdadi view (religion and world) and the Basran view (religion only). Also note the Baghdadi specification of 'aslah as wisdom and governance.
3
Compare the views
Consider why the scope matters: if 'aslah includes worldly affairs, God's obligation is broader. Reflect on the implications for divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب البغداديون من المعتزلة إلى أنه يجب على الله تعالى ما هو أصلح لعباده في الدين والدنيا، وقال البصريون بل في الدين فقط فيعنون بالأصلح الأنفع، والبغداديون الأصلح في الحكمة والتدبير. واتفق الفريقان على وجوب الاقدار والتمكين، وأقصى ما يمكن في معلوم الله تعالى مما يؤمن عنده المكلف، ويطيع وأنه فعل لكل أحد غاية مقدورة من الأصلح، وليس في مقدوره لطف لو فعل بالكفار لأمنوا جميعاً، وإلا لكان تركه بخلاً وسفها، وعمدتهم القصوى قياس الغائب على الشاهد لقصور نظرهم في المعارف الإلهية، واللطائف الخفية الربانية، ووفور غلطهم في صفات الواجب الحق وأفعال الغنى المطلق . قالوا : نحن نقطع بأن الحكيم إذا أمر بطاعته وقدر على أن يعطي المأمور ما يصل به إلى الطاعة من غير تضرر بذلك ، ثم لم يفعل كان مذموماً عند العقلاء، معدوداً في زمرة البخلاء، ولذلك من دعى عدوه إلى الموالاة، والرجوع إلى الطاعة، لا يجوز أن يعامله من الغلظ واللين إلا بما هو أنجع في حصول المراد، وأدعى إلى ترك العناد، وأيضاً من اتخذ ضيافة لرجل واستدعى حضوره، وعلم أنه لو تلقاه ببشر وطلاقة وجه دخل وأكل وإلا فلا. فالواجب عليه البشر والطلاقة والملاطفة لا أضدادها .
The Baghdadiyyun among the Mu'tazila held that what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants in religion and the world is obligatory for God Almighty, and the Basriyyun said rather in religion only, meaning by 'most beneficial' the most advantageous, and the Baghdadiyyun [meant] the most beneficial in wisdom and governance. And the two groups agreed on the obligation of [God's] decreeing ability and enabling [the servant], and the utmost possible within God's knowledge that the accountable [servant] would believe and obey, and that He does for everyone a predetermined end of what is most beneficial, and that there is no gracious favor (lutf) within His power such that if He did it to the disbelievers they would all believe, for otherwise His leaving it would be miserliness and foolishness. And their utmost proof is the analogy of the unseen from the witnessed (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid), due to the shortcoming of their insight in divine sciences and the hidden lordly subtleties, and the abundance of their error concerning the attributes of the Necessary Truth and the acts of the Absolutely Self-Sufficient. They said: We are certain that if a wise person commands obedience to him and is able to give the commanded one that by which he attains obedience without harm to himself, then does not do it, he is blameworthy among rational people and counted among the miserly. Therefore, whoever calls his enemy to friendship and return to obedience, it is not permissible for him to treat him with harshness or gentleness except in the way most effective in achieving the desired end and more conducive to abandoning obstinacy. Also, whoever hosts a man and invites his attendance, and knows that if he receives him with cheerfulness and a smiling face he would enter and eat, otherwise not, then cheerfulness, smiling, and gentleness are obligatory upon him, not their opposites.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial) and their proof for it. The author first distinguishes between the Baghdadi and Basran schools: the Baghdadis hold that God must do what is best for His servants in both religion and world, while the Basrans limit it to religion only, interpreting 'aslah as 'anfa' (most advantageous). The Baghdadis further specify that 'aslah refers to wisdom and good governance. Both groups agree on the obligation of God's decreeing ability (al-iqdar) and enabling (al-tamkin) the servant, meaning God must provide the maximum possible within His knowledge for the servant to believe and obey, and that there is no effortless grace (lutf) within God's power that would cause all disbelievers to believe, for otherwise its omission would be miserliness and foolishness. The author then states that their ultimate proof is qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy of the unseen from the witnessed), criticizing this method as stemming from their deficient understanding of divine attributes and of the Self-Sufficient's acts. He then quotes their argument: a wise human ruler, if he commands obedience and can give the subject means to obey without harm, but does not, is blameworthy; similarly, when inviting an enemy to reconciliation, one must use the most effective means; and a host must greet his guest cheerfully. By analogy, God must do the most beneficial for His creatures. The author presents this as their view before refuting it.

Key Terms
الأصلح
البغداديون
البصريون
الأنفع
الحكمة
التدبير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila were unified on the doctrine of al-aslah.
Correction: In fact, the Baghdadis and Basrans differed: Baghdadis included both religion and world, Basrans limited to religion and defined 'aslah as 'anfa'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the Basran Mu'tazila understand by 'al-aslah'?
A) The most beneficial in religion and world
B) The most advantageous (anfa')
C) The most beneficial in wisdom and governance
D) The most beneficial for the afterlife

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila split on whether God must provide what is best for His servants in religion alone or also in worldly affairs, with the Basrans limiting it to religion and interpreting 'aslah as 'anfa'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that some early scholars debated what God must do for His servants. Do you ever find yourself expecting God to give you what seems best in this life? Reflect on a recent disappointment—did you trust that God's wisdom and governance are at work, even when you couldn't see the benefit? How can you strengthen that trust starting today?
?
The Basrans limited God's obligation to religious benefit. When you make decisions, do you prioritize your worldly comfort over your spiritual well-being? Think of one area where you have been more concerned with dunya than din. What concrete step can you take this week to realign your priorities?
?
The Baghdadis believed God must act with perfect wisdom and governance in all matters. When you face a trial, do you truly believe that God's plan is wiser than your own? If not, what underlying belief in your heart needs to change? Commit to reading this passage again tomorrow and asking God to increase your certainty in His perfect management.

Sects and Disagreements

Agreement on Iqdar and Tamkin and the Lutf Condition
Taftazani presents the Mu'tazilite agreement on iqdar and tamkin and the lutf condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that God is obligated to decree ability and enablement (iqdar wa tamkin) and that there is no lutf that would force belief on all disbelievers.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue God must give every person the maximum ability to believe, but no effortless grace that forces faith.

Building on the doctrine of al-aslah, the Mu'tazila further specify what God's obligation entails. They agree that God must decree ability (iqdar) and enablement (tamkin) for each person to the utmost possible within God's knowledge, and that God does for everyone a predetermined maximum of what is beneficial. They also argue that there is no gracious favor (lutf) within God's power that, if used, would cause all disbelievers to believe; otherwise, withholding it would be miserly and foolish.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Focus on the part from 'واتفق الفريقان' to 'بخلاً وسفهاً'.
2
Identify the agreements
Note the agreement on iqdar and tamkin, and the concept of a predetermined maximum (ghaya maqdur).
3
Understand the lutf clause
Notice the denial of any lutf that would cause all disbelievers to believe, and the implication that if such lutf existed, omitting it would be miserly and foolish.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the two groups agreed on the obligation of [God's] decreeing ability and enabling [the servant], and the utmost possible within God's knowledge that the accountable [servant] would believe and obey, and that He does for everyone a predetermined end of what is most beneficial, and that there is no gracious favor (lutf) within His power such that if He did it to the disbelievers they would all believe, for otherwise His leaving it would be miserliness and foolishness. Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial) and their proof for it. The author first distinguishes between the Baghdadi and Basran schools: the Baghdadis hold that God must do what is best for His servants in both religion and world, while the Basrans limit it to religion only, interpreting 'aslah as 'anfa' (most advantageous). The Baghdadis further specify that 'aslah refers to wisdom and good governance. Both groups agree on the obligation of God's decreeing ability (al-iqdar) and enabling (al-tamkin) the servant, meaning God must provide the maximum possible within His knowledge for the servant to believe and obey, and that there is no effortless grace (lutf) within God's power that would cause all disbelievers to believe, for otherwise its omission would be miserliness and foolishness. The author then states that their ultimate proof is qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy of the unseen from the witnessed), criticizing this method as stemming from their deficient understanding of divine attributes and of the Self-Sufficient's acts. He then quotes their argument: a wise human ruler, if he commands obedience and can give the subject means to obey without harm, but does not, is blameworthy; similarly, when inviting an enemy to reconciliation, one must use the most effective means; and a host must greet his guest cheerfully. By analogy, God must do the most beneficial for His creatures. The author presents this as their view before refuting it.

Key Terms
الاقدار
التمكين
الطف
الغاية المقدورة
البخل
السفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believed that God has a lutf that could force all disbelievers to believe but He withholds it.
Correction: The Mu'tazila actually deny the existence of such a lutf; they argue that if it existed, its omission would be unworthy of God.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, why is there no lutf that would cause all disbelievers to believe?
A) Because God does not want them to believe
B) Because if it existed and God did not use it, He would be miserly and foolish
C) Because disbelievers are incapable of believing
D) Because lutf is not in God's power

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue God must provide maximum ability to believe but deny that He has a special grace that would compel all to believe, as its omission would be unworthy of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God has given you the ability to believe but not a grace that compels faith, what does that require from you? Are you using the ability He gave you to its utmost, or are you neglecting it?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that if God had a lutf to force all to believe but didn't use it, that would be miserly. Do you ever wish God would force you or others to be good? What would that mean for the sincerity of faith and the reality of testing?
?
God does for everyone a predetermined end of what is most beneficial. Do you truly believe that God has given you exactly what is best for your faith, even in moments of hardship or doubt? In what specific area are you struggling to accept His decree as the most beneficial?

The Mu'tazilite Proof: Analogy from the Witnessed
Taftazani presents the Mu'tazilite proof by analogy and its examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite proof for al-aslah based on analogical reasoning from human behavior to God.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue from human rulers' obligations to infer that God must do what is most beneficial.

The Mu'tazila's strongest proof for the doctrine of al-aslah is the principle of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy from the witnessed to the unseen). They argue that rational humans judge a wise person who commands obedience and can assist but does not as blameworthy. Similarly, a person inviting an enemy to reconciliation must use the most effective means, and a host must greet his guest warmly. By analogy, God must do what is most beneficial for His servants. In this lesson, we examine this proof and the author's critique of it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read from 'وعمدتهم القصوى' to the end.
2
Identify the proof method
Note the principle of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid.
3
Examine the examples
Understand each example: the wise commander, the reconciliation, and the hosting scenario.
4
Consider the critique
The author criticizes this reasoning as stemming from shortcoming and error about God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب البغداديون من المعتزلة إلى أنه يجب على الله تعالى ما هو أصلح لعباده في الدين والدنيا، وقال البصريون بل في الدين فقط فيعنون بالأصلح الأنفع، والبغداديون الأصلح في الحكمة والتدبير. واتفق الفريقان على وجوب الاقدار والتمكين، وأقصى ما يمكن في معلوم الله تعالى مما يؤمن عنده المكلف، ويطيع وأنه فعل لكل أحد غاية مقدورة من الأصلح، وليس في مقدوره لطف لو فعل بالكفار لأمنوا جميعاً، وإلا لكان تركه بخلاً وسفها، وعمدتهم القصوى قياس الغائب على الشاهد لقصور نظرهم في المعارف الإلهية، واللطائف الخفية الربانية، ووفور غلطهم في صفات الواجب الحق وأفعال الغنى المطلق . قالوا : نحن نقطع بأن الحكيم إذا أمر بطاعته وقدر على أن يعطي المأمور ما يصل به إلى الطاعة من غير تضرر بذلك ، ثم لم يفعل كان مذموماً عند العقلاء، معدوداً في زمرة البخلاء، ولذلك من دعى عدوه إلى الموالاة، والرجوع إلى الطاعة، لا يجوز أن يعامله من الغلظ واللين إلا بما هو أنجع في حصول المراد، وأدعى إلى ترك العناد، وأيضاً من اتخذ ضيافة لرجل واستدعى حضوره، وعلم أنه لو تلقاه ببشر وطلاقة وجه دخل وأكل وإلا فلا. فالواجب عليه البشر والطلاقة والملاطفة لا أضدادها .
The Baghdadiyyun among the Mu'tazila held that what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants in religion and the world is obligatory for God Almighty, and the Basriyyun said rather in religion only, meaning by 'most beneficial' the most advantageous, and the Baghdadiyyun [meant] the most beneficial in wisdom and governance. And the two groups agreed on the obligation of [God's] decreeing ability and enabling [the servant], and the utmost possible within God's knowledge that the accountable [servant] would believe and obey, and that He does for everyone a predetermined end of what is most beneficial, and that there is no gracious favor (lutf) within His power such that if He did it to the disbelievers they would all believe, for otherwise His leaving it would be miserliness and foolishness. And their utmost proof is the analogy of the unseen from the witnessed (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid), due to the shortcoming of their insight in divine sciences and the hidden lordly subtleties, and the abundance of their error concerning the attributes of the Necessary Truth and the acts of the Absolutely Self-Sufficient. They said: We are certain that if a wise person commands obedience to him and is able to give the commanded one that by which he attains obedience without harm to himself, then does not do it, he is blameworthy among rational people and counted among the miserly. Therefore, whoever calls his enemy to friendship and return to obedience, it is not permissible for him to treat him with harshness or gentleness except in the way most effective in achieving the desired end and more conducive to abandoning obstinacy. Also, whoever hosts a man and invites his attendance, and knows that if he receives him with cheerfulness and a smiling face he would enter and eat, otherwise not, then cheerfulness, smiling, and gentleness are obligatory upon him, not their opposites.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial) and their proof for it. The author first distinguishes between the Baghdadi and Basran schools: the Baghdadis hold that God must do what is best for His servants in both religion and world, while the Basrans limit it to religion only, interpreting 'aslah as 'anfa' (most advantageous). The Baghdadis further specify that 'aslah refers to wisdom and good governance. Both groups agree on the obligation of God's decreeing ability (al-iqdar) and enabling (al-tamkin) the servant, meaning God must provide the maximum possible within His knowledge for the servant to believe and obey, and that there is no effortless grace (lutf) within God's power that would cause all disbelievers to believe, for otherwise its omission would be miserliness and foolishness. The author then states that their ultimate proof is qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy of the unseen from the witnessed), criticizing this method as stemming from their deficient understanding of divine attributes and of the Self-Sufficient's acts. He then quotes their argument: a wise human ruler, if he commands obedience and can give the subject means to obey without harm, but does not, is blameworthy; similarly, when inviting an enemy to reconciliation, one must use the most effective means; and a host must greet his guest cheerfully. By analogy, God must do the most beneficial for His creatures. The author presents this as their view before refuting it.

Key Terms
قياس الغائب على الشاهد
الحكيم
البخلاء
الموالاة
الضيافة
البشر
الطلاقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy from human behavior to God is a sound and universally accepted method in kalam.
Correction: Many theologians, including the author, criticize this analogy as flawed because it ignores the radical difference between the Creator and creatures.

Checkpoint
In the Mu'tazilite proof, what is the consequence for a wise person who commands obedience but does not provide the means to obey without harm?
A) He is praised for his wisdom
B) He is blamed and counted among the miserly
C) He is considered just
D) He is not judged

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use analogies from human conduct to argue that God must do what is most beneficial, a method criticized as anthropomorphic and flawed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God 'owes' you a certain outcome because you obeyed Him, as if He were a human ruler bound by fairness? How does this lesson expose the error in that feeling, and what can you do today to surrender that expectation to His absolute freedom?
?
When you face a situation where the best possible outcome does not happen, do you secretly blame God or doubt His wisdom? In light of Taftazani's critique of the Mu'tazilite method, how can you train yourself to affirm His perfection even when His actions contradict your human reasoning?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed that God must follow human notions of justice. Where in your own life have you judged God's actions by human standards rather than submitting to His unseen wisdom? Commit to one specific area where you will replace that judgment with trust in His self-sufficiency.

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Doctrine of Al-Aslah
Taftazani refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah with three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's three arguments refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that God must create what is most beneficial for His servants.

Core Idea
God is not obligated to create the most beneficial for His servants.

Taftazani engages directly with the Mu'tazilite claim that God must do what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for humanity. After addressing preliminary analogies and clarifying the meaning of obligation, he presents three decisive arguments that expose the logical consequences of this doctrine, especially regarding the existence of disbelievers and eternal punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Observe the overall argumentative flow from preliminary rebuttals to the three main arguments.
Notice the careful distinction Taftazani makes at the start.
2
Understand the preliminary rebuttal
Focus on the first few lines: Taftazani rejects analogies based on human rulers and distinguishes between logical necessity (luzum) and moral obligation (wujub bi-ma'na istihqaq al-dhamm).
This distinction is critical for the entire refutation.
3
Examine the three arguments
Each argument (wajh) is a separate reductio ad absurdum. For each, identify the premise, the conclusion, and how it undermines the aslah doctrine.
The third argument is particularly sharp regarding disbelievers.
4
Consider the responses to objections
Notice how Taftazani anticipates and answers Mu'tazilite counterpoints, such as the objection about reward outweighing harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا: ذاك بعد تسلیم استلزام الأمر الإرادة إنما هو في حكيم محتاج إلى طاعة الأولياء أو رجوع الأعداء، ويتعزز بكثرة الأعوان والأنصار، ويعظم لديه الأقدار، ويكون للشيء بالنسبة إليه مقدار وقد يتمسك بأن عند وجود الداعي والقدرة، وانتفاء الصارف يجب الفعل . ورد بأن ذاك بعد التسليم وجوب عنه بمعنى اللزوم عند تمام العلة. والكلام في الوجوب عليه بمعنى استحقاق الذم على الترك، فأين هذا من ذاك؟ لنا بعد التنزل إلى القول لوجوب شيء على الله ، وأن ليس الصلاح والفساد بخلق الله تعالى وجوه : الأول : لو وجب عليه الأصلح لعباده لما خلق الكافر الفقير المعذب في الدنيا والآخرة، سيما المبتلى بالأسقام والآلام والمحن والآفات. الثاني : يلزم على ما ذكرتم من الأمثلة أنه يجب على كل أحد ما هو أصلح لعبيده ولنفسه فإن دفع بأن المكلف يتضرر بذلك، ويلحقه الكد والتعب. أجيب بأنه يلزم حينئذ أن لا يجب عليه شيء مما هو كذلك. : فإن قيل : يترتب عليه ثواب يربي عليه فيحسن لذلك. قلنا : فليكن الأصلح كذلك. الثالث : يلزم أن يكون الأصلح للكفار الخلود في النار. إذ لو كان الخروج أو عدم الدخول أصلح لفعل . فإن قيل : نعم يلزم أن الأصلح لهم الخلود لعلمه بأنهم لو ردوا لعادوا لما نهوا عنه .
We say: That [analogy] is after conceding that commanding necessitates will, and that [occurs] only in a wise person who needs the obedience of friends or the return of enemies, and is strengthened by many supporters and helpers, and [for whom] matters have significance for him, and things have value relative to him. And [the Mu'tazila] might also cling to [the claim] that when the motive and ability are present and the obstacle is absent, the act is necessitated. And it is replied that that, after conceding, is [a] necessity from it in the sense of necessary consequence when the cause is complete. But the discussion is about obligation upon Him in the sense of deserving blame for omission, so how is this that? For us, after descending to the position that something is obligatory upon God, and that benefit and harm are not by the creation of God Almighty, [we have] several arguments: First: If what is most beneficial for His servants were obligatory upon Him, He would not have created the disbelieving poor person tormented in this world and the next, especially the one afflicted with ailments, pains, trials, and afflictions. Second: It would follow from your examples that everyone must do what is most beneficial for his servants and for himself. But if [the opponent] objects that the accountable person would be harmed by that, and would incur fatigue and trouble, then the answer is that it would then follow that nothing of that sort is obligatory upon him. And if it is said: There results from it a reward that outweighs it, so it is good on that account. We say: Then let the most beneficial be likewise. Third: It would follow that what is most beneficial for the disbelievers is eternal abiding in the Fire, for if exiting or not entering were more beneficial, He would have done it. If it is said: Yes, it follows that what is most beneficial for them is eternal abiding because He knows that if they were sent back they would return to what they were forbidden from. [The answer is that this concedes the point.]
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah. The author (Taftazani) begins by responding to their analogy: the analogy holds only for a wise person who needs the obedience of friends or return of enemies, and who gains strength from supporters, and for whom things have value. God, however, is absolutely self-sufficient and not in need. Then he addresses their further argument that when motive and ability exist and obstacle is absent, the act is necessitated. He replies that this necessity is logical necessity when the cause is complete, not moral obligation deserving blame for omission. The discussion is about the latter, so the argument is irrelevant. Then he presents three arguments (وجوه) after conceding for the sake of argument that something is obligatory upon God and that benefit and harm are not created by God. First: If the aslah were obligatory, God would not have created the poor disbeliever tormented in both worlds, nor the afflicted person. Second: Their examples would imply that everyone must do the most beneficial for his servants and himself; they cannot escape by citing harm or fatigue, because then nothing of that sort would be obligatory. If they say the reward outweighs the harm, the reply is that the same could be said for God's actions not being aslah. Third: The aslah for disbelievers would then be eternal punishment in Hell, because if something else were better, God would have done it. If they object that God knows they would revert to disbelief, that actually concedes that eternal punishment is indeed the aslah for them, which undermines their doctrine's moral force.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الاستلزام
اللزوم
العلة التامة
الكافر
الخلود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'وجوب' (obligation) in kalam always refers to moral obligation that deserves blame if omitted.
Correction: Taftazani shows that 'وجوب' can mean logical necessity (luzum) when a cause is complete, not necessarily moral blame. The Mu'tazilite argument confuses these two meanings.

Checkpoint
What distinction does Taftazani make regarding the meaning of 'وجوب' (obligation/necessity) in his refutation?
A) Necessity due to cause vs. obligation deserving blame
B) Obligation in law vs. obligation in theology
C) Divine obligation vs. human obligation
D) Logical necessity vs. physical necessity

Key Takeaway
Taftazani's refutation shows that the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah leads to absurd consequences, such as the existence of suffering and the eternal punishment of disbelievers being the most beneficial for them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God's plan for you was not 'the most beneficial' because of a trial or disappointment? How does Taftazani's refutation of al-aslah challenge you to trust God's wisdom even when His actions seem contrary to your idea of benefit? Will you surrender your expectations to Him today?
?
Consider a specific hardship you are currently facing. If you truly believe that God is not obligated to give you what is best by your measure, what does that demand from your patience and reliance on Him? Are you willing to say, 'I know that whatever You choose is better for me than what I choose' even when you cannot see how?
?
The third argument shows that even the eternal punishment of disbelievers is, in God's knowledge, the most beneficial for them. If you have a family member or friend who is a disbeliever, how does this truth affect your urgency in calling them to Islam? Does it stir you to action, or do you feel it is a license for apathy? What will you do differently starting today?
